201 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Easley


Crim. No. 21117


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 858; 671 P.2d 813; 196 Cal. Rptr. 309; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 247



November 7, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. CR 6702, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons discussed above, the judgment as to guilt is affirmed, the judgment as to penalty is reversed and the case is remanded for a new penalty trial before a properly instructed jury.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which entered a jury verdict convicting defendant of first degree murder, finding as special circumstances that the murders were intentional and carried out pursuant to an agreement to accept valuable consideration from a person other than the victims and that defendant personally committed more than one offense of murder, and sentencing defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder with special circumstances and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, and the court affirmed the judgment as to defendant's guilt. The court ruled that there was insufficient evidence to demonstrate that the exclusion for cause of prospective jurors who indicated they would vote against the death penalty resulted in a nonneutral jury. The court held that defendant failed to show that the failure to challenge the search warrant at trial amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel, and that hearsay testimony as to numerous statements implicating defendant were admissible because the statements fell within the scope of the coconspirator's exception to the hearsay rule, Cal. Evid. Code §1223, and did not violate defendant's constitutional right to confrontation. The court reversed the judgment as to the death penalty because the trial court erred in instructing the jury not to be influenced by sympathy at the penalty phase and in instructing the jury pursuant to a death penalty law which was in effect at the time of the trial but was not in effect at the time of the crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment as to defendant's guilt, but reversed the judgment as to the sentence because the trial court erred in instructing the jury not to be influenced by sympathy at the penalty phase and in instructing the jury pursuant to a death penalty law which was in effect at the time of the trial but was not in effect at the time of the crime.

 

202 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Shelter Creek Dev. Corp. v. City of Oxnard


L.A. No. 31609


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 733; 669 P.2d 948; 195 Cal. Rptr. 361; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 239



October 13, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 71306, Charles R. McGrath, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, apartment complex owners and real estate developer, appealed the denial of their first summary judgment motion by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), arguing that the amended Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66424, exempted their stock cooperative conversion project from a local ordinance. Defendant city appealed the grant of plaintiffs' second summary judgment motion that declared the ordinance unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff apartment complex owners and plaintiff real estate developer (who held an option to purchase the property) sought to convert the structure into a stock cooperative form of ownership. Defendant city enacted an ordinance that required a special use permit to accomplish the conversion. Plaintiffs filed a complaint seeking a declaration that the ordinance was either unconstitutional or inapplicable to their project because of the grandfather clause in 1979 Cal. Stat. 1192, § 8(b), which amended the Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66424, to exempt certain stock cooperative conversion projects from the requirements of local ordinances. After denying plaintiffs' first motion for summary judgment on the ground that § 8(b) did not exempt plaintiffs' project from local regulation, the trial court granted plaintiffs' second motion for summary judgment by finding that the ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to plaintiffs' project. Both parties appealed. Not reaching the constitutional issues, the court affirmed by holding that the grandfather clause of § 8(b) protected plaintiffs because it became effective before the enactment of defendant's ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of plaintiffs' second motion for summary judgment (albeit on different non-constitutional grounds), holding that defendant city was not able to apply the requirements of the Subdivision Map Act (Act) to plaintiffs' stock cooperative conversion project because defendant enacted its ordinance after the effective date of a grandfather clause that exempted plaintiffs' project from the Act's provisions.

 

203 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Legislature v. Deukmejian


S. F. Nos. 24589, 24596


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 658; 669 P.2d 17; 194 Cal. Rptr. 781; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 236



September 15, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  Let a writ of mandate issue restraining respondents from expending any public funds or otherwise acting to carry out the special election proclaimed by respondent Governor to be conducted on December 13, 1983.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state legislature sought a writ of mandamus to restrain respondents, governor and others, from expending any public funds or otherwise acting to carry out a special election for the purpose of submitting to the voters an initiative measure brought forth by real parties in interest.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state legislature sought a writ of mandamus to restrain respondents, governor and others, from expending any public funds or otherwise acting to carry out a special election proclaimed by respondent governor for the purpose of submitting to voters an initiative measure brought forth by real parties in interest, as proponents. The initiative would have realigned the assembly, senate, and congressional districts and repealed previously enacted statutes. Petitioners claimed the initiative was invalid, because it represented an attempt to redistrict more than once in a decennial period and that action was barred by Cal. Const. art. XXI. Petitioners also argued that the initiative would violate U.S. Const. art. I, § 2. Petitioners also argued that the districts the initiative sought to create violated the equal representation standard and unnecessarily disenfranchised voters by denying them due process and equal protection rights. The court issued a writ of mandate restraining respondents from carrying out the special election, because the constitutional legislative duty of establishing district boundaries for decennial period was previously completed.


OUTCOME: The petition was granted. A writ of mandate was issued based on petitioner state legislature's request to restrain respondents, governor and others, from expending public funds or otherwise acting to carry out a special election to submit an invalid initiative measure to the voters, because the initiative represented an attempt to redistrict more than once in a decennial period in violation of the state constitution.

 

204 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Sand v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24496


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 567; 668 P.2d 787; 194 Cal. Rptr. 480; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 231



September 8, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writ of mandate is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner indigent prisoner sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent superior court (California) to grant his motion for public funds to pay for ancillary defense services under Cal. Penal Code § 987.9 (1977) based on the designation of his original murder charge as a "capital offense" because special circumstances had been alleged.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner indigent prisoner was charged with murder with special circumstances. Prior to and during petitioner's first trial, his attorney was granted funds pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 987.9 (1977) to pay for the services of an investigator, two experts, and jury selection consultants. The jury was unable to reach a verdict and a mistrial was declared. At a hearing on petitioner's motion for a continuance, the prosecutor stated on the record that he would not seek the death penalty, but would ask for a sentence of life imprisonment in the event special circumstances were proved. Petitioner, then moved for additional funds under § 987.9 and respondent Superior Court of Solano County denied the motion because the prosecution was no longer a "capital case." Petitioner sought a writ of mandate, and the court denied the motion and held that the legislative intent of § 987.9 was to assist defendants who faced death as an actual penalty: therefore, petitioner was not entitled to funds under § 987.9 for a "capital offense." Petitioner could have applied under other statutes for additional funds.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ and held that where petitioner indigent prisoner was charged with a "capital offense" of murder with a special circumstance that could lead to death if a jury determined that death was appropriate, where the prosecutor stipulated that the death penalty would not be sought and petitioner's case was no longer a "capital offense" for purposes of the capital offense funding statute.

 

205 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Yates


Crim. No. 22817


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 644; 669 P.2d 1; 194 Cal. Rptr. 765; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 235



September 8, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On October 6, 1983, the Opinion was Modified to read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 24291, Robert J. Cooney, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which convicted defendant of first degree murder and denied defendant 26 peremptory challenges under Cal. Penal Code § 1070.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder without special circumstances and faced a maximum penalty of 25 years to life. He appealed because the trial court denied 26 peremptory challenges. The court reviewed the history and purpose of Cal. Penal Code § 1070, wherein a defendant was entitled to a greater number of peremptory challenges when the penalty was exceptionally severe. The court concluded that it was no longer plausible to adhere to a line of precedent that denied additional challenges in all cases involving an indeterminate life sentence. The court noted that murder without special circumstances was the only offense that carried a potential punishment greater than a determinate life sentence. The court reasoned that by extending to murder defendants the same number of peremptory challenges given defendants facing determinate life sentences, the court avoided inequalities and eliminated constitutional problems inherent in a statutory scheme that provided unequal protection to similarly situated defendants. In reversing, the court held that the term "imprisonment for life" in § 1070 included an indeterminate life sentence equal to or more severe than a determinate life term.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because defendant was charged with murder without special circumstances, calling for an indeterminate life term equally or more severe than a determinate life term. Defendant was entitled to 26 peremptory challenges.

 

206 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Ramirez


Crim. No. 22489


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 541; 668 P.2d 761; 194 Cal. Rptr. 454; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 229



September 6, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 6, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A359598, David J. Aisenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied his motion to suppress drug evidence, claiming that it was discovered through an illegal search incident to his arrest. A police officer had searched defendant while defendant was standing in front of a store.


OVERVIEW: A police officer was suspicious of two men standing in front of a store. He searched them and arrested appellant after the officer was informed of his outstanding bench warrant. In the search incident to arrest, drugs were found. Appellant challenged his conviction for possession of phencyclidine, claiming that his motion to suppress evidence should have been granted because he had been arrested on a recalled warrant. The court reversed, finding that because the officer had no independent probable cause to arrest appellant where his arrest had been based on an invalid warrant, the fact that the officer acted in good faith reliance on the police communication was irrelevant. Because the recall of the warrant was within the collective knowledge of the police, the arresting officer could not rely on his fellow officers' errors of omission. Accordingly, the court held that the fruits of the search incident to the arrest without probable cause had to be suppressed.


OUTCOME: The judgment that denied appellant's motion to suppress was reversed because the drugs discovered during a search incident to his arrest were fruits of the poisonous tree because the arrest, based solely on a recalled bench warrant, was not based on probable cause.

 

207 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Dillon


Crim. No. 21964


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 441; 668 P.2d 697; 194 Cal. Rptr. 390; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 226



September 1, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 6, 1983. Richardson, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 68320, Christopher C. Cottle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed as to the conviction of attempted robbery. As to the conviction of murder, the judgment is modified by reducing the degree of the crime to murder in the second degree and, as so modified, is affirmed. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to arraign and pronounce judgment on defendant accordingly, and to determine whether to recommit him to the Youth Authority.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) that convicted him of attempted robbery and first-degree felony murder, arguing that a standing crop, as realty, could not be the subject of a robbery and that application of felony-murder rule constituted a deprivation of due process of law.


OVERVIEW: While attempting to steal marijuana growing on another's land, defendant was discovered by the landowner, who carried a shotgun. Fearing that he would be shot, defendant shot first, killing the landowner. He appealed his conviction of attempted robbery and first-degree felony murder, arguing that a standing crop of marijuana could not be the subject of a robbery because it was realty, not personalty, and that the felony-murder rule violated due process requirements. In affirming the attempted robbery conviction, the appellate court concluded that a robbery within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 211 was committed when property affixed to realty was severed and removed. Thus, defendant was properly convicted of attempting to commit such a robbery. In modifying the judgment by reducing the crime to second-degree murder, however, the appellate court concluded that, where defendant was unusually immature and his act was in response to a perceived threat, the life sentence constituted cruel and unusual punishment. Nevertheless, because defendant intentionally killed the victim without legally adequate provocation, he could be and ought to be punished for second-degree murder.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of attempted robbery was affirmed. A standing crop, once severed from realty, was property that could be the subject of a robbery or an attempted robbery. The judgment convicting defendant of first-degree felony murder was modified to reflect a conviction of murder in the second degree where the mandatory punishment imposed under the former conviction constituted cruel and unusual punishment.

 

208 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Press v. Lucky Stores, Inc.


L.A. No. 31718


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 311; 667 P.2d 704; 193 Cal. Rptr. 900; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 218



August 18, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-301475, Dickran M. Tevrizian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed insofar as it establishes that plaintiffs are entitled to an award of fees for the services rendered by their attorneys. As to the amount awarded, the judgment is reversed and the case remanded for further proceedings consistent with Serrano III and its progeny.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, citizen and labor group, challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded a small amount of attorney fees to plaintiffs, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5, in their suit against defendant shopping plaza store. Defendant had denied plaintiffs access to the premises to circulate initiative petitions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, citizen and labor group, sought to qualify an initiative measure and circulated petitions at several privately owned shopping centers. Defendant shopping plaza store ordered plaintiffs to stop and threatened plaintiffs with arrest. Plaintiffs obtained injunctive relief and sought attorney fees. The trial court awarded a small amount of attorney fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5, based on the number of signatures plaintiffs obtained in defendant's plaza. The court affirmed the award of attorney fees, noting that plaintiffs' action clarified that previously established law applied to small shopping centers, and the public benefitted from the enforcement of fundamental constitutional rights. Further, plaintiffs used the injunction to gain access to other shopping centers, and plaintiffs had no pecuniary interest in the outcome of the litigation. In reversing the trial court on the amount of the fee, the court opined that the starting point of every award must be a calculation of the attorney's services in terms of the time expended. The lodestar adjustment method of calculation and guidelines were required to insure objectivity. The court remanded for recalculation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which found that plaintiffs, citizen and labor group, were entitled to an award of attorney fees. The court reversed and remanded the amount of attorney's fees that was awarded. The trial court had awarded an amount that had no rational relationship to the skill, time and effort expended by plaintiffs' attorneys.

 

209 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Int'l Ass'n of Firefighters v. City of San Diego


L.A. No. 31523


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 292; 667 P.2d 675; 193 Cal. Rptr. 871; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 217



August 15, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 421183, Louis M. Welsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we reverse the judgment of the trial court on defendants' appeal and affirm its judgment on plaintiff's cross-appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, City of San Diego and San Diego City Employees Retirement Board, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) invalidating an increase in the rate of contribution to defendant city's retirement system required of its firefighter employees. Plaintiff firefighters association cross-appealed from a denial of attorney's fees.


OVERVIEW: Defendant San Diego City Employees Retirement Board raised the average rate of contribution to defendant city's retirement system fund that it required of its safety members, including firefighters. Plaintiff firefighters association brought an action against defendant board and defendant City of San Diego challenging the increase. The trial court invalidated the increase because, although defendant board was justified in increasing the contribution, it was estopped from doing so because of certain statements made in a retirement system handbook distributed to employees. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment. The court held that the change in the contribution rate did not modify the employees' vested contractual rights but rather made adjustments that were provided for in the city ordinances governing the plan. The court also held that the trial court erred in interpreting statements made in the retirement plan handbook. The court held that statements in the handbook that the rate of an employee's contribution would not change "with his age" did not preclude changes for other reasons. The denial of plaintiff firefighters association's request for attorney's fees was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment invalidating an increase in the rate of contribution to a retirement system, holding that defendant city employees retirement board was permitted by ordinance to raise the rate of employee contributions to the retirement plan and that statements in the retirement plan handbook did not estop defendants from effecting the increase. The court affirmed the denial of attorney's fees to plaintiff firefighters association.

 

210 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Smith


Crim. No. 22273


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 251; 667 P.2d 149; 193 Cal. Rptr. 692; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 215



August 8, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 23744, Max Wilcox, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) that convicted him of robbery, arguing that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress his confession where it was obtained in violation of his privilege against self-incrimination as guaranteed by Cal. Const. art. I, § 15.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for possession of stolen property after he stole a car. The arresting officer transported him to the police station, intending to interrogate him about the stolen property. Defendant was advised of his Miranda rights, and he declined to answer any questions. He was moved to the county jail, where his Miranda rights were again read to him, and he was questioned by a county sheriff, to whom he confessed his crime. Defendant moved to suppress his confession, and at the suppression hearing, the county sheriff testified that he had not been told that defendant had previously invoked his right to remain silent. Defendant appealed his conviction, arguing that the trial court improperly denied his motion. In reversing and remanding, the appellate court concluded that the trial court erred in denying defendant's suppression motion. The confession obtained after defendant had exercised his right to remain silent violated his privilege against self-incrimination under Cal. Const. art. I, § 15, and was thus inadmissible. Because the tainted confession was admitted for the jury's consideration, the judgment was reversed and a new trial ordered.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction for robbery was reversed and the matter remanded for a new trial. The trial court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress his confession where the confession was obtained through police interrogation conducted after defendant had invoked his right to remain silent under Miranda.

 

211 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Peterson v. City of San Diego


L.A. No. 31641


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 225; 666 P.2d 975; 193 Cal. Rptr. 533; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 213



August 4, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 469113, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the secrecy provision does not preclude voting by mail. The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff registered voter challenged the order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that denied his action for declaratory and inductive relief challenging the validity of defendant City of San Diego's election by mail as unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. II, § 7, because it was not "secret."


OVERVIEW: After defendant City of San Diego had adopted an ordinance approving a lease in connection with construction of a convention center, it received a referendary petition and authorized a special municipal election to submit approval of the lease to the voters. The city council provided that the election was to be conducted by mail ballot. Before the election was held, plaintiff registered voter sought declaratory and injunctive relief challenging the validity of the election. A preliminary injunction was denied, the election was conducted, and the voters rejected the lease. The trial court held that the election by mail did not violate Cal. Const. art. II, § 7, the constitutional provision for voting secrecy, and plaintiff appealed. The court affirmed and held that voting by mail was often more convenient than voting at the polling place and mail voting increased voter participation. The court found that any concern for voter fraud was outweighed by the compelling interest to secure citizen participation in the maintenance of representative government.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff registered voter's request for injunctive relief and held that the compelling interest was to secure citizen participation in the maintenance of representative government and that interest was equally applicable to absentee and mail ballot voting. The court concluded that the secrecy provision of the California Constitution did not preclude voting by mail.

 

212 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Allen v. Bd. of Admin.


L.A. No. 31702


Supreme Court of California


34 Cal. 3d 114; 665 P.2d 534; 192 Cal. Rptr. 762; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 205



July 11, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 7, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 313948, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: By their constitutional revision of 1966, the people of California elected, in part, to redress inequitably low legislative salaries -- reflecting the increased duties of annual legislative sessions -- while at the same time confining retirement beneficiaries to those gains reasonably to be anticipated from their employment contract. With respect to legislators who retired before the salaries were increased so substantially, that restriction took the form of withholding unforeseen or windfall advantages which bore no relation to the fundamental theory and objective of the LRS, as protected by provision for cost-of-living adjustments. Such action by the people constituted neither the repudiation of any debt nor the unconstitutional impairment of an employment contract. For these reasons, we reverse the trial court's judgment and remand for further proceedings consistent with our opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administrative retirement board challenged a judgment from the Los Angeles County Superior Court (California), which declared that the restrictions of Cal. Const. art. IV, § 4 and Cal. Gov't Code 9359.11 could not be constitutionally applied in computing the retirement allowances of respondents, former state legislators and their surviving spouses.


OVERVIEW: After a change in state retirement laws, respondents former state legislators, applied to appellant retirement board for recalculation of their retirement allowances free of restrictions imposed by Cal. Gov't Code § 9359.11 and Cal. Const. art. IV, § 4. Appellant rejected the application. Respondents then filed a suit for writ of mandate to compel appellant to grant the relief requested. Respondent argued that application of the constitutional and statutory limitations to the computation of their retirement benefits impaired their contractual pension rights in violation of the contract clauses of the federal and state constitutions. The trial court accepted respondents' contentions and issued the mandate. On appeal, the court determined that respondents' claimed contractual rights were theoretical and were not supported by the contract clauses of the federal and state constitutions. The court stated that respondents could not reasonably expect under the terms of their employment contract to obtain retirement allowances on the basis of a salary increase accomplished by a constitutional revision to redress inequitably low legislative salaries. Judgment was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment that declared that restrictions imposed by state constitutional and statutory law could not be applied in computing respondent former state legislators' retirement allowances was reversed and remanded because the limitations did not implicate the contract clauses of the federal and state constitutions. Additionally, respondents could not reasonably expect to obtain allowances on the basis of a salary increase.

 

213 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Sumner v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


L.A. No. 31667


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 965; 663 P.2d 534; 191 Cal. Rptr. 811; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 193; 48 Cal. Comp. Cases 369



June 2, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  The board's continued use of current form 15, in light of our decision in Johnson, is impermissible, and any compromise and release executed on this form after the finality of this decision, which does not conform to the Johnson mandate, shall not be deemed to cover death benefits. The order of the WCAB in this case denying applicant's claim as barred by the compromise and release is annulled and applicant allowed to proceed with her claim in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widow appealed an order from respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California), which denied her request for death benefits for her husband because petitioner's husband had signed a compromise and release of claims pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 5000(b) that released respondent husband's former employer from all liability against future claims.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner widow sought review of an order from respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (board), which upheld denial of her claim for death benefits from respondent husband's former employer because he had signed a compromise and release form in accordance with Cal. Lab. Code § 5000(b). On review the court annulled respondent board's order and instructed that petitioner be allowed to proceed with her claim in accordance with their instructions. The court found that respondent board had failed to comply with their earlier directive to revise the compromise and release forms when used with disability claims and that the continued use of these forms could deprive persons of their due process rights because it was questionable whether they knowingly and voluntarily had signed away their rights to death benefits. The court found that the forms contained ambiguous language and that, when they were used in conjunction with disability claims, there were no procedural safeguards to ensure that a person knowingly had settled all claims, including any death benefits his dependants might receive.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the order of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (board) and instructed that petitioner widow be allowed to proceed with her claim. The court held that respondent board could not continue to use the current compromise and release form in settling disability claims because the ambiguous language called into question whether the release of death benefits was knowing and voluntary.

 

214 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Reed


Crim. No. 22595


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 914; 663 P.2d 216; 191 Cal. Rptr. 658; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 190



May 26, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 20, 1983. Richardson, J., and Kaus, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: The Municipal Court for the Newhall Judicial District is ordered to modify the conditions of probation in People v. Allen Eugene Reed, No. M9186, by eliminating the requirement that petitioner register as a sex offender pursuant to section 290. The order to show cause, having served its purpose, is discharged and the writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant who had been convicted of soliciting lewd or dissolute conduct under Cal. Penal Code § 647(a) and who was therefore required to register as a sex offender under Cal. Penal Code § 290 sought a writ of habeas corpus, arguing that the registration requirement for those convicted under § 290 constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was convicted of soliciting lewd or dissolute conduct under Cal. Penal Code § 647(a), sought a writ of habeas corpus, arguing that the requirement that those convicted under § 647(a) register as sex offenders pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 290 constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The court agreed, concluding that the requirement was void under Cal. Const. art. I, § 17. Finding first that the registration requirement was punitive in nature, it held that it was disproportionate to the crime committed, writing that the offense was relatively minor and that no "victim," other than an undercover officer, was required, that those convicted of arguably more serious sex-related misdemeanors such as producing child pornography, pimping and pandering, raping a person incapable of giving consent, prostitution, and engaging in lewd conduct in front of a child were not required to register, and that most other jurisdictions did not impose a registration requirement for those convicted of similar crimes. It ordered the trial court to modify defendant's conditions of probation to strike the registration requirement and denied the writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: Agreeing with defendant convicted of soliciting lewd or dissolute conduct that the requirement that persons convicted of this offense register as sex offenders was cruel and unusual punishment and was therefore void, the court ordered that the trial court modify defendant's conditions of probation to strike the registration requirement. It denied the writ of habeas corpus sought by defendant.

 

215 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


American Bank & Trust Co. v. Community Hospital


S.F. No. 24171.


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 674; 660 P.2d 829; 190 Cal. Rptr. 371; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 172



March 31, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehg. granted June 15, 1983 (See 36 C.3d 359). Withdrawn from Bound Volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 375491, David S. Lull, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant hospital sought review of a judgment entered in a medical malpractice action in favor of plaintiff patient by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California). The trial court denied defendant's request that it order the periodic payment of future damages more than $ 50,000, on the ground that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 667.7, a section of the 1975 Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patient brought an action for medical malpractice against defendant hospital after she was injured in the shower while hospitalized for cancer treatment. The trial court denied defendant's request to order the periodic payment of future damages more than $ 50,000 as provided by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 667.7, a provision of the 1975 Medical Injury compensation Reform Act. In affirming the trial judge, the court determined that § 667.7 imposed serious detriments on the victims of malpractice, and that they derived no quid pro quo from the legislation to compensate for those disadvantages. Employing the rational-relationship test, the court ruled that § 667.7 violated the equal protection provisions of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1, by setting the victim of medical malpractice apart from other tort victims and by limiting its application to medical malpractice victims who suffered future damages of $ 50,000 or more, and permitting all others to obtain lump-sum payments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of plaintiff patient and against defendant hospital. The court ruled that the provision of the 1975 Medical Injury Compensation Reform Act, which permitted a judgment for periodic payment of "future damages" to be awarded against a "provider of health care services" violated state and federal equal protection guarantees insofar as it applied to judgments against hospitals.

 

216 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Roder


Crim. No. 22442


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 491; 658 P.2d 1302; 189 Cal. Rptr. 501; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 157



February 22, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 72347, Roland K. Hall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The conviction of receiving stolen property is reversed. The conviction of possession of more than one ounce of marijuana is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) his conviction, based on a jury verdict, of receiving stolen property under Cal. Penal Code § 496.


OVERVIEW: After a jury trial, defendant was convicted of receiving stolen property. Defendant argued trial court erred in giving instructions that implied jury was required, under Cal. Penal Code § 496, to presume defendant knew the items at issue were stolen. Analyzing the statute, court noted that standing alone, the statute stated a mandatory presumption of the crime's knowledge element, which was unconstitutional. Nevertheless, following rule requiring interpretation of a statute to preserve its constitutionality when possible, court held that, when read in conjunction with state evidentiary laws, § 496 allowed for only a permissive inference of the knowledge element. Thus, court held trial court's instructions implying jury was required to presume the knowledge element of § 496 were erroneous, and that a proper instruction on remand would inform the jury of its option to infer knowledge.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed, because trial court's instruction implying jury was required to presume criminal knowledge was erroneous.

 

217 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



Gonzalez v. Comm'n on Judicial Performance


L.A. No. 31572


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 359; 657 P.2d 372; 188 Cal. Rptr. 880; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 147



February 7, 1983 



DISPOSITION:  We recognize that since the advent of the Commission on Judicial Performance the bench has been governed by a higher standard of conduct than the bar. ( Geiler, supra, 10 Cal.3d at p. 287.) Because we do not find that Judge Gonzalez' misconduct rises to the level of moral turpitude, dishonesty, or corruption, we order that despite his removal from office he be permitted to practice law in California, on condition that he pass the Professional Responsibility Examination required of applicants seeking readmission or reinstatement to the bar. (Cal. Rules of Court, rule 952(d).) All attorneys who have been suspended from practice must pass this examination before being readmitted ( Segretti v. State Bar (1976) 15 Cal.3d 878, 890-891 [126 Cal.Rptr. 793, 544 P.2d 929]), and we see no reason to exempt from this requirement a judge who, on removal from office, is automatically suspended from practicing law. (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 18, subd. (d).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judge appealed the decision of respondent Commission on Judicial Performance (California) sustaining 21 counts of wilful misconduct and conduct prejudicial, including repeated ethnic and sexual slurs.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner judge appealed the decision of respondent Commission on Judicial Performance, which disqualified him from acting as a judge. Petitioner was accused of making sexual and ethnic slurs against defendants, maligning colleagues, and disregarding judicial procedure. The court agreed with all but 1 of the 21 charges respondent leveled against petitioner. The court held that petitioner's misconduct was persistent and pervasive. Specifically, the court found that petitioner's half-off sentencing bargain day for defendants who pled guilty showed an improper regard for judicial procedures. Further, petitioner improperly used his judicial position to intercede in criminal matters on behalf of family and friends. Also, the court held that petitioner failed to grasp the substance or seriousness of the charges against him. Despite his removal, petitioner was allowed to practice law after passing the professional responsibility examination because his conduct did not rise to the level of moral turpitude, dishonesty, or corruption.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that petitioner judge be removed from the bench for persistent and pervasive misconduct, including repeated ethnic and sexual slurs. The court also held that although petitioner should be removed from the bench, petitioner could continue to practice law on the condition that he pass the professional responsibility examination.

 

218 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People ex rel. Van De Kamp v. Am. Art Enters.


L.A. No. 31520


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 328; 656 P.2d 1170; 188 Cal. Rptr. 740; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 145



January 31, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 2, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 37091, William P. Hogoboom, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owners of a building, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that imposed a damage award of $ 168,000 on remand after a court of appeals decision that found the property constituted a nuisance under the Red Light Abatement Law, Cal. Penal Code §§ 11225-11235.


OVERVIEW: Appellants owned a building that was used as the headquarters of their business of publishing and distributing sexually explicit matter conceded not to be obscene. The people filed an action that alleged that the constituted a nuisance under the Red Light Abatement Law (Act), Cal. Penal Code §§ 11225- 11235, and sought a permanent injunction against the prohibited activity. The action came before the court after the trial court, on remand from a court of appeals decision that found the building was used for prostitution, imposed a monetary fine in lieu of other sanctions as the nuisance had been voluntarily abated. The case presented a narrow question of statutory interpretation as to whether the provisions of the Act authorized an award of damages against an owner of property that has been used for purposes prohibited by the act. The court reversed the judgment on the basis that although the statute did authorize a variety of sanctions to cover the costs of closing the property and the costs of prosecution, those remedies were explicitly in rem, consistent with the primary object to "reform" the property and insure that the nuisance was abated, not to punish for past acts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that imposed a monetary fine on appellants, owners of a building, in lieu of other sanctions because such damages were not included among the sanctions specifically enumerated in the Red Light Abatement Law (Act), and because past authorities established that the primary purpose of the Act was to "reform" the property in question rather than to punish the property owner.

 

219 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



People v. Spears


Crim. No. 22083


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 279; 655 P.2d 1289; 188 Cal. Rptr. 454; 1983 Cal. LEXIS 142



January 20, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 56911, Sheldon H. Grossfeld, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The sentences imposed on counts one and two are modified to two consecutive terms of 25 years to life. (§ 190.) As modified, the judgment is affirmed. Because defendant is no longer under a sentence of "imprisonment for life" within the meaning of Welfare and Institutions Code section 1731.5, subdivision (a)(2), he is entitled as a matter of law to be considered for commitment to the Youth Authority pursuant to Welfare and Institutions Code section 707.2. (In re Jeanice D. (1980) supra, 28 Cal.3d 210; People v. Black (1982) 32 Cal.3d 1 [184 Cal.Rptr. 454, 648 P.2d 104].) The cause is therefore remanded with directions to refer defendant to the Youth Authority and thereafter to determine the appropriate disposition pursuant to section 707.2.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant minor sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which convicted him of two counts of murder with special circumstances and sentenced him to two consecutive terms of life imprisonment without possibility of parole. Defendant challenged the validity of the sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the validity of the sentence imposed upon him after his conviction of two counts of murder with special circumstances, alleging that minors were not to be sentenced to life imprisonment without possibility of parole (LWOP), even under amendments meant to "toughen" the state death penalty law. The court modified the sentences imposed by the trial court from two consecutive terms of LWOP, to two consecutive terms of 25 years to life. The court affirmed the judgment as modified. The court reviewed the text of the recent provisions, the legislative history, the Attorney's General's summary of the amendments and compared these to the old law, and using the same tools of statutory construction and interpretation it had used in concluding that the former law did not impose LWOP on minors, determined that the amended law similarly did not impose LWOP on minors even though the amendments were highly touted as being meant to "toughen" the law. The court held that defendants were entitled to the benefit of the doubt in interpreting ambiguous penal provisions and that the new provisions were devoid of evidence that the legislature meant to depart from the status quo.


OUTCOME: The court modified defendant minor's sentence to two consecutive terms of 25 years to life and as modified, affirmed the judgment, but remanded to the trial court to refer defendant to the Youth Authority. The court modified the sentences imposed by the trial court because the court found that the recently amended death penalty provisions did not allow the imposition of sentences for life imprisonment without possibility of parole on minors.

 

220 of 2274 DOCUMENTS



In re Stanworth


Crim. No. 22522


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 176; 654 P.2d 1311; 187 Cal. Rptr. 783; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 263



December 23, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 97271, Henry J. Broderick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant people challenged the order of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which granted respondent defendant habeas corpus relief to the extent of ordering that defendant was entitled, on constitutional ex post facto and equal protection grounds, to a second parole hearing under the guidelines in effect at the time he was sentenced, the Indeterminate Sentence Law, former Cal. Penal Code, §1168, repealed eff. Jan. 1, 1977.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment under the Indeterminate Sentence Law (ISL), former Cal. Penal Code, §1168 ( repealed eff. Jan. 1, 1977). The issue was whether he was entitled to parole release consideration under both ISL and the administrative guidelines which were in effect at the time he was sentenced, and also under the Uniform Determinate Sentencing Act of 1976 (DSL), Cal. Penal Code, §1170 et seq. and regulations. The court held that ex post facto principles, under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9, required that his right to parole be considered under both laws, and affirmed the trial court's grant of habeas corpus relief. The court reasoned that the new DSL rules, to defendant's prejudice, set a longer range of base terms for defendant's crime, and generally stressed the criminal activities of the inmate rather than, as under the ISL rules, any social or personal factors. The court held that these changes constituted a prejudicial change in punishment enacted after commission of defendant's crime, and as such sufficiently altered the standard of punishment to defendant's prejudice as to violate constitutional ex post facto principles.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in granting habeas corpus relief because respondent defendant was entitled, on constitutional ex post facto grounds, to a second parole hearing under both the guidelines in effect at the time he was sentenced and new, more restrictive standards imposed post-conviction.

Pacific Legal Foundation v. California Coastal Com.


L.A. No. 31564


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 158; 655 P.2d 306; 188 Cal. Rptr. 104; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 262; 18 ERC (BNA) 1856



December 20, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 66555, James E. Cunningham, Sr., Judge, * and Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C268254, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: In Jackson the motion for attorney fees is denied. In Pacific Legal Foundation the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff legal foundation sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) that granted summary judgment in favor defendant coastal commission in an action challenging the validity of access policies by the commission and seeking a declaration that the polices were invalid.


OVERVIEW: The Coastal Act was created to preserve existing public access rights to the shoreline. Two causes of action had been filed against defendant coastal commission. While the court found it inappropriate for the court of appeals to consolidate the cases, it determined that bifurcation would only delay a result. Two property owners filed an action for mandamus requesting the commission strike a specific permit condition requiring dedication of an easement for permit approval. Defendant coastal commission voluntarily dismissed the action. Plaintiff legal foundation, representing the property owners, sought a declaratory judgment to correct asserted invalid public access guidelines. The lower court denied the relief requested, granting summary judgment to defendant coastal commission. Upon review, the court held that a basic prerequisite to judicial review of an administrative act was the existence of a ripe controversy. Plaintiff argued that no specific application of the guidelines was involved, rather, the case was a general challenge on access policies. As such, the court held the issue of whether the guidelines were valid was not a ripe controversy and, therefore, not reviewable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of plaintiff legal foundation's request for declaratory judgment, holding the cause of action was not ripe for controversy, because plaintiff was merely challenging the statutory and constitutional validity of defendant coastal commission's access policies.

 

222 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Easley


Crim. No. 21117.


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 65; 654 P.2d 1272; 187 Cal. Rptr. 745; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 258



December 10, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehg. granted Feb. 23, 1983 (See 34 C.3d 858). Withdrawn from Bound Volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. CR6702, Ralph M. Drummond, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which convicted defendant of murder with special circumstances after two people were killed in a murder-for-hire conspiracy. During the sentencing phase, the jury fixed defendant's penalty at death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder with special circumstances and sentenced to death. Defendant sought review and the court affirmed. The court found that excluding jurors who would vote against death had not created an impartial jury and also found that a particular exclusion was proper because the juror clearly stated death would be voted against. Furthermore, the court found a valid search warrant because probable cause warranted a search out-of-county and of multiple locations, omissions in the supporting affidavit were immaterial or unintentional, and seized evidence not listed in the warrant was rationally linked to the criminal behavior. The court also held that during the guilt and penalty phases, hearsay evidence had been admissible under the coconspirator exception of Cal. Evid. Code § 1223. In addition, the court found the jury had been properly instructed on accomplices during the guilt phase and reasonable doubt regarding a prior crime during the penalty phase. Finally, the court found that a unanimous verdict of guilt was not required for consideration of the prior crime and that consideration of the prior crime as an aggravating factor had not denied due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment convicting defendant of murder with special circumstances and imposing death because jurors were properly excluded, a search warrant was valid, and coconspirator's exception made hearsay admissible. Also, accomplice and reasonable doubt instructions were properly given, unanimous guilt of a prior crime was not required, and consideration of the prior crime as an aggravating factor had not denied due process.

People v. Robertson


Crim. Nos. 20577, 21119


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 21; 655 P.2d 279; 188 Cal. Rptr. 77; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 257



December 10, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 19, 1983. Mosk, J., and Richardson, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. CR 34455, Rex W. Cranmer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to penalty, and affirmed in all other respects. The order to show cause is discharged and the writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which convicted defendant of first degree murder and special circumstances after the stabbing deaths of two women. During the penalty phase, defendant's punishment was fixed at death.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder and special circumstances but reversed defendant's death sentence. In affirming, the court found no reversible error in the failure to instruct the jury not to consider penalty when considering guilt and in counsel's failures to object to a warrantless arrest, statements made absent Miranda warnings, statements made after defendant's request to speak to a psychologist, and to the prosecutorial guilt argument. The court also found no error in counsel's mental defense presentation and voir dire statements. Although the court found counsel's failure to object to prior crimes testimony was prejudicial, the court found no reversible error because of the overwhelming evidence. The court also found Cal. Penal Code § 190.6 was valid and found the special circumstances findings sufficiently supported. In reversing, the court found reversible error in the failure to give a reasonable doubt instruction on defendant's other crimes and the failure to instruct on impairment resulting from mental defects. Furthermore, the court found that foreclosing the jury from weighing sympathetic factors was erroneous under U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part a judgment convicting defendant of murder and special circumstances because reversible error was not found in the trial court's and counsel's actions during the guilt phase and the special circumstances and reversed in part a death sentence because the jury was not instructed on reasonable doubt for other crimes evidence, was not instructed on mental defects, and was foreclosed from weighing sympathetic elements.

John A. v. San Bernardino City Unified Sch. Dist.


L.A. No. 31391


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 301; 654 P.2d 242; 187 Cal. Rptr. 472; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 267



December 10, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 188894, Don A. Turner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to the trial court to issue a peremptory writ of mandate commanding the school district to expunge plaintiff's expulsion from his record.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff student appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate to compel readmission to high school by defendant school district.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff student was involved in an altercation after a high school football game and was expelled by defendant school district. A trial court denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate to compel defendant to readmit him. Plaintiff appealed the trial court's judgment. The court reversed the trial court's judgment and ordered the expulsion removed from plaintiff's record. The court concluded that under Cal. Educ. Code § 48904.5 and Cal. Educ. Code § 48914, it was improper to expel plaintiff without the testimony of any of the witnesses to the altercation. By placing the power of expulsion in the governing board, the legislature manifested its intent that the board determine credibility and weigh evidence. The court determined that defendant failed to establish cause for expulsion by a preponderance of the evidence. However, the record fully supported the trial court's finding that a hearing was conducted in a fair and impartial manner, and the notice was sufficient under § 48914(b). The court concluded that without knowing what matters plaintiff sought to assert at the hearing, defendant was not in a position to determine the availability of free legal services to plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a trial court's judgment denying plaintiff student's petition for writ of mandate compelling readmission to high school by defendant school district because under the state education code, it was improper to expel plaintiff without the testimony of the witnesses to an altercation. The court directed the trial court to issue a peremptory writ of mandate commanding defendant to expunge plaintiff's expulsion from his record.

Odle v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24362


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 932; 654 P.2d 225; 187 Cal. Rptr. 455; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 255



December 10, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied January 19, 1983. Grodin, J., did not Participate therein. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Application should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ of mandamus is discharged and the petition for writ of mandamus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought mandamus relief of a judgment from respondent Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which denied petitioner's motions for a change of venue in petitioner's trial for a stabbing death and for the shooting death of a police officer. Petitioner twice moved for a change of venue on the ground that there was reasonable likelihood that a fair trial would not be received because of prejudicial publicity.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner's arrest for a stabbing death and for the shooting death of a police officer was extensively covered by the media for two weeks. Prior to petitioner's trial, petitioner filed motions for a change in venue, claiming there was a reasonable likelihood that a fair trial could not be received in the county because of prejudicial publicity. After respondent superior court denied the motions, petitioner sought mandamus relief, and the court denied the relief. In determining prejudice, the court examined the extent of news coverage, the community's size, the offense's nature and gravity, and the standings of the victims and petitioner in the community. Although the court found the status of the victims and the accused were neutral in nature, the court examined the size of the county and held that a population of over 666,000 favored denying a venue change. The court also found that although low-key and non-hostile publicity was extensive for two weeks, the amount of time between trial and publicity weighed against a venue change. After weighing these factors, the court held that the reasonable likelihood of an unfair trial had not been established.


OUTCOME: The court denied mandamus relief of a judgment from respondent superior court which denied petitioner's motions for change of venue because the extensive publicity of a two-week period that followed the crimes had not established a reasonable likelihood that a fair trial could not be had in the county and because a sufficient amount of time had passed between the period of extensive media coverage and the trial date.

In re Jerald C.


S.F. No. 24392.


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 1; 654 P.2d 745; 187 Cal. Rptr. 562; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 256



December 10, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehg. granted Mar. 10, 1983 (See 36 C.3d 1). Withdrawn from Bound Volume.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 64723, John D. Brokenshire, Referee.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, father of a minor, sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which ordered the father to reimburse respondent county for the costs of the care and support of the juvenile while in state custody.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the order that had been entered by the lower court that ordered the appellant, father of a minor, to reimburse respondent county for the costs of the care and support of the juvenile while in custody of the state. The court held that the juvenile had been confined to the state institution for the public benefit due to criminal behavior. The court found that the state could not charge appellant with the operations of state functions when the purpose of the state function was to benefit the public as a whole. The court held that it was a violation of appellant's equal protection rights to be charged with the cost of a state function since the cost would fall upon a specific class of individuals and there would be a public benefit. The court held that the cost of maintaining the state institution could not be arbitrarily charged to one class in society and that the lower court erred when it ordered appellant to pay the county the cost of the confinement of his juvenile son.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court judgment that had ordered appellant, father of a juvenile, to pay respondent county for the cost of confinement because the assessment of state function costs to one class of individuals was an equal protection violation.

Citizens Against Forced Annexation v. Local Agency Formation Com.


L.A. No. 31414


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 816; 654 P.2d 193; 187 Cal. Rptr. 423; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 248



December 2, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 289937, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed, and the matter remanded to the superior court with directions to enter an order denying the request for a preliminary injunction.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants Local Agency Formation Commission of Los Angeles County appealed from a preliminary injunction issued by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which barred certification of results of an annexation election under the Municipal Organization Act of 1977, Cal. Gov't. Code §35000 et seq. Election voters were limited to residents of the territory to be annexed and did not include plaintiff annexing city residents.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff annexing city residents were granted a preliminary injunction barring defendant Local Agency Formation Commission of Los Angeles County from certifying the results favoring annexation after an election under the Municipal Organization Act of 1977 (Act), Cal. Gov't. Code § 35000 et seq., in which only residents of the territory to be annexed were allowed to vote, Cal. Gov't. Code §§ 35228, 35231. Plaintiffs maintained that the Act denied them equal protection by limiting the voters to residents of the territory to be annexed. The court reversed the injunction and held that the statutes were constitutional. The court held that the restrictions on voters were necessary to serve the compelling state interest of providing a means for residents of unincorporated areas to join a neighboring city and obtain the advantages of municipal government, which could not be achieved if the annexation election was open to residents of the annexing city who could reject annexation. The court held that § 35228 and §35231 survived the strict scrutiny applicable to provisions which limited fundamental rights, and did not compromise state or federal constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and remanded for entry of an order denying the preliminary injunction. The court concluded that the state had a compelling interest which could not have been achieved if the annexation election had been open to the residents of the annexing city. The court sustained the constitutionality of the challenged statutory restrictions under the strict scrutiny applicable to provisions limiting fundamental rights.

Fullerton Joint Union High School Dist. v. State Bd. of Education


L.A. No. 31415


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 779; 654 P.2d 168; 187 Cal. Rptr. 398; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 247



December 2, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 275518, Robert P. Kneeland, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court is reversed and the cause remanded with directions to modify the judgment in conformity with the views expressed herein. Since the modified judgment will afford Fullerton HSD substantially the relief it seeks, it shall recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both plaintiff school district and defendant State Board of Education appealed an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which prohibited official action on a school district secession plan, barred any election without compliance with the California Environmental Quality Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21050 et seq., and directed the board to vacate its approval.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff school district and defendant State Board of Education challenged the trial court's order finding that defendant's approval of a school district secession plan violated Cal. Educ. Code § 4200, prohibited official action on the plan, prohibited any election for the purposes of submitting the plan to the voters, directed defendant to vacate its approval of the plan, and prohibited any election conducted until the board complied with the requirements of the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA), Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21050 et seq. The court reversed and remanded, holding that because defendant's approval of the plan was not arbitrary, capricious, or unsupported by evidence, the trial court erred in barring further action, and defendant was to consider the plan as long as it conformed to CEQA requirements and did not exclude residents of plaintiff from voting in an election to approve the plan. The trial court's order prohibiting any election for purposes of submitting the plan to the voters was overbroad, and should have prohibited only an election limited to the residents of less than the entire plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's judgment in favor of plaintiff school district, which directed defendant State Board of Education to vacate its approval of a school district secession plan and prohibited any election submitting the plan for approval, because defendant's approval was not arbitrary or capricious, and the trial court's order prohibiting an election was overbroad.

Brown v. Superior Court


S.F. Nos. 24403, 24405


Supreme Court of California


33 Cal. 3d 242; 655 P.2d 1260; 188 Cal. Rptr. 425; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 266



November 1, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reprinted to Allow Incorporation of Order of Court, Opinion on Denial of Rehearing, and Response.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ issue, ordering respondent to vacate its judgment against petitioners and to enter judgment against real parties in interest.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two consolidated cases, petitioners, the Governor and the Controller of the State of California, along with the Administrative Officer of the Courts, challenged orders from respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) entered against them which permanently enjoined them from administering or otherwise taking action pursuant to a statute which significantly expanded the Court of Appeal system in California.


OVERVIEW: Two taxpayers, acting as real parties in interest, filed lawsuits seeking a declaration that a statute passed by the Legislature for the purpose of significantly expanding the Court of Appeal system in California, 1981 Cal. Stat. 959, was unconstitutional. Respondent superior court issued such a declaration as to § 6 of the statute, together with a permanent injunction against the taking of further measures to administer the statute or appropriate funds thereunder by petitioners, the Governor and the Controller of the State of California and the Administrative Officer of the Courts of the State of California. Accordingly, the court ordered the issuance of a peremptory writ requiring respondent to vacate its order.
