Torrance v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


L.A. No. 31517


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 371; 650 P.2d 1162; 185 Cal. Rptr. 645; 47 Cal. Comp. Cases 963; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 226



September 13, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied October 13, 1982.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review of a decision by respondent appeals board, which granted respondent state compensation fund's motion for dismissal of a worker's compensation contribution case, because a 1977 amendment to Cal. Lab. Code § 5500.5 had eliminated respondent fund's liability. Petitioner asserted that the amendment violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9.


OVERVIEW: Prior to his death, decedent fireman worked for petitioner city for 21 years. For 15 of those years respondent state compensation fund (fund) was the workers' compensation insurer for petitioner. Since July 1971, petitioner had not carried insurance. In March 1978, decedent died of lung cancer. His daughter filed an application for workers' compensation death benefits against petitioner and respondent fund. She claimed that her father's death was proximately caused by a cumulative injury that developed during the course of his employment with petitioner. Petitioner settled the claim and sought contribution from respondent fund for 72 percent of the settlement amount under Cal. Lab. Code § 5500.5. Respondent appeals board granted respondent fund's motion to dismiss, based on a 1977 amendment to § 5500.5. The court affirmed because petitioner had originally agreed to incorporate subsequent changes in the law of workers' compensation into its insurance agreements with respondent fund. The court could not complain that those changes impaired respondent fund's obligations in derogation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent appeals board because petitioner city originally agreed to incorporate subsequent changes in the law of workers' compensation into its insurance agreements with respondent state compensation fund (fund). The court could not complain that those changes unconstitutionally impaired respondent fund's obligations because petitioner had every reason to anticipate that its rights would change over time.

 

234 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Ford Dealers Assn. v. Department of Motor Vehicles


L.A. No. 31471


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 347; 650 P.2d 328; 185 Cal. Rptr. 453; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 225



September 7, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 222751, Charles H. Older, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court erred in finding the regulations invalid and in granting an injunction against their enforcement. The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant California Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV), defendant below, challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared regulations appellant DMV promulgated under Cal. Veh. Code §§ 11100-12104 to be invalid, and granted an injunction against their enforcement. Appellant car dealers association, plaintiff below,challenged the trial court's denial of some of their evidence.


OVERVIEW: Both parties appealed the trial court's order invalidating regulations promulgated by appellant California Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV), which were intended to effectuate Cal. Veh. Code § 11713(a). Appellant car dealers association challenged the trial court's refusal to admit evidence as to the reasonableness of one regulation, which the court upheld on appeal, holding that the record was limited to that which was before the administrative agency. The court reversed the trial court's judgment against appellant DMV, holding that the regulations did not exceed appellant DMV 's statutory authority under Cal. Veh. Code § 1651. The court held that the strict statutory construction standard was not appropriate, because false, deceptive, or misleading advertising was subject to restraint. The court held that appellant DMV had the discretion to decide which statements were misleading to the public, and held that the liability of licensees for the acts of their employees was consistent with general agency law. The court held that the regulations were not impermissibly vague.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment against appellant California Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV), because appellant DMV acted within its authority to promulgate regulations under the California Vehicle Code, and the challenged regulations were a reasonable effort to implement the statute in a fair and effective manner.

 

235 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Brosnahan v. Brown


S.F. No. 24441


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 236; 651 P.2d 274; 186 Cal. Rptr. 30; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 222



September 2, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Application of Petitioners Brosnahan and Raven for a Rehearing was Denied October 13, 1982. Bird, C. J., and Broussard, J., were of the Opinion that the Application should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ previously issued is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, taxpayers and voters, sought writs of mandate or prohibition against respondents, public officials and courts, challenging the manner in which Proposition 8, known as The Victims' Bill of Rights, was submitted to the voters, claiming various constitutional defects and objecting to the expenditure of public funds for its implementation. The matter was transferred from a California Court of Appeal as issues of great public importance.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, taxpayers and voters, sought peremptory writs of mandate or prohibition against respondents, public officials and courts, to bar the implementation, application and funding of Proposition 8, also known as The Victims' Bill of Rights. The court had issued an alternate writ placing the measure on the ballot and reserving consideration of substantive issues. The court discharged the alternate writ and denied the peremptory writ, holding that the initiative measures survived petitioners' constitutional objections. The single-subject requirement of Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d) was not violated, as the effects of the parts were reasonably germane to each other and to the general purpose or object of the initiative. Cal. Const. art. IV, § 9 forbade the amendment of a statute by reference to its title unless the section was re-enacted as amended. Any procedural defect in question was harmless as the initiative's severability clause rendered them a nullity, and Cal. Const. art. IV, § 9 did not apply to constitutional amendments. Applying a dual function analysis, the court held that the initiative would not greatly impair or destroy the efficacy of other governmental powers.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternate writ previously issued that placed The Victims' Bill of Rights on the ballot and reserved consideration of substantive issues, and denied the peremptory writ of mandate or prohibition sought by petitioners, tax payers and voters, that sought to bar respondents, city officials and courts, from implementing or applying the initiative and objecting to the use of public funds for its implementation.

 

236 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Metromedia, Inc. v. City of San Diego


L.A. No. 30782


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 180; 649 P.2d 902; 185 Cal. Rptr. 260; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 218



August 30, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. 332881 and 333292, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court enjoining enforcement of San Diego Ordinance No. 10795 (New Series) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States Supreme Court remanded the case with instructions to determine whether an ordinance banning outdoor advertising on billboards could be saved by a limiting judicial construction or by severance of unconstitutional provisions, in an action by plaintiff outdoor advertising companies to enjoin defendant city from enforcing San Diego, Cal., Ordinance 10795.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff outdoor advertising companies filed suit against defendant city seeking to ban the enforcement of an ordinance requiring the removal of, and preventing the erection of billboards within the city limits. The trial court issued an injunction banning enforcement of the ordinance and the California Supreme Court reversed and upheld the ordinance. The United States Supreme Court reversed and held that the ordinance's prohibition on noncommercial billboards violated the U. S. Const. amend. I. On remand, the court affirmed the original order enjoining enforcement of the ordinance because the ordinance could not fairly and reasonably be construed in a manner that would preserve its constitutionality. The court found that the intention of the city council in enacting the billboard was to include noncommercial billboards and that any other interpretation of, or severance of the ordinance which preserved its constitutionality did not give effect to the city council's intent.


OUTCOME: In an action by plaintiff outdoor advertising companies, the court affirmed the order enjoining defendant city from enforcing an ordinance that banned advertising on outdoor billboards, because the ordinance as written violated the First Amendment and it could not fairly and reasonably be construed to preserve both its intent and its constitutionality.

 

237 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City and County of San Francisco v. Farrell


S.F. No. 24177


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 47; 648 P.2d 935; 184 Cal. Rptr. 713; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 209



August 5, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied September 30, 1982. Richardson, J., and Kaus, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought the issuance of a writ of mandate to compel respondent city controller to certify that funds were available from the proceeds of certain increases in payroll and gross receipt taxes to pay for the improvement of the elevator system at a hospital owned by petitioner.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city sought to use the proceeds of increases in the payroll and gross receipts taxes to make improvements to the elevator system at a hospital it owned. Respondent city controller refused to certify that the funds were available because he maintained that the tax increases in question constituted a "special tax," and that therefore the collection of the tax increases was illegal because the tax was not approved by two-thirds of the electorate as required by Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4. On petition for a writ of mandate, the court found in favor of petitioner and issued a writ of mandate that required respondent to certify that the funds were available. The court applied a strict construction of the language of § 4 and determined that the term "special taxes" meant taxes that were levied for a specific purpose rather than a levy placed in the general fund to be utilized for general governmental purposes. The court noted that its construction would further the purpose of § 4 while ascribing some meaning to every word used by that section.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner city's motion and issued a peremptory writ of mandate which required respondent city controller to certify that funds were available from the proceeds of increases in the payroll and gross receipts tax.

 

238 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Black


Crim. No. 22228


Supreme Court of California


32 Cal. 3d 1; 648 P.2d 104; 184 Cal. Rptr. 454; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 212



July 29, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 71471, Harry F. Brauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded with directions to determine the appropriate disposition consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California) that accepted his nolo contendere plea to false imprisonment, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 236 and rejected his request for sentencing as a youth under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707.2.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged in a juvenile court. Pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707, he was remanded to the adult court. Appellant pled nolo contendere to false imprisonment, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 236. At the request of the prosecutor, with appellant's concurrence, the sentencing hearing was adjourned from February 15, 1980 to February 22, 1980 so the presentence report could be completed. Appellant turned 18 on February 19, 1980. The judge denied appellant's request to be sentenced as a juvenile under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707.2. Appellant sought review. In construing legislation, the court was required to ascertain the legislative intent. Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707.2 was designed to aid the trial court in making its determination as to the proper sentence of a youthful offender. The court found the word "minor" in § 707.2 included any person who was within the age of those subject to training as a youthful offender. The court reversed because appellant's right to evaluation as a youthful offender, and his opportunity to receive a youthful commitment could not be denied because appellant turned 18 during the pendency of the proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant's conviction because he had the right to evaluation as a youthful offender, and his opportunity to receive a youthful commitment should not have been denied because he turned 18 during the pendency of the proceeding.

 

239 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Engert)


S.F. No. 24338


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 3d 797; 647 P.2d 76; 183 Cal. Rptr. 800; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 198



July 1, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied July 28, 1982. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged; the petition for peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned the court for writs of mandate to compel respondent superior court to set aside orders striking, as unconstitutionally vague, special circumstance allegations based on Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(14) in separate prosecutions for murder.


OVERVIEW: Respondent court ordered special circumstance allegations that murders were especially heinous, atrocious, and cruel, manifesting exceptional depravity as described in Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(14) stricken on grounds that it was unconstitutionally vague and violative of due process. The court found it was difficult to assign any specific content to the pejoratives contained in § 190.2(a)(14) and that none of the terms met the standards of precision and certainty required of statutes which rendered persons eligible for punishment, either as elements of a charged crime or as a charged special circumstance. The court concluded that § 190.2(a)(14) was unconstitutionally vague and violative of the due process clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7(a), 15. The court denied the state's petition for a peremptory writ and found that Cal. Const. art. I, § 27 was not intended to insulate every potential capital case from the requirement of state constitutional due process.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writ and denied the state's petition for peremptory writ. The court found that special circumstance allegations contained in informations in separate prosecutions for murder were unconstitutionally vague and violative of the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and of the Constitution of the State of California.

 

240 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Flaherty


S.F. No. 24307


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 3d 637; 646 P.2d 179; 183 Cal. Rptr. 508; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 189



June 17, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Shasta County, No. 62049, Richard W. Abbe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father, in a petition for a hearing, challenged the decision of the California Court of Appeal that affirmed the trial court's order denying him child support and ordering the dividing of the transportation costs for his daughters' visits to and from respondent mother.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was awarded custody of his daughter upon the dissolution of his marriage. The division of transportation costs was included in the order and he was denied child support. Appellant challenged the trial court's denial of child support and the division of transportation costs. The court of appeal affirmed the trial court and fined appellant's attorney for filing a frivolous appeal pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 907. Appellant was granted a hearing by the court. The grant of the petition nullified the penalty and vacated the court of appeal's opinion. Appellant argued that the court based its denial of child support on a Cal. Civ. Code § 196, which was a sex-biased statute. The court found that appellant did not provide any support for the sex-based argument and that the trial court did examine the wife's earning capacity when it denied child support and divided the transportation costs. There was no abuse of discretion by the trial court. The court further concluded that the appeal was not frivolous, that there was no evidence of subjective bad faith, and that sanctioning of the attorneys should not occur prior to following basic fundamental due process principles.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court holding that denial of appellant father's child support request was not solely based on an alleged sex-biased statute. The court further concluded that the court of appeal violated fundamental principles of due process by sanctioning appellant's attorney.

 

241 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Long Beach v. Bozek


L.A. No. 31448


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 3d 527; 645 P.2d 137; 183 Cal. Rptr. 86; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 166



June 1, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 325047, Ronald H. Prenner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City of Long Beach challenged an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which dismissed appellant's malicious prosecution action against respondent citizen on the basis that municipalities should not be permitted to sue for malicious prosecution.


OVERVIEW: Respondent citizen field suit against appellant City of Long Beach for false imprisonment, false arrest, and other causes of action. The jury found for appellant, and appellant instituted a malicious prosecution action against respondent. The trial court sustained respondent's general demurrer to the complaint, and the court, viewing the order as one of dismissal, affirmed. The court found that the right to petition the government, like the other rights contained in U.S. Const. amend. I and in the Cal. Const. Declaration of Rights, was accorded a paramount place in the democratic system. Because the act of filing suit against a governmental entity represented an exercise of the right of petition, it was absolutely privileged. Thus, the court found that governmental entities may not maintain actions for malicious prosecution against those who have previously sued such entities without success. The court stated that appellant could perhaps obtain redress for its litigation expenses through statutory remedies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant City of Long Beach's malicious prosecution action against respondent citizen. The court found that governmental entities may not maintain actions for malicious prosecution against those who have previously sued such entities without success; the bringing of such suits is absolutely privileged and protected as a fundamental right to petition the government.

 

242 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Atlantic Richfield Co. v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


S.F. No. 24335


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 3d 715; 644 P.2d 1257; 182 Cal. Rptr. 778; 47 Cal. Comp. Cases 500; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 182



May 20, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of respondent Department of Industrial Relations for a rehearing was denied June 23, 1982, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY:  31 Cal.3d 409. Modified and Reprinted.



DISPOSITION: The order joining the State of California, Department of Industrial Relations as a party is vacated, and the case is remanded to the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed above.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, employer and insurer, challenged an order of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board, which awarded respondent widow the statutory maximum of $ 50,000 as a death benefit, after the board found that she was entirely dependent on her husband's support under Cal. Lab. Code § 3502.


OVERVIEW: A compensation judge awarded employed respondent widow four times the amount of one-half of her husband's salary as a workers' compensation death benefit. Upon her petition for reconsideration, respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board determined that respondent widow was entitled to the maximum allowed under Cal. Lab. Code § 4702(d), finding that the proper method for computing her benefit was to treat the entire earnings of the husband as the measure of her actual support. After challenge by appellants, employer and insurer, the court found that, under Cal. Lab. Code § 3502, the proper method for determining partial dependency was to require the survivor to establish the amount annually devoted to her support from the earnings of the decedent. The court found that expenses which had been personal to decedent did not count toward the benefit. Thus, the court remanded to respondent board for determination of respondent widow's partial dependency benefit. The court determined that, under Cal. Lab. Code §4706.5(a), the Department of Industrial Relations was not entitled to the difference between the actual benefit amount and the statutory maximum.


OUTCOME: The court partly vacated the order and remanded the case to respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board. The court found that the appropriate method for determining partial dependency was to require that respondent widow establish the actual amount annually devoted to her support from her husband's earnings. The Department of Industrial Relations was not entitled to any difference between the benefit amount and the statutory maximum.

Peterson v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 31439


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 3d 147; 642 P.2d 1305; 181 Cal. Rptr. 784; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 168



April 8, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to grant leave to amend the complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff victim and his family sought a writ of mandate to command respondent Superior Court of Ventura County to grant their motion for leave to file a first amended complaint to allege punitive damages against the real party in interest, an intoxicated driver. In order to allow such recovery, the trial court had to apply a judicial holding retroactively to plaintiffs' accident, which it refused to do.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff victim and his family filed a civil suit against the real party in interest, the intoxicated driver who drove the car in which plaintiff victim was riding. The complaint sought damages for personal injuries. Plaintiffs alleged that the intoxicated driver acted in conscious disregard of plaintiffs' safety within the meaning of malice set forth in Cal. Civ. Code § 3294. The automobile accident occurred on April 24, 1979. Plaintiffs' complaint was filed on April 11, 1980. On August 21, 1979, between the two cited dates, the California Supreme Court held that punitive damages were recoverable in a personal injury action against an intoxicated driver who caused personal injury. In July 1980, plaintiffs sought to amend their complaint to request punitive damages. The trial court denied the motion. Thereafter, plaintiffs petitioned for a writ of mandamus and the court granted the writ and held that the ruling allowing punitive damages in a personal injury action against an intoxicated driver was properly applied retroactively. The court further held that such a retroactive application was not unfair or against public policy.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiff victim and his family a peremptory writ of mandate and directed the trial court to grant leave to amend plaintiffs' complaint because plaintiffs were allowed to recover punitive damages against the real party in interest, the intoxicated driver. The court held that a judicial precedent was properly applied retroactively to allow such recovery.

Brosnahan v. Eu


S.F. No. 24393.


Supreme Court of California


31 Cal. 3d 1; 641 P.2d 200; 181 Cal. Rptr. 100; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 156



March 11, 1982.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county electors filed a writ of mandate and prohibition with the court (California) to prevent respondent secretary of state from certifying an initiative for the ballot relating to "criminal justice" and "The Victims' Bill of rights."


OVERVIEW: Petitioner county electors sought to prevent respondent secretary of state from instituting measures preparatory to placing on the ballot an initiative measure relating to criminal justice and entitled "The Victims' Bill of Rights." Petitioners assert that respondent failed to comply with certain statutory provisions regarding the number of valid signatures required to qualify the measure for the ballot. Respondent determined that the petitions contained 108.76 percent of the signatures required for a place on the ballot. While this litigation was pending, the California legislature passed, and the governor signed, an urgency measure providing that if the secretary of state received from the several county clerks certificates, based on a random sampling technique, establishing that the number of valid signatures affixed to an initiative petition is more than 105 percent of the number of qualified voters needed, the petition shall be deemed to have qualified for the ballot. The court held that the initiative measure should have been placed on the ballot because there were enough valid signatures.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner county electors' peremptory writs of mandate and prohibition because the initiative had the number of valid signatures required qualifying it for the ballot.

People v. Haskett


Crim. No. 21136


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 841; 640 P.2d 776; 180 Cal. Rptr. 640; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 152



February 18, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 614862, James M. Ideman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to penalty, and affirmed in all other respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of two counts of first degree murder and one count of second degree attempted murder. The jury sentenced defendant to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree murder and one count of second degree attempted murder and sentenced to death. The court held that the evidence of both planning and motive were sufficient to support the conclusion that the killings were deliberately planned in a preconceived design. The court held that the lower court's refusal to further delay proceedings was within its discretion because defendant's second request for substitution of counsel at the "eleventh hour" was an attempt to cause additional delay. The court held that defendant received effective assistance of counsel. The court held that the search of defendant's home was legitimately based on the consent of his wife, who possessed independent and coequal authority to consent to a search of commonly occupied areas, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 5105. The court held that photographs of the victim's were properly admitted because their probative value was not outweighed by their prejudicial effect. The court held that the lower court erred in giving jury instructions at the penalty trial. The court reversed the judgment of the lower court as to penalty and affirmed as to all other respects.


OUTCOME: The court reversed judgment of the lower court as to penalty and affirmed as to all other respects. The court held that defendant's wife gave a legitimate consent to search defendant's house and that the victim's photos had probative value that was not outweighed by prejudicial effect. The court reversed the death penalty and remanded for retrial because the trial court erred in giving jury instructions at the penalty phase.

Kilgore v. Younger


L.A. No. 31266


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 770; 640 P.2d 793; 180 Cal. Rptr. 657; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 149; 8 Media L. Rep. 1886



February 18, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-250781, Jess Whitehill, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff listed in report as being among 92 persons suspected of organized criminal activity appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed his action against defendant attorney general and defendants newspaper reporters and publishers to recover damages for alleged defamation, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and invasion of privacy.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff listed in report as being among 92 persons suspected of organized criminal activity appealed the trial court's dismissal of his action against defendant attorney general and defendant newspaper reporters and publishers for defamation, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and invasion of privacy. The court held that the newspaper publications were privileged under Cal. Civ. Code § 47(5) with regard to defamation and intentional infliction of emotional distress because they were fair and true reports of the proceedings of a public meeting, and that there was no liability for invasion of privacy for giving further publicity to information that was already made public. The court held that defendant attorney general was entitled to the absolute privilege of Cal. Civ. Code § 47(1) for publications made in the discharge of an official duty. The court found that there was no reasonable possibility that plaintiff could have amended his complaint to state a cause of action against defendant attorney general predicated upon theory he disseminated information made confidential by sections of the penal code. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing plaintiff's action against defendant attorney general and defendants newspaper reporters and publishers for defamation, intentional infliction of emotional distress, and invasion of privacy because publications by defendants were privileged.

 

247 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wright


Crim. No. 21692


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 705; 639 P.2d 267; 180 Cal. Rptr. 196; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 145



February 4, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 19616, William A. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Court of Appeal (California) that upheld his sentences for burglary as calculated in accordance with Cal. R. Ct. 421, 423. Defendant charged that the rules, which provided criteria governing aggravation and mitigation for sentencing purposes, were an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority to the judicial council.


OVERVIEW: Holding that Cal. R. Ct. 421, 423, were effected pursuant to a valid legislative delegation of authority consistent with Cal. Const. art. VI, § 6, the court affirmed the judgment. The court explained that the direct constitutional authorization to adopt rules relating to court administration, practice, and procedure did not limit the legislature's ability to grant the judicial council additional power to adopt other rules, including rules governing substantive matters like sentencing. The court further explained that in general, an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power occurred when the legislature conferred upon an administrative agency unrestricted authority to make fundamental policy decisions. This was not the case here, as the legislature had made the fundamental decision to change from a scheme of indeterminate sentencing to one with fixed terms. It only delegated to the judicial council, via Cal. Penal Code § 1170.3(a), the task of implementing this decision by selecting the criteria for mitigation and aggravation of sentences.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences. The rules by which defendant's sentencing terms were selected were enacted pursuant to a valid, constitutional delegation of authority from the legislature to the judicial council.

 

248 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Assembly v. Deukmejian


S.F. Nos. 24348, 24349, 24354, 24356


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 638; 639 P.2d 939; 180 Cal. Rptr. 297; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 143



January 28, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  The alternative writs of mandate heretofore issued are discharged, and the petitions for writs of mandate are denied. Each party shall bear its own costs in the proceedings herein.

The judgment is final forthwith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought writs of mandate compelling state and local officials to omit referenda on legislation passed by the California legislature and signed by the Governor, that established new voting districts, claiming that the petitions for such referenda were invalid.


OVERVIEW: Based on census data, the California Legislature passed legislation that established new voting districts. Various parties, referred to as real parties, then gathered signatures for referendum petitions so that the reappointment legislation could be voted on by the electorate. Petitioners brought this action challenging the referendum petitions, claiming that they were invalid for a variety of reasons. The most serious of the asserted flaws was the failure of the petitions to require a signer to affix his or her residence address, as mandated by Cal. Elec. Code § 3516(c). Real parties asserted that they substantially complied with the applicable requirements regarding address. However, the court found that the petitions were non-compliant. At that point, the court was left with having to decide what districting plan would govern for purposes of the upcoming elections. After weighing both federal and state constitutional interests, the court decided that use of the recently enacted reapportionment statutes minimized the potential disruption of the electoral process and provided a better alternative than use of the old, unconstitutional plans.


OUTCOME: The court agreed with petitioners that referenda petitions were non-compliant with law and therefore determined that the recently enacted redistricting plan should be utilized in the upcoming elections. Because there was no reason to believe that the parties to these proceedings would not accede to the holdings of the court, the court determined that no purpose would be served by issuing writs of mandate.

 

249 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ramos


Crim. No. 21352


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 553; 639 P.2d 908; 180 Cal. Rptr. 266; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 141



January 25, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied February 24, 1982, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-42382, Richard J. Beacom, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it relates to penalty. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant obtained automatic review of his conviction and sentence from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) which convicted appellant of robbery and murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with robbery, murder, and attempted murder in connection with a robbery and shooting incident at a fast food establishment. The jury found that the charged special circumstance was true, i.e., that the murder was committed while appellant was engaged in the commission of a robbery. Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i). As a result, the jury returned a verdict of death and appeal to this court was automatic. This was the court's first opportunity to consider a judgment of death imposed pursuant to the 1978 death penalty initiative popularly known as the Briggs Initiative which required the penalty phase jury to be instructed regarding the Governor's power to commute a sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole. The court found that the Briggs Initiative constituted a violation of defendant's due process rights by encouraging the jury to consider an irrelevant and confusing factor in their deliberations and biasing the jury in favor of the death penalty. Accordingly, the judgment was reversed insofar as it related to the penalty.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed insofar as it related to the death penalty because the court concluded that the Briggs Initiative jury instruction regarding the Governor's power to commute a sentence was an unconstitutional violation of appellant's due process rights.

 

250 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


San Jose Mercury-News v. Municipal Court


S.F. No. 24253


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 498; 638 P.2d 655; 179 Cal. Rptr. 772; 1982 Cal. LEXIS 140; 7 Media L. Rep. 2522



January 14, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  The peremptory writ of mandate is denied. The alternative writ, having served its purpose, is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner newspaper sought mandate to compel respondent judge of the Sunnyvale-Cupertino Municipal Court (California), who presided over a preliminary hearing in a felony complaint, to vacate his order closing the hearing under Cal. Penal Code § 868 and to issue a new order opening the hearing to media and public. Petitioner contended that § 868 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner newspaper sought a writ of mandate vacating respondent municipal court's order closing a preliminary hearing pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 868. The court denied the writ, finding that § 868 was constitutional. The court held that the right of access did not arise under the federal constitution because there was no consensus of a federal constitutional right to attend preliminary hearings. The court held that § 868 did not violate the state constitution because § 868 was already in effect when the public trial guarantee was included in the constitution and the delegates to the convention apparently saw no inconsistency between it and the constitutional amendment. The court also concluded that Cal. Const. art. I, § 15 provided no unqualified right in the public to attend a preliminary hearing. Finally, the court noted that courts traditionally deferred where possible to legislation seeking to implement crucial constitutional interests. The legislature was not powerless to accommodate competing free speech and fair trial interests and could give fair trial precedence where danger of prejudice was strong but proof of its existence appeared difficult.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner newspaper's request for a peremptory writ of mandate because the statute requiring closure of hearings at defendant's request, as applied to a preliminary hearing, was constitutional under both the federal and state constitutions.

People v. Williams


Crim. No. 21783


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 470; 637 P.2d 1029; 179 Cal. Rptr. 443; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 204



December 31, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of both parties for a rehearing were denied February 3, 1982. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 019612, Ernest L. Kelly, Judge.



DISPOSITION: This case is remanded to the trial court for exercise of the court's discretion to determine whether or not there is a basis for dismissing the finding of two special circumstances. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of murder with special circumstance allegations that the murder was committed during the commission of a robbery and burglary, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 190.2(a)(17)(i), and 190.2(a)(17)(ii), two counts of burglary, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 459, and three counts of robbery, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 211.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole after being convicted of murder with special circumstance allegations, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 190.2(a)(17)(i), and 190.2(a)(17)(ii), that the murder was committed during the commission of a robbery and burglary. Defendant was also convicted of burglary and robbery. Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385, the trial court could have, on its own motion, dismissed the action in furtherance of justice. The court determined that § 1385 permitted dismissals in the interest of justice in any situation where the legislature had not clearly evidenced a contrary intent. The court also held that § 1385 was applicable to a special circumstance finding and provided the authority for the trial court to strike a special circumstance finding so that defendant could be eligible for parole. The court affirmed the judgement and remanded to the trial court for a determination of whether there was a basis for dismissing the finding of special circumstances to allow defendant's parole eligibility.


OUTCOME: The court remanded to the trial court for exercise of the trial court's discretion to determine whether there was a basis for dismissing the finding of special circumstances to allow defendant's parole eligibility and affirmed the judgment in all other aspects. The court held that the trial court could dismiss the special circumstance finding in furtherance of justice because the legislature had not clearly evidenced a contrary intent.

 

252 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mirmirani


Crim. No. 21945


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 375; 636 P.2d 1130; 178 Cal. Rptr. 792; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 197



December 7, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied January 14, 1982. Kaus, J., did not participate therein. Tobriner, J., + participated therein. Richardson, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


+ Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 142563, Armand Arabian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent's Cal. Penal Code § 995 motion, based on the fact there was no evidence to support a finding that respondent made threats to achieve social or political goals. Respondent cross-appealed, challenging the constitutionality of Cal. Penal Code §§ 422 and 422.5.


OVERVIEW: The court was asked to determine whether Cal. Penal Code §§ 422 and 422.5, which made it a felony to threaten to commit certain crimes in order to achieve social or political goals, were unconstitutionally vague. The trial court had granted respondent a dismissal, under Cal. Penal Code § 995, based on lack of sufficient evidence. The state appealed, and respondent cross-appealed and contended that the statutes in question were unconstitutionally vague. The court held that the limitation the legislature attempted to place on the reach of the statute, by confining it to situations in which social or political goals were present, was fundamental to the crime it attempted to punish. The court could not redefine that crime to include conduct specifically excluded by the legislature in an attempt to save the statute from constitutional infirmity. Thus, the court held that the phrase social or political goals was unconstitutionally vague. Since that portion of the statute section was vital to the statute, the court had no other choice than to find the statute unconstitutional. Hence, the judgment of dismissal was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the dismissal of the charges against respondent for threatening to commit certain crimes in order to achieve social or political goals, finding that the phrase "social or political goals" was unconstitutionally vague, making the entire statute unconstitutional.

Hustedt v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


L.A. No. 31384


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 329; 636 P.2d 1139; 178 Cal. Rptr. 801; 46 Cal. Comp. Cases 1284; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 194



December 7, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining the Board from proceeding further in the pending disciplinary action against petitioner. The Board is directed to dismiss Workers' Compensation Appeals Board proceeding Misc. No. 115. In all other respects, the alternative writ is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney applied for a writ of prohibition to bar respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California) from proceeding with a contempt action or a disciplinary proceeding arising out of an allegation that petitioner filed a motion that contained contemptuous statements and was filed for the purpose of delaying proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney represented a client for workers' compensation claim. Petitioner was unable to make it to a hearing and filed a motion to disqualify a judge after petitioner was advised contempt proceedings would be initiated for failure to appear. The judge brought a complaint to respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board, which in turn brought contempt and disciplinary proceedings against petitioner. Petitioner filed for writ of prohibition to bar respondent from pursuing any such sanctions. On review, the court examined the constitution and applicable code. The court found that respondent did not have jurisdiction because the discipline of attorneys was a judicial function. The court found that the separation of powers doctrine precluded the Legislature from granting respondent the power to discipline petitioner by temporarily or permanently prohibiting him or her from practicing before respondent. Also, respondent did not have authority to proceed with the contempt action it instituted against petitioner. Therefore, the writ of prohibition was issued, restraining respondent from proceeding further in the pending disciplinary action against petitioner.


OUTCOME: The writ of prohibition was issued, restraining respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board from proceeding further in the pending disciplinary action against petitioner attorney because the separation of powers doctrine precluded respondent from disciplining petitioner by temporarily or permanently prohibiting him from practicing before respondent. Also respondent did not have authority to proceed with the contempt action.

 

254 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Employment Development Dept. v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 24216


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 256; 636 P.2d 575; 178 Cal. Rptr. 612; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 189



November 27, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' application for a rehearing was denied January 7, 1982, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, public agencies and officials, sought mandate or prohibition to compel respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) to vacate its order certifying plaintiff worker's suit as a class action, alleging primarily that respondent lacked authority to approve class certification after the principal legal issue was decided on the merits.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff worker sued defendants, public agencies and officials, challenging the validity of Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1264, which denied unemployment benefits to employees who did not provide sole support to family or left employment for domestic duties, claiming sex discrimination. When the trial court certified a class action, defendants sought mandate or prohibition to compel the trial court to vacate order, claiming lack of authority to approve class certification after the principal legal issue was decided on the merits. The court denied writs. Defendants could not reasonably assert that the timing of the decision on the merits, which they controlled, precluded class certification. Nor was the class as certified broader than the complaint permitted. The trial court properly concluded that it was not foreclosed from certifying the action as a class action. When defendants failed to object to or acquiesced in a determination of the merits before class certification or notification, they waived whatever right to a pre-merit determination of the class action issues they might have had. The court concluded that the trial court ruling that defendants waived the issue was proper.


OUTCOME: The court denied writs of mandate or prohibition because defendants, public agencies and officials, could not reasonably assert that the timing of the decision on the merits, which they controlled, precluded class certification, nor was the class as certified broader than the complaint permitted.

 

255 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Austin


Crim. No. 21843


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 155; 636 P.2d 1; 178 Cal. Rptr. 312; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 183



November 9, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A 559079, A 557749 VP, Mary G. Rogan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed and the causes remanded for resentencing in accordance with the requirements of sections 1170 and 1170.1 and the sentencing rules of the Judicial Council. We affirm the trial court's denial of conduct credits for the period defendant was in custody prior to sentencing, and further deny the petition for writ of habeas corpus seeking similar credits for the period of confinement in YA.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sentenced him to two consecutive three year terms in the California Youth Authority. The first sentence was imposed for his conviction of burglary, and the second sentence was imposed for the violation of probation terms that were assessed for a separate offense.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of burglary and the trial court imposed two consecutive three year sentences in the California Youth Authority. The first sentence was imposed for the burglary charge, and the second sentence was imposed because the burglary conviction violated the terms of probation that he had been assessed for a separate offense. Appellant sought review of the imposed sentence and alleged that the trial court erred when it imposed the maximum sentence for each offense and when it refused to give him pre-sentence conduct credit. On review, the court reversed the sentence and affirmed the denial of conduct credit. The court held that the trial court erred at the sentencing trial, because it failed to state its reasons for imposing the maximum sentence on the trial record, and thereby violated Cal. R. Ct. 439, 453(a) and Cal. Penal Code § 1170. However, the trial court did not err when it refused to calculate conduct credits for the time that appellant spent in pre-sentence incarceration as he was not entitled to have his sentence to the youth authority reduced with the application of such credit. The matter was remanded to the trial court for re-sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's commitment order sentencing appellant to two consecutive three year terms in the California Youth Authority, because the trial court violated sentencing procedure in its imposition of the sentences. The court affirmed the lower court's refusal to give appellant conduct credit for presentence incarceration because such credit was not applicable to a youth authority sentence.

In re Deborah C.


Crim. No. 21768


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 125; 635 P.2d 446; 177 Cal. Rptr. 852; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 181



November 5, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. J-818294, Robert R. Devich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to credit Deborah with 32 days of detention credit for time spent in juvenile hall prior to her dispositional hearing. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained two wardship petitions that each contained one count of petty theft, in violation of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §602; Cal. Pen. Code, §§484(a), 488, and 490.5(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was twice arrested by a plainclothes private detective after she attempted to leave a store with unpurchased merchandise. One arrest was made after the detective saw defendant stuff items into her bag in a fitting room. Defendant made incriminating statements after both her arrests; she was never given Miranda warnings by the store detective. Defendant was subsequently charged in two wardship petitions with petty theft, in violation of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §602, and Cal. Pen. Code, §§484(a), 488, and 490.5(a). The juvenile court sustained both petitions over evidentiary objections by defendant. Defendant appealed. The court affirmed and held that a private detective was not required to follow Miranda procedures before eliciting defendant's incriminating statements and that no illegal search had occurred because the detective's observations of defendant did not infringe defendant's reasonable expectation of privacy while in a fitting room. The court modified defendant's sentence to credit defendant with 32 days as credit for time spent in juvenile hall prior to her dispositional hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the wardship petitions because defendant juvenile's constitutional rights were not violated when she was questioned by the plainclothes detective without Miranda warnings, and the detective's view of defendant in the fitting room was not a search that required exclusion of evidence. The court modified the judgment to credit defendant with 32 for time spent in juvenile hall prior to her dispositional hearing.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Through a petition for writ of mandamus, appellant drunk driver challenged the administrative suspension of his driver's license and mandamus was denied by the Superior Court No. SWC 46311of Los Angeles County (California).


OVERVIEW: Appellant drunk driver was stopped for driving while intoxicated. He refused to take any one of three chemical tests that would establish his level of intoxication. His driver's license was suspended for six months, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13353. Appellant filed a petition for writ of mandamus with the trial court after receiving confirmation of the administrative suspension of his license. Appellant challenged Cal. Veh. The court affirmed appellant's driver's license suspension, holding that the constitutionality of legislative measures regulating the granting and suspension of driver's licenses for the public safety was to be judged against the restrained substantive due process standard traditionally applied to ordinary police power measures.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the administrative suspension of appellant's drunk driver driver's license, holding that the constitutionality of legislative measures regulating the granting and suspension of driver's licenses for the public safety was to be judged against the restrained substantive due process standard traditionally applied to ordinary police power measures.

In re Wilson


Crim. No. 21750


Supreme Court of California


30 Cal. 3d 21; 634 P.2d 363; 177 Cal. Rptr. 336; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 175



October 8, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied November 13, 1981. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Emotion and sympathy, however well intentioned, cannot properly play a role in this court's resolution of this legal issue. Since the law allows no other recourse, the order to show cause is discharged and the petition for a writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Native American sought habeas corpus relief after he was convicted in the trial court (California) of unlawful possession of deer taken during closed season. Petitioner asserted that a local Indian aboriginal right to hunt had not been extinguished and that the state could regulate Indians' hunting activities within their aboriginal territory only if the regulation met applicable federal standards.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner Native American was caught with deer carcasses in his possession while he was on lands within the aboriginal territory of a branch of his tribe. Petitioner was cited and convicted of unlawful possession of deer taken during closed season. Petitioner thereafter sought habeas corpus relief and asserted that a local Indian aboriginal right to hunt had not been extinguished and that the state could regulate Indians' hunting activities within their aboriginal territory only if the regulation met applicable federal standards. On review, the court examined the plight of the Native Americans and the compensation legislated for the same by Congress. The court found that it was established that the tribe's aboriginal "right to occupy" their native territory had been extinguished. The court found that this extinguishment of the tribe's title to, or right to occupy, their aboriginal territory operated to extinguish the tribe's, and in effect petitioner's, aboriginal hunting rights. Therefore, the petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied. The court did suggest that exceptions be made at other levels of government, however.


OUTCOME: Petitioner Native American's writ of habeas corpus was denied because the court found that it was established that petitioner tribe's aboriginal "right to occupy" their native territory had been extinguished. This extinguishment of the tribe's title to, or right to occupy, their aboriginal territory operated to extinguish petitioner's aboriginal hunting rights.

People v. Davis


Crim. No. 21721


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 814; 633 P.2d 186; 176 Cal. Rptr. 521; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 173



August 31, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 019446, John A. Arguelles, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it relates to penalty, and we affirm the judgment in all other respects. We order that defendant's sentence be reduced to life imprisonment, the only alternative sentence authorized by the applicable statute. (Former § 190.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions for rape and first degree murder by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) and his sentence to life in prison without the possibility of parole under Cal. Penal Code § 190.4(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of rape and first degree murder primarily on the basis of a confession. Defendant stated that he followed the victim into a secluded area and choked the victim until she was unconscious. Defendant then raped the victim and, when the victim started to show signs of regaining consciousness, strangled the victim because defendant was angry and feared she would tell others what defendant had done. Defendant was sentenced to life in prison without the possibility of parole. Defendant challenged the admission of the confession, contending that the confession was the fruit of an illegal seizure, the fruit of an arrest made without probable cause, and involuntary. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the seizure was not illegal because defendant was not actually in custody until later in the investigation and that there was probable cause for the arrest. The court reversed the life sentence without parole, however, and ordered that defendant's sentence be reduced to life imprisonment pursuant to the minor's exception to Cal. Penal Code § 190.4(a).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction for murder in the first degree and rape because defendant voluntarily made the confession to the police and the police had probable cause to arrest defendant based upon the evidence. The court reversed the life sentence without possibility of parole and reduced the sentence to a life sentence.

Martori Bros. Distributors v. Agricultural Labor Relations Bd.


L.A. No. 31310


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 721; 631 P.2d 60; 175 Cal. Rptr. 626; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 162; 95 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,371



July 27, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Real Party in Interest United Farm Workers of America for a Rehearing was Denied August 26, 1981. Bird, C. J., did not Participate therein.



DISPOSITION: Let a decree issue setting aside the board's order and remanding the cause for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer sought review of an order of respondent, the California Agricultural Labor Relations Board, which concluded that petitioner had committed an unfair labor practice in terminating the employment of a former employee, arguing that the termination of the employee was justified.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employer terminated an employee, who filed a complaint with respondent agricultural labor relations board. Respondent concluded that petitioner committed an unfair practice in terminating the employee in retaliation for union activities, and petitioner sought judicial review, arguing that the employee's termination was justified. In setting aside respondent's order, the reviewing court concluded that Cal. Lab. Code § 1148 required respondent to follow precedent of the National Labor Relations Board; as such, respondent was required to apply the "but for" standard in assessing the dual motive for the discharge of agricultural workers. Under that standard, the question to be determined was whether the discharge would not have occurred but for the protected activity. Although some evidence existed to support a conclusion that the discharge was improper, there was ample evidence to support petitioner's action, including evidence that the employee was insubordinate, threatened the employer and his family members, and engaged in outrageous and disruptive conduct. Because respondent applied an incorrect legal standard, the matter was remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: Respondent agricultural labor relations board's order was set aside and the matter was remanded for reconsideration. The proper standard of review required respondent to determine whether the discharge would not have occurred but for the employee's union activities. Where substantial evidence of the employee's outrageous, threatening, and disruptive conduct was presented, the finding of an unfair labor practice needed to be reconsidered.

People v. Murtishaw


Crim. No. 20958


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 733; 631 P.2d 446; 175 Cal. Rptr. 738; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 165



July 27, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 26, 1981, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 19333, John D. Jelletich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it relates to penalty. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Kern County (California) of three counts of first degree murder, and one count of assault with intent to commit murder. The jury found the special circumstance of multiple murder and imposed the death penalty under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3. In the ensuing automatic appeal under Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b), defendant objected to the penalty phase testimony of his propensity to future violence.


OVERVIEW: In an automatic appeal under Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b) of defendant's three first degree murder convictions and sentence of death, defendant objected to the penalty phase testimony of his propensity for future violence and alleged other errors. The court affirmed the verdict of guilt but reversed the death sentence. The court ruled that penalty phase testimony predicting defendant's future violence was unreliable and irrelevant under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3 and required reversal. The court ruled that submission of the issue of felony murder and the failure to instruct on express malice for assault were not prejudicial because the evidence of guilty malice was overwhelming. The court also ruled that while the trial court had discretion to allow discovery of prosecution juror investigations and records, their value to defendant was speculative. On defendant's objection that the trial court failed to instruct that his unreasonable belief in his necessity to act in self-defense rebutted malice and reduced the homicides to manslaughters, the court ruled that defendant did not request such a charge and his trial antedated the decision requiring such an instruction sua sponte.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's three convictions of first degree murder but reversed his death sentence. The court ruled that the evidence so overwhelmingly supported the guilty verdict that defendant's points of error could not have prejudiced the result, but the court held that penalty phase evidence to predict his future propensity for violence was unreliable, irrelevant, and prejudicial and constituted reversible error.

The Pines v. Santa Monica


L.A. No. 31338


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 656; 630 P.2d 521; 175 Cal. Rptr. 336; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 157



July 16, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 161735, Alexander R. Early III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed the judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found for plaintiff condominium developers in their action that alleged defendant's condominium tax law was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff condominium developers challenged a tax law imposed by defendant city on condominiums. The trial court found that the tax law was invalid. The court reversed that decision. Plaintiffs argued that the tax law was preempted as it concerned a matter of statewide concern. The court reasoned that the taxation power was granted to charter cities by Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(a). Contrary to plaintiffs' contention, the Subdivision Map Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 66410 et. seq.(Map Act) did not limit defendant's power to tax for revenue purposes. There was no clear intention by the legislature to preempt local subdivision taxes by the Map Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision that defendant city's tax law was invalid because state law did not preempt the tax law, and defendant had the power to tax under the California Constitution.

 

264 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mandel v. Myers


S.F. No. 24217


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 531; 629 P.2d 935; 174 Cal. Rptr. 841; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 155



June 18, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Hg. den. July 22, 1981


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 427816, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, various state agencies and officials, challenged the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) which directed that defendant state controller pay plaintiff state employee's final judgment award of attorney fees that resulted from an action brought to enjoin defendants from giving state employees paid time off for Good Friday from the funds of the Department of Health Services.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff state employee was awarded attorney fees in an action to enjoin defendants, various state agencies and officers, from giving state employees paid time off for Good Friday. Because defendants failed to pay the judgment, plaintiff filed a claim with the State Board of Control, which then included the amount in a legislative bill. The appropriation was opposed and deleted from the bill. Plaintiff filed a motion to enforce the award. The trial court ordered defendant state controller to pay the award from the funds of the Department of Health Services. Defendants challenged it as a violation of the separation of powers. The court affirmed, holding that the trial court's order sustained constitutional principles by simply directing the proper state official to pay a sum out of funds that the California Legislature had already appropriated to the Department of Health Services. The court noted that the separation of powers doctrine precluded the California Legislature from according differential treatment in the payment of specific attorney fee awards on the basis of its own case-by-case readjudication of the very issues that the trial court already properly adjudicated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order directing defendant state controller to pay the attorney fees judgment to plaintiff state employee from the funds of the Department of Health Services because it did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers. The court noted that the trial court simply ordered defendant to do its job by paying the award from proceeds that the California Legislature had already appropriated to the department.

People v. Samuel


Crim. No. 21684


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 489; 629 P.2d 485; 174 Cal. Rptr. 684; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 150



June 15, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C 39229, Alicemarie H. Stotler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole after a jury decided defendant was competent to stand trial pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1368 hearing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to life without possibility of parole. On appeal, defendant argued that the evidence presented at his Cal. Penal Code § 1368 hearing was insufficient to support the jury verdict of competence to stand trial. The court set aside defendant's conviction and held that the record was devoid of substantial evidence that defendant was competent to stand trial. The court held that it subjected the jury's verdict to a closer than normal scrutiny because the right to a jury in a § 1368 hearing was statutory and no constitutional rights were involved. The court also stated that the demeanor of witnesses were an insignificant factor in resolving the question of competence and that in the court's reversal of a finding of competence, it was not affecting the question of guilt or penalty to be imposed. The court held that the jury could not reasonably reject the persuasive evidence proving defendant's mental incompetence to stand trial.


OUTCOME: The court set aside defendant's conviction of first-degree murder and held that there was substantial evidence presented at defendant's competency hearing that established that defendant was incompetent to stand trial. The court held that expert testimony showed that defendant's mental infirmities affected his ability to understand the proceedings and to assist his attorney.

 

266 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Cory v. Shierloh


S.F. No. 24211


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 430; 629 P.2d 8; 174 Cal. Rptr. 500; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 146



June 11, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied July 8, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 40691, Warren K. Taylor, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), which dismissed plaintiff's complaint in favor of defendant social host on grounds that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25602 and Cal. Civ. Code § 1714 immunized defendant from civil liability for injuries attributable to intoxication, in plaintiff's action alleging various forms of tortious conduct on the part of defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff injured was a minor who became intoxicated at defendant social host's party. Plaintiff sued defendant alleging the negligent operation and control of leased premises, that the use and occupancy of the premises constituted a nuisance, that defendant's unlicensed and unlawful conduct in furnishing alcohol was the proximate cause of plaintiff's intoxication and injuries, and that defendant negligently sold or furnished alcohol to plaintiff knowing that he was intoxicated and would be driving a car. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer on grounds that 1978 amendments to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25602 and Cal. Civ. Code § 1714 barred plaintiff's action. The court affirmed and held that plaintiff's causes of action each required that defendant's acts be the proximate cause of the injuries sustained by plaintiff. The court held that the negligent management of premises, serving of alcohol without a license or furnishing alcohol to intoxicated minor were not proximate causes of plaintiff's injuries, and that by legislative mandate, the consumption of alcohol was the proximate cause of injuries resulting from intoxication.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff injured's complaint for failure to state a cause of action against defendant social host. The court held that statutory amendments were applicable in this case and that plaintiff's causes of action failed to show that defendant's actions were the proximate cause of plaintiff's injuries.

 

267 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Committee to Defend Reproductive Rights v. Myers


S.F. Nos. 24069, 24053, 24192


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 252; 625 P.2d 779; 172 Cal. Rptr. 866; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 137; 20 A.L.R.4th 1118



March 20, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 29, 1981. Richardson, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 741710, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge [case on appeal].



DISPOSITION: In S.F. 24069, the judgment is reversed. In S.F. 24053, the alternative writ, having served its purpose, is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied. In S.F. 24192, a peremptory writ of mandate shall issue, directing respondents to refrain from enforcing the unconstitutional restrictions in the Budget Act of 1980 challenged herein. Plaintiffs shall recover their costs in these proceedings, including reasonable attorneys fees pursuant to the provisions of section 1021.5 of the Code of Civil Procedure. Because these three proceedings are inextricably interrelated, on remand of S.F. 24069 the San Francisco Superior Court shall fix an appropriate cost award, including attorneys fees, by reference to the costs and attorney services expended in all three proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff indigent women sought writ of mandate regarding a judgment from the Court of Appeal of California, which upheld respondent state officials' enforcement of the California Budget Act funding restrictions that limited Medi-Cal funding for abortions. The instant court issued an alternative writ for similar actions pending at the time of the appeal and stayed enforcement of the restrictions until resolution of the merits of plaintiffs' suit.


OVERVIEW: On review, plaintiff indigent women contended that the challenged California Budget Act funding restrictions constituted a selective and discrimination public funding scheme that violated plaintiffs' constitutional right to privacy. The court held that when examined in light of the special scrutiny required under Danskin-Bagley principles, the statutory scheme at issue was unconstitutional. The court found that the funding restrictions were antithetical to the purpose of the Medi-Cal program; that the benefits of the funding restrictions did not manifestly outweigh the impairment of the constitutional right to privacy; that the fiscal advantages of the restrictions were illusory; that the asserted state interest in protecting fetal life was subordinate to a woman's fundamental right of procreative choice; and that the Medi-Cal program, as qualified by the funding restrictions, did not aid poor women who chose to bear children in a manner least offensive to the rights of those who chose abortion. The court reversed, discharged the alternative writs, and issued peremptory mandate relief to preclude the enforcement of the unconstitutional restrictions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that upheld the validity of the subject Budget Act funding restrictions on the Medi-Cal program and issued a writ of mandate to prevent enforcement of the unconstitutional restrictions that operated to withhold selectively medical service benefits from plaintiffs solely because they sought to exercise their constitutional right of procreative choice in a manner not favored by respondent state officials.
