State of California v. Superior Court (Lyon)


S.F. No. 23981


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 210; 625 P.2d 239; 172 Cal. Rptr. 696; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 135; 11 ELR 20476



March 20, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition of Real Parties in Interest Raymond R. Lyon and Margaret L. Lyon for a Rehearing was Denied April 29, 1981 Richardson, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to vacate its order granting Lyon partial summary judgment, and to grant the People's motion for partial summary judgment insofar as consistent with the views expressed above.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Lake County (California), to vacate its order that no portion of certain land lying landward of the ordinary low water mark of a lake was sovereign property of petitioner, or subject to a common law public trust, but that the waters of the lake were impressed with a public servitude, and to grant petitioner's motion for partial summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The trial court determined that no portion of land owned by the real party in interest lying landward of the ordinary low water mark of a lake was sovereign property of petitioner state or subject to a common law public trust, but that the waters of the lake were impressed with a public servitude so that when the water rose above the low water mark, the public had the right to navigate between that line and the ordinary high water mark. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent trial court to vacate its order, and to grant a motion for partial summary judgment. The court found that petitioner succeeded to the ownership of the beds of nontidal navigable lakes and rivers upon its admission to the Union, to the high water mark. The court also found that under Cal. Civ. Code § 830, the real party in interest received title to the low water mark of the lake. The grant of lands between high and low water made by § 830 to riparian landholders was not free of a public trust. The real party in interest could utilize such lands in any manner not incompatible with the public's interest in the property. The court ordered that the writ of mandate issue.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that a writ of mandate issue directing respondent trial court to vacate its order granting the real party in interest partial summary judgment, and to grant the motion of petitioner state for partial summary judgment. The real party in interest was not deprived of the use of the lands between low and high water, and he was permitted to utilize them in any manner not incompatible with the public's interest in the property.

People ex rel. Deukmejian v. Brown


S.F. No. 24252


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 150; 624 P.2d 1206; 172 Cal. Rptr. 478; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 132



March 12, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied April 22, 1981. Richardson, J., was of the Opinion that the Application should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated, we enjoin the Attorney General from proceeding in this matter and order that the alternative writ be discharged and the petition be dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents governor and agencies filed a motion to dismiss the mandate request filed by petitioner attorney general with the Court of Appeal (California), attacking constitutionality of the State Employer-Employee Relations Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3512-3524. Respondents contended that petitioner's action was barred under ethical rules due to petitioner's participation in a prior case on the same matter.


OVERVIEW: The legislature adopted a State Employer-Employee Relations Act (SEERA), Cal. Gov't Code, §§ 3512- 3524. Petitioner attorney general initiated the instant proceeding in the Court of Appeal, seeking a writ of mandate against respondents governor and agencies, contending the legislation was unconstitutional. Petitioner, however, had participated in a case commenced by another entity to attack the legislation, in effect serving as counsel for the state personnel board. Thus, respondents filed a motion to dismiss the instant action, which the court granted. There was no constitutional, statutory, or ethical authority for petitioner's conduct in suing former clients over matters that arose during the period when, by law, he was counsel for those same entities. The court granted the motion of respondents to enjoin petitioner's adverse representation, and it dismissed the petition for writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court enjoined petitioner attorney general from proceeding in the case, as he had a conflict due to prior participation in a case on the same matter previously before respondent governor and agencies. Thus, the court dismissed the petitioner for mandate.

 

270 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Pac. Legal Found. v. Brown


S.F. No. 24168


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 168; 624 P.2d 1215; 172 Cal. Rptr. 487; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 133; 109 L.R.R.M. 2674



March 12, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied April 22, 1981. Richardson, J., was of the Opinion that the Application should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ previously issued is discharged and the petition for a peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner foundations sought writ of mandate regarding a judgment from the Court of Appeal of California, which upheld respondent government officials' enforcement of the State Employer-Employee Relations Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3512 et. seq. Petitioners had sought to strike down the Act and to compel respondents to conduct their operations without regard to the Act's provisions.


OVERVIEW: On review, petitioner foundations contended that the State Employer-Employee Relations Act (Act), Cal. Gov't Code § 3512 et. seq., was facially unconstitutional. The court disagreed, discharged the alternative writ, and denied petitioners' request for peremptory writ of mandate. The court found that the Act's collective bargaining process did not conflict with the general merit principle of civil service employment established by Cal. Const. art. VII. The court then found that the Act did not contravene Cal. Const. art. VII in reserving the ultimate salary setting decision with respect to civil service employees to respondent governor, noting that the State Personnel Board's constitutional authority to prescribe classifications did not encompass the power to set the classifications' salaries. The court concluded that the provisions of the Act which granted the Public Employment Relations Board initial jurisdiction to investigate and to devise remedies for "unfair practices" did not conflict with Cal. Const. art. VII, § 3(a), noting that the Act was not an unlawful delegation of legislative authority or an improper infringement on respondent's veto power.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the previously issued alternative writ, denied the petition for a peremptory writ of mandate, and upheld the determination that the challenged collective bargaining provisions of the State Employer-Employee Relations Act were not unconstitutional on their face as a usurpation of the "merit system" of employment for civil service employees or of the authority vested in the State Personnel Board.

Deas v. Knapp


S. F. No. 24224


Supreme Court of California


29 Cal. 3d 69; 623 P.2d 775; 171 Cal. Rptr. 823; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 125



February 19, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 415397, Lyle E. Cook, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judgment creditors and defendants appealed the decision of the Court of Appeal (California) which followed the trial court decision that limited plaintiffs' recovery to $ 20,000 under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 10474(a) and that declined to allow defendant to relitigate the issue of fraud.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff judgment creditors obtained a judgment of over $ 50,000 from defendant real estate broker for overcharges on trust deed loans. Plaintiff sought to apply the judgment to a state fund set up under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 10450.6, 10470 et seq. for payment of claims on unsatisfied judgment against licensees for fraud or conversion of trust funds. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision in part, holding that plaintiff's recovery including costs was limited to $ 20,000 under § 10474 even though defendant had more than one real estate license. The court further held that under § 10473.1 defendant was permitted to relitigate the merits of the fraud claim underlying the judgment and was not barred from doing so by principles of res judicata.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the appellate court judgment holding that judgment creditors were limited under applicable statute to total recovery of $ 20,000, and defendants were s permitted to relitigate merits of claim underlying the judgment notwithstanding res judicata principles.

DeRonde v. Regents of Univ. of Cal.


S.F. 24145


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 875; 625 P.2d 220; 172 Cal. Rptr. 677; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 119



February 11, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 11, 1981, and the Dissenting Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Newman, J., did not Participate therein. Rattigan, J., * Participated therein. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 32781, James G. Changaris, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed to the extent that it (1) declares the University's admissions program is discriminatory and a violation of equal protection of the laws, (2) enjoins the University from any consideration of race, color or ethnic origin in the admissions process at King Hall, and (3) awards costs of suit to DeRonde. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff applicant and defendant law school appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), which did not grant plaintiff's request to compel defendant to admit him. Defendant's admission procedures were found to be facially discriminatory and violated the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions. Defendant was enjoined from using any admission criteria based on race, color, or ethnic origin.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff applicant applied for enrollment at defendant law school but was unsuccessful. He sought mandamus to compel defendant to admit him on the ground that defendant's admission criteria was unconstitutional. One of the criteria used was ethnic minority status. The lower court held that although plaintiff was not entitled to relief sought because he would have been unsuccessful even had the challenged procedures not been used, defendant's procedures were facially discriminatory and violated the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions. The lower court enjoined defendant from using admission criteria based on race, color, or ethnic origin. The court reversed the judgment that declared defendant university's admissions program as discriminatory, violated equal protection, and which enjoined defendant from any consideration of race, color or ethnic origin in the admissions process. The court held that it was one of several factors used by defendant, was needed to prevent a disproportionate underrepresentation of minorities due to past societal discrimination, and was not quota based. The criteria was not violative of the federal or state constitutions.


OUTCOME: The lower court's judgment that found defendant law school's admissions program was discriminatory and violated equal protection was reversed. The court held that ethnic minority status was only one of the factors used, was necessary to prevent minority underrepresentation, and did not violate the federal or state equal protection clauses.

People v. Harris


Crim. No. 20888


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 935; 623 P.2d 240; 171 Cal. Rptr. 679; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 121



February 11, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 20, 1981. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 44135, Eli. H. Levenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: None of defendant's contentions having merit, the judgment imposing the death penalty is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which imposed the death penalty after defendant was convicted of kidnapping, robbery and first-degree murder of two teenagers.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered the car the victims were sitting in and forced them to drive to a secluded location. Defendant then shot and killed the victims without provocation. Defendant was captured after robbing a bank. The court affirmed the trial court's imposition of the death penalty. The trial court correctly denied defendant's change of venue motion. The size of the community where the trial was held was very large and dissipated the impact of pretrial publicity in the case. Defendant was not entitled to a mistrial based on the prosecutor's cross-examination of him that led defendant to exclaim that he was a prior felon. Defendant's general denial of the crime allowed the prosecutor wide latitude in cross-examination. Any error was cured when the jury was instructed not to consider the admission during the guilt phase. The delay in arraignment did not affect the voluntariness of defendant's fourth confession. There was no reason to believe the confession was less voluntary than the other three. Defendant was not entitled to a diminished capacity instruction based on voluntary intoxication where the only evidence consisted of defendant's own statements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the imposition of the death penalty after defendant's convictions for robbery, kidnapping, and first degree-murder. There was no evidence that pretrial publicity prevented defendant from receiving a fair trial. Defendant's fourth confession was as voluntary as the first three and was not affected by a delay in arraignment. Defendant's self-serving statements did not entitle him to a diminished capacity instruction.

People v. Chadd


Crim. No. 21024


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 739; 621 P.2d 837; 170 Cal. Rptr. 798; 1981 Cal. LEXIS 113



January 19, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Petitions of Both Parties for a Rehearing were Denied March 2, 1981. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the Opinion that the Respondent's Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 45638, Earl H. Maas, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: On counts 1, 6, 7, and 10 the judgment is reversed with directions to the trial court to strike from its records defendant's pleas of guilty and his admissions of charged enhancements and special circumstances allegations. On counts 2, 3, 4, and 5 the judgment is reversed. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) which imposed a sentence of death following defendant's plea of guilty to a charge of first degree murder. Defendant argued that the plea should not have been accepted without the consent of defendant's counsel as required by Cal. Penal Code § 1018.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by the trial court of first degree murder following his plea of guilty and was given a sentence of death. Defendant appealed the judgment arguing that the trial court committed prejudicial error in accepting the guilty plea without the consent of defendant's counsel as required by Cal. Penal Code § 1018. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment as to the majority of the counts against defendant holding that under § 1018 a capital defendant was not permitted to plead guilty against the advice of his attorney, and that because the attorney's consent was not given, it was error for the trial court to accept the plea with regard to the capital offenses. The court further held that the convictions on some of the counts for rape, sodomy, robbery, and burglary had to be reversed because the prosecution failed to file the required information charging the felonies within three years after their commission as required by the applicable statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court as to the majority of the counts against defendant holding that the court erred in accepting defendant's guilty plea on the capital offenses in violation of California statutes.

In re Michael B.


L.A. No. 31275


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 548; 620 P.2d 173; 169 Cal. Rptr. 723; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 235



December 18, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. J-101643, William L. Todd, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The dispositional order pertaining to the determination of the aggregate maximum permissible term of confinement is reversed for proceedings consistent with this opinion to permit a redetermination upon notice with the full opportunity for the minor to be heard. In all other respects, the order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the California Court of Appeals, Fourth Appellate District, which reversed the dispositional order of the trial court pertaining to the determination of the aggregate maximum permissible term of confinement on a petition under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 so as to permit a redetermination upon notice with a full opportunity for defendant to be heard, and which, in all other respects, affirmed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from a dispositional order of the juvenile court, which was entered on prosecution's petition under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, and which continued his status as a ward and committed him to the Youth Authority. He contended that prosecution had failed to provide notice of its intent to seek aggregation of the terms of confinement based on prior sustained § 602 petitions. Although the court of appeals recognized that a trial court could consider a juvenile's entire record in determining the maximum period of confinement under a new § 602 petition, the court of appeals held that a § 602 petition must contain notice of the intent to rely on previous sustained § 602 petitions in order for the trial court to aggregate the maximum period of confinement based on those prior offenses. The court of appeals also held that the imposition of consecutive terms for driving with a suspended license and driving while intoxicated did not constitute a double punishment in conflict with Cal. Penal Code § 654. Upon defendant's further appeal, the court adopted the opinion of the court of appeals.


OUTCOME: The court adopted the opinion of the court of appeals, which reversed the trial court's dispositional order pertaining to the determination of the aggregate maximum permissible term of confinement, because the prosecution failed to provide notice of its intent to seek aggregation and thereby failed to afford defendant the opportunity to rebut material contained in his prior record.

 

276 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Arnel Dev. Co. v. Costa Mesa


L.A. No. 31205


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 511; 620 P.2d 565; 169 Cal. Rptr. 904; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 233



December 18, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. 287707 and 287708, Walter W. Charamza, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The cause is retransferred to the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District, for disposition in light of this opinion. With respect to the proceeding before this court each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court, on its own initiative transferred the cause from the judgment of the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District (California), pursuant to Cal. R. Ct. 29(a), because the hearing involved appeared necessary to secure uniformity of decision and settle an important question of law.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff development company proposed a 50-acre development consisting of single family homes and apartments. Objecting to this proposal, an initiative was circulated to rezone the property. The voters approved the initiative and plaintiff brought suit. The trial court upheld the initiative but the appellate court reversed, holding the initiative to be an adjudicatory act. The court transferred the cause on its own motion to further examine the appellate court's finding that the rezoning of specific, relatively small parcels of privately owned property was adjudicatory in nature, and could not be enacted by initiative. The court rejected that notion and reasoned that to hold otherwise would unsettle established rules governing the enactment of land use restrictions, creating confusion which would take years to resolve. Because such a decision was unnecessary to protect either the rights of the landowners or the public interest in orderly community planning and development, the court concluded that the ordinance in question was a legislative act and the case was re-transferred to the appellate court for further disposition in light of the court's opinion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the holding of the appellate court, holding that the initiative in question was a legislative act as settled by precedent. The court reasoned to hold otherwise would present a situation that would unsettle established rules regarding land use restriction which would take years to resolve. Nothing warranted a deviation from the rule set by precedent.

Libertarian Party of California v. March Fong Eu


L.A. No. 31270


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 535; 620 P.2d 612; 170 Cal. Rptr. 25; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 234



December 18, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 297384, Robert H. Green, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, secretary of state, challenged an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted a peremptory writ of mandate commanding her and the registrar of voters to certify respondent candidates on the general election ballot and place their names on the ballot with the political party designation of Libertarian.


OVERVIEW: Respondent candidates qualified to have their names placed on an election ballot by submitting nomination papers signed by a sufficient number of voters under Cal. Elec. Code § 6831, which is part of the independent nomination procedure. Respondents demanded that their names appear on the ballot as Libertarian, rather than "Independent." Appellant, secretary of state, refused their request until ordered to do so by the trial court. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment requiring that respondents' name appear as Libertarian and held that Cal. Elec. Code § 10210, directed that candidates such as respondents, who appear on the ballot by virtue of an independent nomination, should have the word Independent printed next to their names instead of a political party designation. The court held that § 10210 did not constitute an unconstitutional impairment of respondent candidates or respondents fundamental rights to associate for political activity and to vote.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the peremptory writ commanding that respondent candidates' names appear on the ballot with the political party designation of Libertarian, holding that the applicable statute required that candidates such as respondents, who appear on the ballot through an independent nomination procedure, have a designation as Independent, and that the statute was constitutional.

Jarvis v. Cory


S.F. No. 24047


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 562; 620 P.2d 598; 170 Cal. Rptr. 11; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 236



December 18, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' Application for a Rehearing was Denied February 17, 1981. Bird, C. J., and Tobriner, J., did not Participate therein. Files, J., * and Brown (Gerald), J., * Participated therein. Richardson, J., and Files, J., * were of the Opinion that the Application Should be Granted.


* Assigned by the Acting Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writs of mandate are discharged and the petitions are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner complainants sought a hearing after decision in the Court of Appeal (California) in their consolidated cases seeking writs of mandate to declare S.B. 91, Reg. Sess. (Cal. 1979), a salary appropriation bill, unconstitutional and to order respondent state controller to refrain from expending any funds pursuant to the bill.


OVERVIEW: S.B. 91, Reg. Sess. (Cal. 1979), was the focus of petitioner complainants' petitions for writs of mandate in which they argued that the bill was invalid as a pre-budget act appropriation under Cal. Const. art. IV, § 12(c), and that it violated the extra compensation clause of Cal. Const. art. IV, § 17. The court granted a hearing on these arguments. In rejecting petitioner's first contention, the court relied on respondent state controller's defense of the bill's history and language and on the purpose underlying the constitutional provision. The court similarly relied on the argument that prior California holdings had construed the extra compensation prohibition to exclude adjustments comparable to this case. The court concluded that state employees had "uncertain" salaries during the 1978-1979 year and that the payments authorized by the bill were merely retroactive adjustments to their salaries for the period. The court discharged the alternative writs of mandate and denied the petitions holding that the bill was neither a pre-budget appropriation nor did it violate the California constitution extra compensation clause.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the alternative writs of mandate and denied the petitions. The court relied on the bill's history, language and the purpose behind the constitutional provision in rendering a decision regarding the pre-budget act appropriation argument. Likewise, the bill did not violate the extra compensation clause because payments were analogous to retroactive public employee payments.

Arneson v. Fox


L.A. No. 31259


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 440; 621 P.2d 817; 170 Cal. Rptr. 778; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 227



December 1, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 415995, Ross G. Tharp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant real estate broker challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied appellant's application for a writ of mandate to overturn the decision of respondent real estate commissioner. Respondent revoked appellant's real estate license as the result of a nolo contendere plea and conviction in federal court.


OVERVIEW: Appellant real estate broker pled nolo contendere and was convicted in federal court of conspiracy. Respondent real estate commissioner, following administrative hearings, revoked appellant's license as a real estate broker. Appellant sought review. The court noted that California followed the minority view that use of nolo contendere pleas as the basis of administrative action was improper. The court also noted, however, that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code 10177(b) specifically allowed respondent to take the action taken. Additionally, the court noted that the charges against appellant bore a reasonable and substantial relationship to the qualifications, functions and duties of a real estate broker. The court detected no violation of appellant's constitutional rights in the application of the statute or in the administrative process. Accordingly, the judgment of the trial court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed. Respondent real estate commissioner's action to revoke appellant real estate broker's license was authorized by state law, policy considerations did not bar respondent from relying on appellant's conviction, and the charges against appellant bore a reasonable and substantial relationship to the qualifications, functions or duties of a real estate broker.

In re Rogers


Crim. No. 21429


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 429; 619 P.2d 415; 169 Cal. Rptr. 222; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 226



November 24, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied December 24, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 54979, John J. Boskovich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Therefore, the trial court's judgment, disallowing such an improper use of the prior convictions, is hereby affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Community Release Board sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted respondent criminal defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus and ordered appellant to recompute the determinate term and delete three years.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Community Release Board added three extra years to petitioner criminal defendant's determinate term on the basis of two prior convictions that had been found unconstitutional by the sentencing court. Petitioner sought and obtained a writ of habeas corpus after unsuccessfully appealing appellant's decision administratively, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed, as appellant's use of invalid prior convictions to enhance petitioner's determinate term by three years and foreclose the possibility that he might be considered for an earlier release date was not permissible. The court found that as petitioner's two prior convictions were ruled constitutionally defective, that ruling was final and barred their use in the same criminal proceeding or a related administrative proceeding.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that granted respondent criminal defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus to and ordered appellant to recompute the determinate term and delete three years was affirmed, as appellant use of two prior convictions to enhance petitioner's determinate term was improper where the sentencing court ruled the priors constitutionally defective.

Ramirez v. The State Bar


S.F. No. 24117


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 402; 619 P.2d 399; 169 Cal. Rptr. 206; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 225



November 10, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing was Denied December 10, 1980. Bird, C. J., Tobriner, J., and Newman, J., were of the Opinion that the Application Should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: It is ordered that petitioner be suspended from the practice of law in this state for a period of one year commencing on the effective date of this order; that execution of the order of suspension be stayed and petitioner be placed on probation for such one-year period upon conditions (1) he be actually suspended from the practice of law in this state for the first 30 days of the probationary period, (2) he take and pass the Professional Responsibility Examination administered by the Committee of Bar Examiners within one year after the date upon which this order becomes effective (see Segretti v. State Bar (1976) 15 Cal.3d 878 [126 Cal.Rptr. 793, 544 P.2d 929]), and (3) he comply with the State Bar Act and Rules of Professional Conduct of the State Bar during the probationary period. This order shall become effective 30 days after the opinion herein is filed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney challenged the finding of respondent, the California State Bar, that he had violated his oath as an attorney due to certain defamatory statements he had made regarding a panel of state appellate judges.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney was brought before the court in a disciplinary action pursued by respondent state bar disciplinary board. Petitioner filed pleadings in the federal court system in which he alleged that a panel of three state appellate judges had conspired with his opponent when they vacated a civil verdict in favor of his client. The court held the statements were defamatory, as they were unsupported by facts or even a reasonable investigation. Since they were defamatory, they were not constitutionally protected. Although petitioner had apologized to the panel for his comments, he followed that up by submitting another pleading containing similar allegations. The court held that sanctions were therefore appropriate. The court ordered petitioner suspended from the practice of law for one year, but stayed that punishment and imposed a one-year probationary term, with a condition of that probation being a one-month actual suspension.


OUTCOME: Petitioner violated his oath due to his statements about three appellate judges, in that his claims that the judges were allied with his opponent were unsupported by facts or even an investigation. A one-year suspension was ordered, stayed during a one-year probation with a one-month actual suspension.

PEOPLE v. JACKSON


Crim. Nos. 20910, 21285


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 264; 618 P.2d 149; 168 Cal. Rptr. 603; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 221



October 23, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:
Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied December 10, 1980. Bird, C. J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 018145, D. Sterry Fagan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed, and the petition for habeas corpus relief is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of two counts of first-degree murder and two counts of burglary and imposing the death penalty. Appellant also filed a writ of habeas corpus based on his claim that he was ineffectively represented by his trial counsel.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of an order that convicted him of two counts of first-degree murder and two counts of burglary, and that imposed the death penalty on him. Appellant also filed a writ of habeas corpus based on his claim that his trial counsel ineffectively represented him. Appellant argued that the trial court erred in refusing to grant his request for additional counsel, in admitting his statements to police officers, in admitting photographic evidence of the victim's injuries, in failing to instruct the jury on voluntary manslaughter, in admitting the statements of an accomplice, in conducting a mistrial hearing without appellant present, in excluding impeachment evidence, and in imposing the unconstitutional punishment of the death penalty. Appellant also argued that he was ineffectively represented at the guilt and penalty phases of the trial and that the prosecutor engaged in misconduct. The court disagreed, finding that there were no errors and, even if there had been errors, there was not likely to have been a more favorable verdict if the asserted errors had not occurred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions and death penalty. The court denied appellant's request for habeas corpus relief.

Conservatorship of Hofferber


L.A. No. 31121


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 161; 616 P.2d 836; 167 Cal. Rptr. 854; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 224



September 15, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied October 15, 1980. Bird, C. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. MP-10369, David A. Ziskrout, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and order appealed from are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) establishing a civil conservatorship under the Lanterman-Petris-Short (LPS) Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code, §§5000 et seq., based on a finding that appellant was "gravely disabled" because he was an incompetent defendant charged with a violent felony.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) establishing a civil conservatorship under the Lanterman-Petris-Short (LPS) Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code, §§5000 et seq., based on a finding that appellant was "gravely disabled" because he was an incompetent defendant charged with a violent felony. Appellant argued that (1) a person charged with a violent felony and found mentally incompetent to stand trial may not be civilly committed for reasons and under procedures that differ from those applicable to other mentally disordered persons, (2) to establish grave disability his incompetency must be proved beyond a reasonable doubt, and (3) his conservatorship violates the proscription of retroactive or ex post facto laws. The court disagreed. However, the court found that a Lanterman-Petris-Short Act determination that appellant was "gravely disabled" because he is currently dangerous as the result of a mental disease, defect, or disorder must follow a hearing addressed to that specific issue. The court found that appellant did not have such a hearing and reversed the order of conservatorship.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order establishing a civil conservatorship.

 

284 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hovey v. Superior Court of Alameda County


S.F. No. 24093


Supreme Court of California


28 Cal. 3d 1; 616 P.2d 1301; 168 Cal. Rptr. 128; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 208



August 28, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at People v. Moore, 162 Cal. App. 3d 709, 208 Cal. Rptr. 771, 1984 Cal. App. LEXIS 2820 (Cal. App. 1st Dist., 1984)
Later proceeding at Hovey v. McCarthy, 479 U.S. 1104, 107 S. Ct. 1336, 94 L. Ed. 2d 187, 1987 U.S. LEXIS 918 (1987)



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the trial court to conduct the voir dire in accordance with the views set forth in part VII hereof. In all other respects, the requested relief is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, facing a possible death penalty, sought writ of mandamus challenging order of the Superior Court for Alameda County (California) overruling petitioner's pretrial motion to limit exclusions for cause of prospective jurors to be called to try his case, and challenging the in-court procedures used to identify prospective jurors whose views on capital punishment justified their exclusion from trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder, kidnapping, and enhancements which, if established, could subject defendant to penalty of death. Defendant filed a pretrial motion challenging the exclusion for cause of prospective jurors who would be disqualified by their unequivocal opposition to the death penalty, but who could be fair and impartial at the guilt phase, challenging the voir dire procedures for selecting a death-qualified jury. The motions were overruled, and defendant sought a writ of mandamus. The court held that defendant had failed to show empirically that the exclusion for cause of prospective jurors unequivocally opposed to the death penalty resulted in a jury which was less than neutral with respect to the guilt of the accused. But the court held that, by failing to sequester jurors during the voir dire of a death penalty jury, the voir dire process predisposed prospective jurors to believe in the guilt of the accused.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ directing the trial court to conduct voir dire in accordance with the court's opinion, failing to sequester jurors during voir dire phase of a death penalty jury predisposed jurors to believe in the guilt of the accused. The court denied other requested relief, since petitioner failed to show that excluding jurors unequivocally opposed to the death penalty produced a jury which was less than neutral as to guilt.

 

285 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Simplicity Pattern Co. v. State Board of Equalization


L.A. No. 31243


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 900; 615 P.2d 555; 167 Cal. Rptr. 366; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 206



August 18, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied September 17, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 84444, David N. Eagleson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a judgment from the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) in favor of defendant Board of Equalization denying plaintiff a refund of sales tax on the transfer of film negatives and master recordings used to make audiovisual materials that helped train nurses, paramedics, and other medical personnel.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff had sought a refund of sales tax on the transfer of film negatives and master recordings used to make audiovisual materials. The transfer was part of a sale by plaintiff of that segment of its business through an exchange of the assets for common stock of the transferee's parent. Defendant Board of Equalization denied the refund, and the lower court entered judgment for defendant board. On appeal, plaintiff contended the sale of negatives and recordings was not taxable because it was not a sale of tangible personal property, was a sale for resale, and should have escaped tax by reason of its resemblance to a statutory merger. The court disagreed and affirmed the lower court's judgment. The court concluded that the completed film negatives and master recordings were tangible personal property for sales tax purposes. Though valued in part for their intellectual content, they also were physically useful in the manufacturing process. Since the primary purpose of selling the film negatives and master recordings was to use them in manufacturing the final product rather than to incorporate it into that product, their sale did not escape tax as one for resale.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying plaintiff a refund of sales tax because the completed film negatives and master recordings were tangible personal property for sales tax purposes.

Perez v. City of San Bruno


S.F. No. 24101


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 875; 616 P.2d 1287; 168 Cal. Rptr. 114; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 205



August 14, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied September 11, 1980. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 204818, Alan W. Haverty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen challenged the order of the Superior Court, San Mateo County (California), which granted summary judgment to defendant city in plaintiff's claim for invasion of her constitutional rights through the discontinuance of her water service. Plaintiff was billed for water and garbage disposal on the same bill; she refused to pay the garbage disposal portion but paid the other portion. Defendant terminated her water service.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff citizen was billed for water and garbage disposal on the same bill. Plaintiff refused to pay for the garbage disposal portion of the bill but paid the other portion. Defendant city terminated her water service. Plaintiff sought damages for invasion of her constitutional rights through the discontinuance of her water service. The trial court granted defendant's motion for summary judgment. The court affirmed and held that the issue as to whether defendant was acting within its powers in imposing the charges for garbage collection even though plaintiff did not avail herself of the garbage collection services was barred by collateral estoppel. The court found that the legislative scheme, which allowed defendant to terminate water service for nonpayment of other services, bore a reasonable relationship to a proper legislative goal. The court held that termination of plaintiff's water service was not a violation of her constitutional rights. Despite defendant's failure to provide notice regarding plaintiff's rights, her failure to receive notice and hearing prior to the one-month termination of service did not deprive her of her right to fundamental fairness of procedure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that granted defendant city summary judgment in plaintiff citizen's claim for invasion of her constitutional rights through the discontinuance of her water service. The legislative scheme, which allowed defendant to terminate water service for nonpayment of other services billed on the same bill, was constitutional because it bore a reasonable relationship to a proper legislative goal.

 

287 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Citizens against Rent Control v. Berkeley


S.F. No. 24124


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 819; 614 P.2d 742; 167 Cal. Rptr. 84; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 201



August 7, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 493837-0, John P. Sparrow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City of Berkeley challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which enjoined enforcement of appellant's ordinance that limited political contributions concerning ballot measures. The issue was whether the ordinance unconstitutionally prevented effective political advocacy or interfered with associational rights, as guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2.


OVERVIEW: The superior court enjoined enforcement of Berkeley, Cal., Election Reform Act of 1974, Ord. No. 4700-N.S., which limited political contributions concerning ballot measures, and appellant City of Berkeley challenged the injunction. On appeal, the court applied the strict scrutiny test in balancing the purposes of the ordinance against any intrusion on constitutionally protected rights. The court determined that the ordinance served the compelling interest of preserving the integrity of the initiative and referendum process. The situation was analogous to limitations on contributions to political campaigns, which were constitutional. The interest was served in a manner which did not frustrate countervailing rights under U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Const. art. I, § 2. The ordinance did not completely prohibit contributions and did not interfere with effective advocacy or dissemination of information. The ordinance did not impermissibly limit associational rights. The means chosen in the ordinance of furthering the compelling interests were the least restrictive of respondent's constitutional rights. The $ 250 limit was not too low. The court reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed. Using strict scrutiny, the court determined that the ordinance served the compelling interest of preserving the integrity of the initiative and referendum process. That interest was served in a manner that did not frustrate countervailing constitutional rights. The least restrictive means had been chosen.

 

288 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Glaze


Crim. No. 21123


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 841; 614 P.2d 291; 166 Cal. Rptr. 859; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 202



August 7, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 31592138, David M. Kennick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. 8



8 Because appellant's freedom of expression claim has merit, his equal protection and preemption arguments are not reached.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction from the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) for operating a picture arcade between the hours of 2:00 a.m. and 9:00 a.m. in violation of Los Angeles Municipal Code § 103.101(g), contending that his freedom of expression and equal protection rights were violated by the statute.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction under Los Angeles Municipal Code § 103.101(g), arguing that the statute was a violation of his free speech rights under U.S. Const. amend. I. The law made illegal the operation of any picture arcade with two or more booths illegal between 2:00 a.m. and 9:00 a.m. On appeal, defendant contended that the statute was overbroad, and made protected speech, the viewing of arcade movies, illegal. The court held that the operation of a picture arcade was protected activity under Cal. Const. art. I, § 2 and U.S. Const. amend I, and that in order to pass constitutional muster, any statute regulating the operation of picture arcades must be precise and narrowly drafted. The court further held that the statute was not reasonable, because its goals included vague policies, such as limiting activity in the arcade during hours when, generally, overall criminal activity was relatively high. The court finally held that because the statute proscribed conduct that was protected and did not achieve its legitimate policy goals in the least restrictive manner, the statute violated defendant's First Amendment rights, and the court reversed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for operating a picture arcade, holding that the statute, as written, proscribed protected activity, and was not as narrowly drafted as it could have been in order to attain the desired effect.

Thompson v. County of Alameda


S.F. No. 24006


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 741; 614 P.2d 728; 167 Cal. Rptr. 70; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 196; 12 A.L.R.4th 701



July 14, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied August 21, 1980. Manuel, J., did not participate therein. Tobriner, J. and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Time of grt. or den. rehg. extd. to September 12, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 470490-6, John P. Sparrow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents disputed a decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted the general demurrer of respondent County of Alameda in appellants' claims for damages due to the murder of their son. The issues were whether any action or inaction by respondent in releasing a known dangerous juvenile offender on temporary release had led to the death of appellants' son and whether respondent was protected by immunity.


OVERVIEW: Respondent County of Alameda released a known dangerous juvenile offender on temporary release into the custody of his mother. Within 24 hours, he killed appellant parents' son. Appellants sued, and the trial court granted the county's general demurrer. On appeal, the court held that, as to the decisions to release the juvenile and the choice of his mother as custodian, there was no immunity for the county under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 818.2, 845, or 846, because its actions did not involve the adoption or failure to adopt any enactment or lack of enforcement of any law. However, the county was protected by immunity under Cal. Gov't Code § 820.2, because the decisions were a matter of discretion. Under Cal. Gov't Code § 845.8(a), immunity was granted specifically to decisions regarding the release of a prisoner. The county had no affirmative duty to warn appellants because it had no special and continuous relationship with appellants nor did it knowingly place their son into a foreseeably dangerous position. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. Respondent County of Alameda was protected by immunity under one statute, because its decisions were matters of discretion and, under another statute, immunity was granted specifically to decisions regarding the release of a prisoner. Respondent had no affirmative duty to warn about the release of the juvenile offender, who had made nonspecific threats of harm directed at nonspecific victims.

Mack v. Younger


S.F. No. 23597


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 687; 612 P.2d 966; 165 Cal. Rptr. 876; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 193



July 10, 1980 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner litigant filed a motion for an award of attorney's fees against respondent state of California and counsel for the parties thereafter negotiated a settlement of petitioner's claim and executed an agreement and release to that effect.


OVERVIEW: The Attorney General had claimed that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11361.5(b) that required law enforcement agencies, upon request, to destroy records of arrests or convictions for certain marijuana offenses was unconstitutional in that it had violated the separation of powers. In a companion case, the court upheld the constitutionality of § 11361.5(b) and granted an original writ of mandate compelling the Attorney General to process petitioner's application for destruction of the records of a conviction coming within the terms of § 11361.5(b). Petition sought attorney's fees for services rendered in the proceeding and the court appointed a referee because it was unable to follow the practice of remanding to the trial court because the underlying proceeding had been a petition for original writ. The court concluded that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5 provided that the attorney's fees to a successful party in any action which had resulted in the enforcement of an important right affecting the public interest were appropriate if a significant benefit, whether pecuniary or nonpecuniary, had been conferred on the general public or a large class of persons.


OUTCOME: The court adopted the terms of the settlement of attorney's fees, without interest, against the state of California in favor of petitioner in his claim for attorney's fees for services rendered in his proceeding compelling the Attorney General to process petitioner's application for destruction of his conviction records for marijuana offenses.

Van Atta v. Scott


S.F. No. 23946


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 424; 613 P.2d 210; 166 Cal. Rptr. 149; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 183



July 3, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of appellant Scott for a rehearing was denied August 14, 1980, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 662928, Joseph G. Kennedy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is modified in two respects. The burden of producing evidence as to community ties is to be with the detainee and the requirement of a written statement by the court of the reasons for the denial of OR release is disapproved. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed. Plaintiffs are to recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) finding that the pre-trial release system, as employed by the San Francisco courts, violated the due process clauses of the federal and state constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers brought an action challenging the constitutionality of the pretrial release and detention system employed by the City and County of San Francisco. The trial court found that, when applications are presented to the San Francisco courts, the presumption was against own recognizance release and the detainee bore the burden of showing that his application is meritorious. The trial court ruled that the system violated due process rights because the prosecution was not required to assume the burden of proving bail would be necessary to assure the detainee's presence in court and the court was not required to make written findings. On appeal, the reviewing court held that: (1) the prosecution must bear the burden of producing evidence of the detainee's record of non-appearance at prior court hearings and of the severity of sentence the detainee faces; (2) the detainee should bear the burden of producing evidence of community ties; (3) the prosecution must bear the burden of proof concerning the detainee's likelihood of appearing at future court proceedings; and (4) the court was not constitutionally required to issue a statement of reasons when OR release is denied.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed as to the finding that the pretrial release system violated due process rights in that the prosecution was not required to assume the burden of proving that bail was necessary, and modified to establish that the burden of producing evidence as to community ties was with the detainee, and the court was not required to record its reasons for the denial of own recognizance release.

Kinney v. Vaccari


S.F. No. 24122


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 348; 612 P.2d 877; 165 Cal. Rptr. 787; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 178



June 30, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 76075, Ross A. Carkeet, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landlord and managing agent challenged the judgment and assessment of damages pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 789.3, which were entered against them by the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California). Plaintiff tenants cross-challenged the trial court's interpretation of "tenant" under § 789.3. Defendants were charged with knowingly failing to pay utility bills in an attempt to force plaintiffs to move.


OVERVIEW: Defendant managing agent knowingly allowed gas service to plaintiff tenants' building to be terminated. Plaintiffs then filed suit for injunctive relief against defendant landlord and managing agent and sought damages pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code §7 89.3. The trial court found that defendant landlord was vicariously liable for the acts of defendant managing agent, and entered judgment against defendants for compensatory and punitive damages. The trial court also assessed penalties against defendants pursuant to § 789.3 in the amount of $ 100 a day, per "tenant." Plaintiff cross-challenged the court's definition of "tenant" under § 789.3. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and stated that the willful and malicious conduct of defendant managing agent was unprovoked, warranted the assessment of penalties under § 789.3, and that defendant landlord was vicariously responsible for the actions of defendant managing agent. The court also found that § 789.3 was constitutional and that the penalties assessed were justified by the evidence. Finally, the court affirmed the definition of "tenant" as applied by the trial court in its assessment of damages under § 789.3.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and assessment of damages against defendant landlord and managing agent because the damages were proportioned to defendants' misconduct, were necessary to achieve the statute's penalty deterrent purpose, and were not unconstitutionally excessive. The court also affirmed the trial court's definition of "tenant" as used in the penalty section of the statute and awarded plaintiffs costs on appeal.

Pacific Gas & Electric Co. v. State Board of Equalization


S.F. No. 24111


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 277; 611 P.2d 463; 165 Cal. Rptr. 122; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 174



June 5, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied July 9, 1980, and the judgment was modified to read as printed above. Tobriner, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 740578, Byron Arnold, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to dismiss the action. Respondent shall recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff public utility companies filed an action for mandamus and declaratory relief after the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco upheld the California State Board of Equalization's refusal to adjust the assessment of plaintiffs' real property to its 1975-1976 value in accordance with the recently enacted Cal. Const. art XIIIA.


OVERVIEW: After Cal. Const. art. XIIIA became effective, one of plaintiff public utility companies requested a ruling from the California State Board of Equalization regarding the applicability of the "rollback" provision to state-assessed public utility property. The board declined to adjust its 1978 assessments of plaintiff's property to conform with Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, § 2(a). Its action was upheld by the superior court, which ruled that the rollback provision did not apply to state-assessed property. Plaintiffs filed an action for mandamus and declaratory relief. The court concluded that plaintiffs' action was barred by Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 32 and that their proper recourse was an action for refund under Cal. Rev. Tax Code §§ 5096 and 5140. Although plaintiffs claimed that there was an inadequate remedy at law, because they would be required to file claims in several counties, the court noted that there was no exception to art. XIII, § 32 based on an inadequate remedy at law. The judgment was reversed with directions to dismiss the action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and dismissed the action because plaintiff utility companies' action was barred by the California Constitution, and there was no exception to the constitutional provision for cases of inadequate remedy at law. The proper recourse was for plaintiffs to pay the taxes and file an action for refund.

Santa Barbara v. Adamson


L.A. No. 31126


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 123; 610 P.2d 436; 164 Cal. Rptr. 539; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 168; 12 A.L.R.4th 219



May 15, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied June 19, 1980. Clark J., Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 121167, Arden T. Jensen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is reversed. The case is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant housing residents sought reversal of an order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), granting respondent municipality an injunction barring appellants from sharing the same residence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant housing residents challenged an injunction granted to respondent municipality barring appellants from living together as a household. The court reversed and remanded, holding that the state constitutional right to privacy precluded respondent from prohibiting unrelated persons from living together. Appellants were a group of unrelated persons who lived together in the house owned by the lead appellant. Respondent sought to bar them from living together on the grounds that their conduct violated respondent's zoning ordinance restricting the number of unmarried and unrelated people who could live in a single dwelling. In reversing, the court noted that the right to privacy was a fundamental right which could be restricted only if respondent could establish a compelling state interest for doing so. Finding no compelling interest which existed, and finding the alternatives suggested by respondent unsatisfactory, the court vacated the injunction.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed, and the case was remanded. Respondent municipality lacked a compelling state interest to justify its zoning ordinance, which barred appellant housing residents from sharing living quarters, and thus lacked sufficient grounds to interfere with appellants' privacy rights.

People v. Green


Crim. Nos. 20555, 21068


Supreme Court of California


27 Cal. 3d 1; 609 P.2d 468; 164 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 165



April 24, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied May 28, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sutter County, No. 23490, James G. Changaris, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In Crim. 20555 the judgment is reversed as to count III; on count I the findings of special circumstances are set aside and the judgment is reversed insofar as it relates to penalty; in all other respects the judgment is affirmed.

In Crim. 21068 the motion to strike is granted, the order to show cause is discharged, and the petition for habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sutter County (California), which sentenced defendant to death following his convictions for first-degree murder, robbery, and kidnapping. In a consolidated proceeding, defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, alleging that he was denied effective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death following his convictions for the crimes of first-degree murder, robbery, and kidnapping. The jury found special circumstances existed, because the murder was willful, deliberate, premeditated, and personally committed by defendant during the commission of a robbery and a kidnapping. Defendant appealed and also filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus, alleging that he was denied effective assistance of counsel. The court found that defendant had taken his estranged wife to the woods where he shot her in the head after she sought an annulment. Defendant was not denied effective assistance of counsel, because counsel was not required to make a motion for a change of venue. Thus the petition for habeas corpus was denied. However, the court ruled that the special circumstances findings had to be vacated, because there had been no robbery or kidnapping. Asportation by fraud alone did not constitute kidnapping, and there was no intent to commit robbery before the act. Therefore the punishment of death could not stand, and the judgment was partially reversed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied, but the judgment was reversed as to the conviction for kidnapping. Defendant was not entitled to habeas corpus relief. However, defendant's death sentence could not stand and the findings of special circumstances were set aside.

 

296 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Metromedia, Inc. v. San Diego


L.A. No. 30782


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 848; 610 P.2d 407; 164 Cal. Rptr. 510; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 160; 10 ELR 20862; 14 ERC (BNA) 1865



April 14, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied May 14, 1980, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. 332881 and 333292, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged summary judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), in favor of respondent media companies in respondents' action to enjoin enforcement of an ordinance banning all off-site advertising billboards and requiring the removal of existing billboards following expiration of an amortization period.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city enacted an ordinance which banned off-site commercial billboards and required removal of existing billboards following expiration of an amortization period. Respondent media companies owned billboards affected by the ordinance and sued to enjoin its enforcement. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondents and held the ordinance was unconstitutional. On appeal of the summary judgment the reviewing court held that ordinance which was enacted to aid in elimination of traffic hazards and improvement of city appearance was within the police power. The ordinance did not violate U.S. Const. amend. I free speech requirements, because it was a time, place, and manner restriction. The failure of the ordinance to allow for compensation for removal of signs within 660 feet of the highway was in contradiction to state law, and was therefore, preempted by the Outdoor Advertising Act (Bus. & Prof. Code § 5200 et seq. Summary judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment for respondent media companies was reversed and the cause was remanded. Enactment of an ordinance banning off-site bill-board advertising was within the city's police power, but removal of existing signs within 660 feet of highways without compensation violated a preemptive state law.

 

297 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lanphear


Crim. Nos. 20934, 21167


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 814; 608 P.2d 689; 163 Cal. Rptr. 601; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 159



April 10, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of both parties for a rehearing were denied May 14, 1980. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 35417, Don A. Turner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it relates to penalty. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed. The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's automatic appeal was taken, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1239, from a judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), imposing the death penalty following defendant's conviction of first degree murder. Defendant also sought a writ of habeas corpus based on allegations of ineffective representation by appointed trial counsel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. An automatic appeal was taken, and defendant sought a writ of habeas corpus based on allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. The appellate court found that counsel's actions that were challenged by defendant were informed tactical choices, and that counsel performed within the range of reasonable competence. Cross-examination of defendant was proper, the prosecution's calling a crucial witness after its case in chief was proper, and the jury was appropriately admonished to avoid media influence. Two prospective jurors were excused after they offered equivocal statements regarding their ability to deliberate on the death penalty. Neither juror indicated that his or her beliefs or feelings would have automatically prevented him or her from voting for the death penalty or would have affected his or her determination of defendant's guilt. Defense counsel's failure to object to excusal of these two jurors did not bar the claim of error. This error resulted in defendant's death penalty being reversed; judgment was affirmed in all other respects, and defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied.


OUTCOME: Defendant's judgment of guilt of first-degree murder was affirmed, judgment imposing the death penalty was reversed, an order to show cause was discharged, and defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied. Defense counsel's assistance was reasonably competent, but the court erred in excusing two prospective jurors who did not offer unequivocal statements of their inability to deliberate regarding imposition of the death penalty.

Olson v. Cory


L.A. No. 31142


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 672; 27 Cal. 3d 203; 27 Cal. 3d 532; 609 P.2d 991; 636 P.2d 532; 164 Cal. Rptr. 217; 178 Cal. Rptr. 568; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 154



March 27, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehg. den. May 30, 1980
Reprinted as modified 27 C.3d 203; Reprinted as modified 27 Cal. 3d 532, 178 Cal. Rptr 568, 636 P.2d 532 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA 000437, Ernest J. Zack, Judge.
Olson v. Cory, 93 Cal. App. 3d 942, 156 Cal. Rptr. 127, 1979 Cal. App. LEXIS 1825 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., June 11, 1979)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a state controller and a county auditor-controller, sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) declaring unconstitutional a legislative amendment purporting to place a limit on cost-of-living increases previously provided for judicial salaries in an action filed by plaintiff judges.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a state controller and a county auditor-controller, challenged an order declaring unconstitutional a legislative amendment purporting to place a limit on cost-of-living increases previously provided for judicial salaries. The lower court had awarded judgment to plaintiff judges, finding the amendment unconstitutional on the ground that it constituted an impermissible impairment of vested contractual rights. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part, finding that the legislative amendment infringed upon constitutionally protected rights when applied to some, but not all, judges. The court explained that the amendment was unconstitutional as to judges serving during any term prior to the effective date of the legislative amendment. The amendment was also unconstitutional as to judicial pensioners whose benefits were based on some proportionate amount of the salary for the office of such a judge.


OUTCOME: The order awarding judgment to plaintiff judges and declaring unconstitutional a legislative amendment purporting to place a limit on cost-of-living increases previously provided for judicial salaries was affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court found that the amendment was unconstitutional as applied to judges serving during any term prior to its effective date.

Younger v. Jensen


L.A. No. 31024


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 397; 605 P.2d 813; 161 Cal. Rptr. 905; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 141; 1980-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P63,172



January 31, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of respondent Exxon Corporation for a rehearing was denied March 13, 1980. Tobriner, J., did not participate therein. White, J., * participated therein. Clark, J., Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C-170995 and C-184173, John L. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders denying enforcement of the subpoenas are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of orders from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied two petitions for enforcement of subpoenas to give evidence at an investigation into possible antitrust violations affecting California in the marketing of natural gas that originated in Alaska.


OVERVIEW: Appellant delegated authority to conduct an investigation into sale and distribution of natural gas insofar as it affected the state of California and to determine the existence, nature, and scope of violations of the federal and state antitrust laws. Responses to appellant's subpoenas were deemed inadequate; therefore, appellant commenced an enforcement proceeding. The court denied enforcement on the grounds that appellant's investigation was preempted by federal regulation of interstate distribution on natural gas under laws conferring jurisdiction on the federal power commission (FPC). On appeal, the court found appellant's investigation was authorized under Gov't. Code § 11180. Further, the Natural Gas Act (Act), 15 U.S.C.S. §§ 717-717w, did not preclude application of federal antitrust laws to interstate gas transactions under FPC regulation and the Act did not preempt state regulation of matters only peripherally affecting federal concerns. Accordingly, the orders denying enforcement of the subpoenas were reversed.


OUTCOME: Orders denying enforcement of subpoenas reversed because the Natural Gas Act did not preclude the application of federal antitrust laws to interstate gas transactions under federal power commission regulation.

People v. Chavez


Crim. No. 20673


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 334; 605 P.2d 401; 161 Cal. Rptr. 762; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 139



January 29, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 332520, Bonnie Lee Martin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment is modified to provide that defendant shall serve only one additional period of imprisonment pursuant to section 12022.5. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of three counts of assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury and was found to have used a firearm as to each count, in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 245(a), 12022.5.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of three counts of assault by force likely to produce great bodily injury and was found to have used a firearm as to each count. Defendant sought review of his conviction. The court affirmed the conviction, asserting that exercise of the trial court's discretion in the appointment of counsel should have rested upon consideration of the facts and interests involved. Although the court found that the trial court erred in refusing to consider the retention of defendant's initial appointed counsel, nothing in the record suggested that defendant was prejudiced in any way by the error. The court further concluded that the state constitutional confrontation clause, Cal. Const. art. I, § 15, was not violated by admission of a prior statement of a witness who testified at trial and was subject to full cross-examination. The record contained sufficient evidence to support defendant's assault convictions and firearm finding. Defendant's sentence was modified to provide for a single enhancement under Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5. At the sentencing hearing, the trial court failed to indicate to the jury that only one enhancement under § 12022.5 should have been imposed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of assault on finding substantial evidence to support the conviction and no prejudicial error by the trial court. However, the court modified defendant's sentence to provide for a single enhancement because the trial court erred in failing to indicate at sentencing that only one enhancement could have been imposed.
