Price v. Civil Service Commission


S.F. No. 23836


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 257; 604 P.2d 1365; 161 Cal. Rptr. 475; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 136; 21 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1512; 22 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P30,589



January 25, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied March 20, 1980, and the dissenting opinion was modified to read as printed above. Mosk, J., Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 257623, Vernon Stoll, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Civil Service Commission appealed a decision by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) favoring plaintiff district attorney and permanently enjoining defendant from implementing an affirmative action program as unconstitutional and violating Civil Rights Act of 1964, title VII, § 703(a), 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e, the California Fair Employment Practice Act (FEPA), Cal. Lab. Code § 1420(a), and the county charter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant Civil Service Commission adopted Sacramento County, Ca., Civil Service Commission Rule 7.10 to provide a procedure for adjustment of disproportionate representation of minority personnel in defendant's work force which was a result of discriminatory employment practices. Plaintiff district attorney filed suit seeking to enjoin defendant from implementing the hiring program, which was granted by the trial court. Defendant sought review. The court reversed judgment and remanded the case, because neither the pertinent antidiscrimination enactments nor the constitutional equal protection guarantee prohibited a governmental employer from voluntarily implementing a reasonable race-conscious hiring program to remedy the effects of past discriminatory employment practices. The court found that defendant was authorized to adopt a general remedial affirmative action program. Further, the race-conscious hiring ratios authorized under Rule 7.10 did not violate the county charter, the California Fair Employment Practice Act (FEPA), Cal. Lab. Code § 1420(a), the Civil Rights Act of 1964, title VII, § 703(a), 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e, or constitutional equal protection principles.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded judgment of the trial court in favor of defendant Civil Service Commission in an action filed by plaintiff attorney general seeking to enjoin defendant from implementing an affirmative action hiring plan. The court held that neither antidiscrimination statutes nor constitutional equal protection prohibited a governmental employer from voluntarily implementing a reasonable race-conscious hiring program.

County of Los Angeles v. Berk


L.A. No. 31099


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 201; 605 P.2d 381; 161 Cal. Rptr. 742; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 134



January 24, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied March 13, 1980, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. 999043, SWC 19574, Richard Schauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The motion of County to dismiss the appeal herein is denied. The judgments, which we interpret and construe to quiet title in the subject public easement in plaintiffs as trustees for the people of the State of California, are and each of them is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant buyer appealed a judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) favoring plaintiffs city and county who brought separate actions to establish a public beach recreation easement on certain property owned by defendant. Defendant's cross-complaints sought declaratory relief negating the existence of the claimed easement, and also money damages on theories of slander of title, unjust enrichment, and inverse condemnation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs city and county brought separate actions to establish a public beach recreation easement on certain property owned by defendant. A cross-complaint was filed by defendant, seeking declaratory relief that there was no easement, and requesting money damages for slander of title, unjust enrichment, and inverse condemnation. Judgment was entered in favor of plaintiffs, and defendant appealed. The court affirmed and held that the evidence established that the property had been subject to public recreational use of substantial diversity and scope for several decades. Plaintiff county maintained the property as if it were publicly owned, cleaning it regularly of the debris generated by public use and providing lifeguard services. The use made of the property by the public was as part of a public recreational area. This was a sufficient finding of adversity requisite to implied in law dedication. The court further concluded that public use was sufficient notice to the world at large, which continued for the requisite period of time for a dedication to the public as implied in law, and bound not only record owners but all persons having interests in the subject property.


OUTCOME: The affirmed the judgments and quieted title in the subject public easement for plaintiffs county and city as trustees for the people of the State of California. The property owned by defendant buyer was subject to an implied in law dedication to the public because it had been substantially adversely used by the public for recreation over a long period of time.

People v. Cruz


Crim. No. 19162


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 233; 605 P.2d 830; 162 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 135



January 24, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied February 20, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lassen County, No. 13105, Arthur J. Anderson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, insofar as it provides for the penalty of death, is modified to life imprisonment, and as modified, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Lassen County (California), which convicted him of three counts of first degree murder and sentenced him to the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of three counts of first degree murder and sentenced to death. He sought review and challenged the sufficiency of the evidence to support his conviction, and the trial court's refusal to instruct the jury, sua sponte, on malice aforethought. He also contended that the improper insanity test was used at trial and that his death sentence was unconstitutional. On review the trial court affirmed the judgment, and modified the sentence to life imprisonment. The court modified the death penalty sentence to provide for life imprisonment to make the punishment constitutionally valid. However, the court held that there was substantial evidence of premeditation, deliberation, and malice to support the jury's decision to reject appellant's diminished capacity defense. Moreover, the jury also rejected appellant's insanity defense. The court also held that the trial court's refusal to instruct the jury, sua sponte, that malice aforethought included the awareness of the obligation to act within the general body of laws regulating society was not prejudicial, because appellant failed to present evidence on his awareness of such obligation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and modified his sentence to life imprisonment. The court held that there was substantial evidence to support the jury's rejection of his diminished capacity, the trial court's refusal to give appellant's proposed instruction on malice aforethought was not prejudicial, and that the jury rejected his claim of insanity. However, the trial court's imposition of the death penalty was unconstitutional.

County of Inyo v. Public Utilities Com.


S.F. No. 23990


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 154; 604 P.2d 566; 161 Cal. Rptr. 172; 1980 Cal. LEXIS 131



January 7, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  The order of the Public Utilities Commission is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought review of a ruling by respondent public utilities commission (PUC) (California), which held that it did not have jurisdiction over municipally owned utilities unless expressly provided by statute, and that the legislature had not granted the PUC such jurisdiction. Petitioner had sought to have respondent PUC regulate the water rates charged by the respondent city department of water and power, real party in interest.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner county sought review of respondent public utilities commission (PUC)'s ruling that it did not have the authority to regulate the water rates being charged citizens of petitioner county by respondent city department of water and power, real party in interest. Petitioner emphasized the need for respondent PUC to regulate because petitioner's residents were unable to vote in municipal elections and therefore, lacked any political leverage to prevent exploitation by respondent city department of water and power. By purchasing water rights, the city had acquired virtually complete control of valley water. The city's authority to acquire the water rights were granted to it by Cal. Const. art. XI, § 9 and Los Angeles, Ca., City Charter § 211(j). The court agreed with respondent PUC that it lacked the authority to regulate the water rights in the county. The court held that California statutes failed to grant respondent PUC any authority over municipal utilities other than common carriers, and the legislature had the authority under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5, to grant the authority. The order of respondent PUC was upheld.


OUTCOME: The order of respondent public utility commission that it lacked authority to regulate the water rates being charged residents of the county by the city department of water and power was affirmed, because the commission had no inherent or statutory authority to regulate the water rates for the county.

W. v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 23580


Supreme Court of California


26 Cal. 3d 41; 603 P.2d 1296; 160 Cal. Rptr. 700; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 339



December 12, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed for.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon appeal by the people, the United States Supreme Court vacated and remanded the judgment of the court, which had issued a writ of prohibition restraining respondent juvenile court from proceeding pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 253 and Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 254 to redetermine petitioner juvenile's status as a ward of that court, after a referee of respondent court had absolved petitioner of charged misconduct.


OVERVIEW: After the court issued petitioner's writ of prohibition restraining respondent juvenile court from proceeding pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 253 and Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 254 to redetermine his status as a ward of that court after a referee of such court had absolved petitioner of charged misconduct, the court's order was vacated. On remand, the court again issued the writ of prohibition, holding that jeopardy attached in juvenile proceedings when "entered upon" whether before a juvenile court judge or referee, notwithstanding that a referee's findings and orders may be advisory only.The court held that such proceedings subjected petitioner to double jeopardy in violation of U.S. Const. amend. V, and thus, were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court issued petitioner juvenile's writ of prohibition restraining respondent juvenile court from proceeding to redetermine his status as a ward of that court after a referee had absolved him of charged misconduct. Jeopardy attached in juvenile proceedings when "entered upon" whether before a judge or referee, notwithstanding that a referee's findings and orders might be advisory only, and thus petitioner was subjected to double jeopardy.

Consumers Lobby Against Monopolies v. Public Utilities Com.


S.F. Nos. 23863, 23868


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 891; 603 P.2d 41; 160 Cal. Rptr. 124; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 349; 33 P.U.R.4th 148



December 6, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioners' application in No. 23868 for a rehearing was denied January 3, 1980. Bird, C. J., and Tobriner, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: As appears from the concurring and dissenting opinions hereto all members of the court concur in Part I of this opinion. Justices Clark, Richardson and Manuel concur in parts III and IV, holding that the commission lacks authority to award attorney fees and costs in quasi-legislative ratemaking proceedings. For the reasons there stated, the decision in S.F. 23868 (TURN) is affirmed.

The Chief Justice and Justices Tobriner and Newman concur in parts II and V, holding that the commission has jurisdiction to award attorney fees and costs under the common fund doctrine in quasi-judicial reparation proceedings, and in those actions may make such awards to nonattorneys who appear before it in a representative capacity. For the reasons there stated, the decision in S.F. 23863 (CLAM) is annulled, and the proceeding is remanded to the commission for a determination of the fees and costs to be awarded to Wilner and CLAM in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two consolidated proceedings, plaintiff lobby group and plaintiff interest group sought award of fees and costs from respondent Public Utilities Commission (California) for representations against Pacific Telephone & Telegraph Company.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff lobby group hired a nonattorney to investigate the failure of the telephone company to collect required tariff charges on equipment of commercial customers. After plaintiff filed a complaint with respondent Public Utilities Commission, the telephone company settled, and the nonattorney sought to recover costs and fees. In the second case, the telephone company filed an application for a rate increase that was challenged by plaintiff interest group, who then sought attorney fees and costs. Respondent held in both cases that it lacked the power to award fees and costs. The court affirmed the decision in plaintiff interest group's appeal but annulled the decision in plaintiff lobby group's appeal, with remand for determination of fees and costs. The court held that the commission had jurisdiction to award attorney fees and costs pursuant to the equitable "common fund" doctrine in quasi-judicial reparation proceedings, but not in quasi-legislative rate-making proceedings. The court further held that the commission had the authority to award fees and costs to nonattorneys for their services in a representative capacity.


OUTCOME: The court annulled respondent Public Utilities Commission's decision in the appeal of plaintiff lobby group because respondent did have the authority to award fees and costs to nonattorneys for their services in a representative capacity. The court affirmed the decision by respondent in plaintiff interest group's appeal, holding that it did not have the authority to award fees and costs in quasi-legislative rate-making proceedings.

Hays v. Wood


S.F. No. 23830


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 772; 603 P.2d 19; 160 Cal. Rptr. 102; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 344



November 30, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 36319, Arthur B. Broaddus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to enter judgment in conformity with the views herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city councilman appealed an order of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which upheld a portion of the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code § 87207(b)(2), which required public officials who were also attorneys to disclose clients' names if the official's income from the client equaled or exceeded $ 1,000 in a calendar year, in plaintiff city attorney's action to compel defendant to disclose the information.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city councilman, who was an attorney, challenged the trial court's order upholding a portion of the Political Reform Act of 1974, in plaintiff city attorney's action to compel disclosure pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 87207(b)(2). Defendant sought to establish the unconstitutionality of the disclosure requirements of § 87207(b)(2), which required public officials who were also attorneys or brokers to disclose their clients' names if they contributed at least $ 1,000 to the official's income in a calendar year. The trial court upheld § 87207(b)(2)'s validity, and directed disclosure. The court reversed the trial court's order and invalidated § 87207(b)(2), holding that even though § 87207(b)(2) did not dilute the attorney-client privilege or defendant's duty to preserve his client's confidences, and that in general, § 87207(b)(2) was not unconstitutionally overbroad because it was restricted to high-level offices which had a substantial influence on public policy, it violated state and federal constitutional requirements of equal protection because the classification based on the two professions bore no fair and reasonable relationship to the stated legislative objective.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment upholding the Political Reform Act of 1974's disclosure requirement in plaintiff city attorney's action to compel defendant city councilman's disclosure of clients' names, because the requirement, which applied only to public officials who were also attorneys or brokers, bore no reasonable relationship to the stated legislative objective and thus violated equal protection principals.

 

309 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Blair


Crim. No. 20936


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 640; 602 P.2d 738; 159 Cal. Rptr. 818; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 329



November 9, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 135621, Earl C. Broady, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of two murders. Defendant argued that the trial court should have suppressed evidence obtained pursuant to subpoenas, but without prior judicial approval.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder, but held that information regarding the charges made on defendant's credit card, and records of a telephone call that defendant made from his hotel room, should have been suppressed because they were received before authorities obtained judicial approval to receive the evidence. The court ruled that a subpoena duces tecum issued pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1326 was purely a ministerial act, and that it did not constitute legal process entitling law enforcement authorities access to the records until a judicial determination was made that the authorities were legally entitled to the information. The court found that there was other evidence to support the conviction. The evidence established that defendant was in Los Angeles on the night of the murders, that he was acquainted with one of the victims, and that he asked directions to the street on which the victims lived about the time of the murders.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for two murders. The court held that in light of other evidence offered at trial, it was not reasonably probable that a result more favorable to defendant would have been reached absent the trial court's errors in admitting the credit card and telephone records evidence, which the authorities received pursuant to subpoenas, but before obtaining court approval.

 

310 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


M. v. Superior Court of Sonoma County


S.F. No. 23929


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 608; 601 P.2d 572; 159 Cal. Rptr. 340; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 327



November 5, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied December 5, 1979. Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of prohibition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minor sought a writ of prohibition, based on state and federal equal protection grounds, to compel respondent Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), to dismiss the information that charged him with unlawful sexual intercourse, under Cal. Penal Code § 261.5, for having sex with a woman under 18 who was not his wife.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner minor was charged with a felony violation of unlawful sexual intercourse under Cal. Penal Code § 261.5 for having engaged in sexual intercourse with a young woman who was not his wife and who was under the age of 18 years. Defendant sought a writ of prohibition to compel respondent lower court to dismiss the information by which he was charged, on the grounds that Cal. Penal Code § 261.5 violated the equal protection clauses of both the state and federal constitutions as only females were protected by § 261.5, while only males were prosecuted under it. The court held that while § 261.5 did classify both victims and offenders by gender, there was a compelling and demonstrable state interest in minimizing both the number of illegitimate teenage pregnancies and their consequences. Thus, gender classifications were justified under the strict scrutiny standard and equal protection requirements of both state and federal constitutions were met. Defendant's petition for writ of prohibition was, therefore, denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner minor's request for writ of prohibition to compel respondent lower court to dismiss the information by which he was charged with unlawful sexual intercourse. The court held that there was a compelling state interest which justified gender classifications, and thus, the equal protection requirements for both state and federal constitutions were satisfied.

 

311 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. McGaughran


Crim. No. 20293


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 577; 601 P.2d 207; 159 Cal. Rptr. 191; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 325



October 25, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 5986, David Menary, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which convicted defendant of burglary committed by breaking into a locked automobile with intent to steal in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459.


OVERVIEW: An officer stopped defendant for driving down the wrong direction on a one-way street. The officer asked for both defendant and the passenger's identification. The officer then initiated a radio check for outstanding arrest warrants. Ten minutes later the dispatcher reported a burglary warrant for defendant and two traffic warrants for the passenger. Defendant's car was searched and a radio that had been stolen from a car parked a block away from where defendant was stopped was discovered. Defendant was convicted of burglary pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 459. Defendant appealed, claiming that the lower court should have granted his pretrial motion to suppress the evidence under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5. The court reversed, holding that under U.S. Const. Amend. IV and Cal. Veh. Code § 40504, because the detention was investigatory, the officer was not justified in detaining defendant beyond what was reasonably necessary to issue him a citation. The court held that the additional period of detention of defendant for the purpose of seeking out unrelated arrest warrants was not reasonably necessary to the purpose of the detention, and that the evidence should have been suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for burglary because the investigatory stop of defendant by the officer exceeded constitutional bounds. The court held that the detention of defendant for the purpose of a warrant check was not reasonably related to the purpose of the stop, which should have been to issue a citation or a warning for defendant's driving violation.

 

312 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Eric J.


L.A. No. 31076


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 522; 601 P.2d 549; 159 Cal. Rptr. 317; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 321



October 22, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied January 17, 1980, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 18082, Richard L. Vaughn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The commitment order is modified to recite that appellant's maximum term is three years, two months, and that he is to receive forty-six days credit for time in custody prior to commitment. As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile ward sought review of the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which continued his juvenile court wardship, and committed him to the Youth Authority pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. §§ 602, 731, after finding that he committed burglary, and in contempt of court for violating conditions of an earlier probation order.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile ward was committed to the youth authority for three years and six months after he was found guilty of burglary, and in contempt of court for violating conditions of an earlier probation. He appealed, and the court modified his commitment to three years, two months with credit for time served in custody prior to commitment. The court initially considered and rejected appellant's contention that his confession to his employer in a police officer's presence should have been suppressed because he did not receive a Miranda warning prior to interrogation. The court held that since the police officer did not participate in the questioning, no Miranda warning was necessary. The court also ruled that there was no violation of equal protection in the disparate sentencing for a juvenile and an adult convicted of burglary because the two groups were not similarly situated in that the goals (punishment for the adult versus rehabilitation for the juvenile) were different. Finally, the court ruled that appellant's sentence was erroneously computed using the maximum terms for both the burglary and misdemeanor instead of utilizing only one-third of the misdemeanor term.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's commitment order to change appellant juvenile ward's maximum term to three years, two months less 46 days credit for time in custody prior to commitment, because although there was no denial of equal protection, and the trial court should have sentenced appellant to three years for the burglary, but only two consecutive months for the misdemeanor contempt.

Mosk v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 31140


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 474; 601 P.2d 1030; 159 Cal. Rptr. 494; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 319



October 18, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On November 15, 1979, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the Los Angeles County Superior Court to vacate the order entered in the case of Mosk v. Commission on Judicial Performance, No. SOC 55720, on July 12, 1979, and to enter a new order quashing the Commission's subpoena of Justice Mosk in C.J.P. No. 3012.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner associate justice sought review of the judgment of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his petition to quash a subpoena ordering him to appear as a witness before the Commission on Judicial Performance, real party in interest. The proceeding was transferred to the court after the Court of Appeal, Second Appellant District (California) granted petitioner's writ of mandate, and commission appealed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, an associate justice of the Supreme Court of California, was served with a subpoena which ordered him to appear before the Commission on Judicial Performance, the real party in interest. Plaintiff filed a petition to quash the subpoena. The appellate court had issued a judgment that reversed the order of respondent trial court, but the California Supreme Court, composed of seven justices pro hac vice, transferred the matter to itself for hearing before that judgment became final. Petitioner contended that a court composed solely of assigned justices pro tempore did not have constitutional authority or jurisdiction to act, that Cal. R. Ct. 902.5 was unconstitutional because it violated the requirement of Cal. Const. art. VI, § 18(f), and that petitioner could not be compelled to testify at a public hearing before the real party in interest. The court held it did have jurisdiction to act, that Cal. R. Ct. 902.5 was unconstitutional, that plaintiff could not be compelled to testify before real party in interest, and that the matter be remanded to respondent for an order quashing the subpoena.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and held that real party in interest could not issue a subpoena to petitioner, an associate justice of the Supreme Court of California, to testify in a public hearing. The court held that Cal. Const. art. VI, § 18(f) did not authorize public investigations and hearings, and that Cal. R. Ct. 902.5 was unconstitutional. The court also held that a panel composed of justices pro hac vice could exercise the authority of the court.

In re Anders


Crim. No. 20198


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 414; 599 P.2d 1364; 158 Cal. Rptr. 661; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 314



October 4, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied without prejudice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus to challenge his conviction and punishment for disorderly conduct in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(a) issued from a California trial court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of disorderly conduct under Cal. Penal Code § 647, based upon the testimony of a police officer who observed defendant masturbating in a closed pay toilet stall in a bus station restroom. The door to the toilet stall was solid, except for two 12-by 18-inch wire mesh grates; the officer looked through the upper grate to observe petitioner's conduct. Defendant contended that the officer's observation constituted an illegal search. The court denied the petition without prejudice and held that the claim of an illegal search was not cognizable in a habeas corpus proceeding. The court held that it had narrowly construed § 647 so that it prohibited only the solicitation or commission of conduct in a public place or one open to the public or exposed to public view, which involved the touching of the genitals, buttocks, or female breast, for purposes of sexual arousal, gratification, annoyance or offense, by a person who knew or should have known of the presence of persons who may be offended by the conduct.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's habeas corpus petition without prejudice and held that, while defendant might be entitled to relief, the record was not sufficient for the court to determine whether a narrow construction of the disorderly conduct statute could be retroactively applied to defendant, and that the dismissal was without prejudice to defendant's right to seek relief by writ of habeas corpus in a proper court.

In re Waters of Long Valley Creek Stream Sys.


S.F. No. 23932


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 339; 599 P.2d 656; 158 Cal. Rptr. 350; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 309



September 14, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied October 25, 1979. Clark, J., Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lassen County, No. 12999, Arthur J. Anderson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant riparian landowner sought review of a decree by the Superior Court of Lassen County (California) denying appellant's exceptions to an order by respondent, the State Water Resources Control Board, which established the rights of several riparian landowners to the water in a stream system in a proceeding under Cal. Water Code § 2500 et seq.


OVERVIEW: After extensive litigation among the landowners claiming rights to the water in a stream system, several claimants filed a petition with respondent, the State Water Resources Control Board, to settle the conflicts that gave rise to the litigation. Pursuant to Cal. Water Code § 2500 et seq., respondent conducted an investigation, considered numerous claims, and entered an order establishing the claimants' rights to the water in the stream system. In a proceeding before the trial court, appellant riparian landowner raised exceptions to the order because respondent extinguished his prospective water rights with regard to a portion of his land. The trial court denied appellant's exceptions. The court reversed, concluding that respondent had broad authority to adjudicate and limit water rights, including future riparian rights, in a statutory proceeding under § 2500. However, respondent exceeded its authority by forever extinguishing appellant's future water rights because it failed to establish that the most reasonable and beneficial use of the waters could not be promoted as effectively by placing other, less severe restrictions on appellant's rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decree extinguishing appellant riparian landowner's future water rights because respondent, the State Water Resources Control Board, did not have the authority to extinguish appellant's rights in the absence of a finding that the extinguishment was necessary to insure that the water in the stream system would be put to the most reasonable and beneficial uses. The court remanded the matter for further proceedings.

Sears, Roebuck & Co. v. San Diego County Dist. Council of Carpenters


L.A. No. 30562


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 317; 599 P.2d 676; 158 Cal. Rptr. 370; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 308; 102 L.R.R.M. 2312; 87 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,208



September 14, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 347511, Joseph A. Kilgarif, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a preliminary injunction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff department store appealed the decision of the court which held that the National Labor Relations Board had primary jurisdiction regarding a dispute with defendant union regarding plaintiff's refusal to use only union carpenters. The U.S. Supreme Court granted certiorari and remanded the case to the court for further action pursuant to the Moscone Act, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 527.3.


OVERVIEW: Defendant union began picketing plaintiff department store due to plaintiff's refusal to use only union carpenters. When defendant rejected plaintiff's request that the pickets be removed from plaintiff's property, plaintiff sued and obtained a preliminary injunction restraining defendant, its officers, agents, representatives and members, from picketing on plaintiff's property. Defendant appealed, and the court of appeal reversed the order of the trial court finding that the picketing was protected activity under the National Labor Relations Act, 29 U.S.C.S. § 151, et seq. Plaintiff appealed, and the court held that the National Labor Relations Board (NLRB) had primary jurisdiction over the controversy. The U.S. Supreme Court granted certiorari and concluded that the court erred in holding the NLRB had primary jurisdiction and remanded the case. On remand, the court found defendant's picketing to be lawful under California law and ruled that under the Moscone Act, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 527.3, the trial court lacked the power to enjoin the picketing. As a result, the order granting a preliminary injunction was reversed.


OUTCOME: On remand, the trial court's order granting a preliminary injunction was reversed because defendant union's peaceful picketing outside plaintiff department store, which involved neither fraud, violence, breach of the peace, nor interference with access or egress, was not subject to injunction by the court.

Pryor v. Municipal Court


L.A. No. 30901


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 238; 599 P.2d 636; 158 Cal. Rptr. 330; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 305



September 7, 1979 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned for writs of prohibition and mandate to bar his trial by the Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) on a charge of disorderly conduct, a misdemeanor in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(a). Defendant argued that the phrase "lewd and dissolute conduct" in § 647(a) was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant solicited a police officer to perform an act of oral copulation. He was charged with violating Cal. Penal Code § 647(a) by soliciting a lewd or dissolute act and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11357(b) for possession of marijuana. Defendant pled guilty to § 11357(b). On the § 647(a) charge, the jury was instructed that oral copulation between males was "lewd or dissolute" and that if the solicitation occurred in a public place, it was immaterial that it was intended to occur in a private place. The jury deadlocked and a mistrial was declared. Defendant filed a petition for writs of prohibition and mandate, as to the drug conviction and the retrial. The court issued an alternative writ of prohibition limited to the proceedings related to retrial of the § 647(a) charge. The court discharged defendant's alternative writ of prohibition and held that § 647(a) did not meet constitutional standards of specificity as interpreted by earlier decisions. However, the court construed § 647(a) to prohibit only the solicitation or commission of a sexual touching, done with specific intent when persons were offended by the act and held § 647(a) constitutional.


OUTCOME: After granting defendant an alternative writ of prohibition to decide the constitutionality of the disorderly conduct statute, the court discharged the alternative writ of prohibition and denied the preemptory writ to bar his trial for disorderly conduct. The court adopted a limited and specific construction consistent with the present function of the disorderly conduct statute.

People v. Saffell


Crim. No. 20837


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 223; 599 P.2d 92; 157 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 304



September 5, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-42183, Robert W. Conyers and Hugo M. Fisher, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction and the order committing defendant as an MDSO are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of forcible rape under Cal. Penal Code §261 and forcible sexual perversion under Cal. Penal Code § 288a (c), found both offenses were aggravated with dangerous weapon use under Cal. Penal Code § 12022 (b), and committed him to state hospital under provisions of the Mentally Disordered Sex Offenders Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6300 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of forcible rape in violation of Cal. Penal Code §261 and forcible sexual perversion in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a(c). Following his conviction, a judge of the trial court's psychiatric department found defendant could benefit by treatment in state hospital under the provisions of the Mentally Disordered Sex Offenders Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §6300 et seq. Defendant argued that if he had been sentenced as a criminal, rather than committed as an MDSO, he would have been eligible to be confined for the middle or lower term for the base criminal offense and could have received good time credits which would have, in combination, accelerated his release date. He argued that exclusion of these sentencing possibilities denied him equal protection of the law. The court held the discriminatory scheme imposed by the Act was justified under an equal protection analysis because state had a compelling interest in providing medical attention to those individuals amenable to treatment who commit sexually motivated criminal acts. The court also held that credit for good behavior available to prison inmates was not suitable in a hospital context.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and the order committing defendant as a mentally disordered sex offender because the procedures of the Mentally Disordered Sex Offender Act were justified in that there were two compelling state interests, providing effective treatment for those disposed to the commission of sexually motivated criminal acts and assuring the safety of the public.

People v. Frierson


Crim. Nos. 20624, 20882


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 142; 599 P.2d 587; 158 Cal. Rptr. 281; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 302



August 31, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A192962, Bernard Lawler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in Crim. 20624 is reversed, and the writ of habeas corpus in Crim. 20882 is granted. Defendant is remanded to the custody of the Los Angeles Superior Court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) imposing the death penalty following his conviction of first-degree murder and other lesser offenses. Defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and other lesser offenses, and then sentenced to death. On appeal, he argued that his conviction should have been reversed for a variety of reasons. He also sought a writ of habeas corpus based on his claim that he was ineffectively represented by his trial counsel. The reviewing court concluded that, while the contentions raised on defendant's appeal lacked merit, the uncontradicted facts presented in his habeas corpus pleadings disclosed that he was deprived of adequate and effective representation during both the guilt and penalty phases of his trial. Defendant's counsel, despite his admitted awareness of the possibility of developing a successful diminished capacity defense, neglected either to seek or obtain an expert appraisal of defendant's mental condition or of the effect of the drug PCP upon his physical and mental condition. This was defendant's sole defense to the serious crimes with which he was charged. As a result, the court concluded that defendant was deprived of his right to effective trial counsel, reversed his conviction, and remanded the case for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of murder and other charges, and sentencing him to death, was reversed and defendant's writ of habeas corpus was granted because defendant was deprived of his right to effective assistance of counsel in that his counsel failed to explore or present evidence on defendant's sole defense of diminished capacity.

Fair Political Practices Com v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 30904


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 33; 599 P.2d 46; 157 Cal. Rptr. 855; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 294



August 23, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied October 11, 1979. Bird, C. J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue directing respondent court to vacate its judgment and to enter judgment in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner fair political practices commission sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to vacate a judgment enjoining enforcement of the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code § 81000 et seq., an initiative measure.


OVERVIEW: The Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code § 81000 et seq., was an initiative declared invalid in its entirety by respondent trial court. Petitioner Fair Political Practices Commission sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to vacate its judgment. The court granted mandamus relief and directed respondent to enter judgment in accordance with the following views. The court held that the prohibition against lobbyist contributions in Cal. Gov't Code § 86202 was a substantial limitation on associational freedoms guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. I, and was therefore invalid. Finding that the right to petition for grievances guaranteed by U.S. Const. amend. I could not be conditioned on disclosure of private financial matters irrelevant to the petition activity, the court held that Cal. Gov't Code §§ 86107(d), (e) and 86109(d), (e) were also invalid. However, the court found that the other reporting requirements, the registration requirements, and the limitation on gifts did not constitute substantial limitations on petition and speech rights. The court also held that the initiative did not violate the single subject rule under Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d).


OUTCOME: The court found that the initiative's prohibition against lobbyist contributions and required disclosure of private financial matters irrelevant to petition activities violated the First Amendment and were therefore invalid. Upholding other portions of the initiative, the court issued a writ of mandate directing respondent trial court to vacate its judgment and to enter judgment in accordance with the court's opinion.

Longshore v. County of Ventura


L.A. No. 31090


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 14; 598 P.2d 866; 157 Cal. Rptr. 706; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 293



August 22, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified August 29, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 59215, Steven J. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed as to plaintiff Longshore and reversed as to the remaining plaintiffs. Exercising our discretion in the premises, it is further ordered that each party shall bear its own costs. (Cal. Rules of Court, rule 26(a).) The cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, retired and current deputy sheriffs, appealed a judgment from the Superior Court, Ventura County (California) which dismissed their action against defendant county for the recognition of and payment for overtime credits alleged to have been accumulated by them prior to July 1961.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, retired and current deputy sheriffs, sued defendant county for the monetary value of plaintiff retiree's accumulated pre-1961 overtime credits and for the recognition of alleged contractual obligations to the remaining plaintiffs who each held pre-1961 overtime credits. The trial court sustained defendant's general demurrer without leave to amend the complaint. Plaintiffs appealed from the judgment of dismissal. The court affirmed the judgment as to plaintiff retiree, concluding that the pre-1961 ordinances created no vested rights to cash compensation for overtime service. Notwithstanding representations by plaintiff retiree's supervisors, the court held that defendant was not estopped from asserting the forfeiture provisions of subsequent salary laws. The court reasoned that enlargement of plaintiff retiree's rights would thwart proscriptions against retroactive compensation for public employees set forth in Cal. Const. arts. IV, § 17 and XI, § 10(a). The court reversed and remanded as to the remaining plaintiffs, however, finding that their claims for declaratory relief were neither fatally uncertain nor necessarily barred by any statutes of limitations.


OUTCOME: Concluding that defendant county's pre-1961 salary ordinances created no vested rights to cash compensation for overtime service, the court affirmed judgment for defendant as to plaintiff retiree. However, holding that the remaining plaintiffs' claims were neither barred by the statute of limitations nor fatally uncertain, the court reversed the judgment as to the remaining plaintiffs and remanded for further proceedings.

People v. Belleci


Crim. No. 20604


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 879; 598 P.2d 473; 157 Cal. Rptr. 503; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 290



August 20, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 65293, William J. Fernandez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to resentence defendant in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied defendant's motion to strike those portions of his presentence report discussing evidence that had been suppressed at his trial and to resubmit the matter to the probation department for reconsideration without reference to that evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted on two counts of possession for sale of phencyclidine. Pursuant to a suppression motion, one count was suppressed on the basis that it had been obtained by illegal search and seizure. Defendant entered a plea of guilty on the other count and the matter was referred to the probation department for a presentence report. When that report was made available, it included a detailed statement of the suppressed evidence. In evaluation of the case the probation officer drew adverse inferences from defendant's possession of contraband on two separate occasions, approximately six months apart, and relied on that evidence in recommending that probation be denied and that defendant be committed instead to the California Youth Authority. The trial court agreed. Defendant contended the trial court committed prejudicial error in admitting, at the sentencing hearing, evidence that had previously been ordered suppressed. The court agreed and reversed the trial court. The court held that under the plain language of Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(d), the illegally seized evidence subject to the suppression motion was not admissible at the sentencing hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment with directions to resentence defendant. The evidence that was the subject of a final order granting defendant's pretrial motion to suppress such evidence was rendered inadmissible at defendant's sentencing hearing by the terms of the statute that governed the admissibility of evidence. It should have been stricken from the presentence report.

Egan v. Mut. of Omaha Ins. Co.


Docket No. L.A. 30747.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


24 Cal. 3d 809; 598 P.2d 452; 157 Cal. Rptr. 482; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 355



August 14, 1979, Decided

 

325 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Egan v. Mut. of Omaha Ins. Co.


L.A. No. 30747


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 809; 620 P.2d 141; 169 Cal. Rptr. 691; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 286



August 14, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of appellant Mutual of Omaha and respondent for a rehearing were denied October 11, 1979. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. EAC 15054, Howard D. McClain, Judge.



DISPOSITION: (See fn. 7.) The judgment against Segal and McEachen is reversed. The judgment against Mutual is affirmed as to the award of compensatory damages 7 and reversed as to the award of punitive damages. Segal and McEachen shall recover their costs on appeal from plaintiff. Plaintiff and Mutual shall bear their own costs on appeal.



7 Mutual also contends that the compensatory damage award should be reversed because the trial court permitted the jury to award future policy benefits. It is true that in Erreca v. Western States Life Ins. Co. (1942) 19 Cal.2d 388, 402 [121 P.2d 689, 141 A.L.R. 68], this court allowed the recovery of only accrued benefits in an action for breach of a contract for disability insurance. We have never held, however, that future policy benefits may not be recovered in a valid tort cause of action for breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing, nor does defendant offer any compelling reason for extending Erreca to such actions. Thus, in applying to these facts the general rule for fixing tort damages ( Civ. Code, § 3333), the jury may include in the compensatory damage award future policy benefits that they reasonably conclude, after examination of the policy's provisions and other evidence, the policy holder would have been entitled to receive had the contract been honored by the insurer. To the extent it is inconsistent herewith, Austero v. National Cas. Co. (1978) 84 Cal. App. 3d 1, 24-25 [148 Cal. Rptr. 653], is disapproved.

 



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, an insurer and its agents, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that awarded plaintiff insured compensatory and punitive damages for defendants' breach of a disability insurance contract based on a bad-faith failure to settle plaintiff's claim and breach of a duty of fair dealing.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff insured was covered by a disability insurance contract issued by defendant insurer. The policy provided for lifetime disability benefits if plaintiff was totally disabled because of an accident. Plaintiff made a claim for permanent benefits following an injury because he was unable to return to work. Defendant insurer's agents denied the claim and offered only three months' temporary benefits although they never consulted with plaintiff's physicians or obtained an independent medical examination. The trial court directed a verdict for plaintiff as to liability and left the issue of compensatory and punitive damages to the jury. Defendants appealed. The court reversed the verdict against defendant agents and reversed the award of punitive damages, but affirmed the compensatory damage award. The court said that defendant agents were acting solely as agents of defendant insurer and there was no independent basis for liability against them personally. Failure to properly investigate the claim constituted breach of the implied duty of good faith as a matter of law, and defendant agents' actions were properly imputed to the company.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of compensatory damages against defendant insurer because the failure to investigate plaintiff insured's claim constituted a breach of implied duty of good faith and fair dealing. An award of punitive damages was warranted by record, but the award made was reversed because it was excessive.

 

326 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Cal. Hotel & Motel Ass'n v. Indus. Welfare Comm'n


S.F. No. 23938


Supreme Court of California


25 Cal. 3d 200; 599 P.2d 31; 157 Cal. Rptr. 840; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 303; 88 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,239



August 13, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Nevada County, No. 21625, Harold F. Wolters, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment is reversed with directions to issue a writ of mandate to compel the commission to take further action in a manner consistent with this opinion within 120 days of the finality of the opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Nevada County (California), which denied its petition for writ of mandate to invalidate respondent Industrial Welfare Commission's Order 5-76. Appellant contended that the order, which fixed wages, hours, and conditions of employment in the public housekeeping industry, was invalid because respondent did not comply with the requirements of Cal. Lab. Code §§ 1177, 1178.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court rejected appellant association's assertion that the challenged order was invalid because respondent Industrial Welfare Commission did not investigate and find that wages were inadequate or that the hours or working conditions were harmful, as required by Cal. Lab. Code § 1178. The court, however, did find merit that Order 5-76 was invalid under Cal. Lab. Code § 1177 because respondent did not include an adequate statement of basis to support the order that fixed wages, hours, and working conditions for the public housekeeping industry. Accordingly, the court reversed and directed the issuance of a writ to compel respondent to take further action on the matter. The court concluded that the order's mere recitation of respondent's authority and of the procedures outlined in Cal. Lab. Code §§ 1171-1204 did not fulfill any of the functions of an effective statement of basis, noting that the requisite statement of findings was not included in the order, that the statement was not published or mailed to employers, and that the statement of findings did not address salient comments and alternatives presented during public hearings on the proposed order.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant association's petition for writ of mandate and directed the issuance of a writ to take further action, finding that the challenged order, which fixed the public housekeeping industry's wages, hours, and working conditions, was invalid because it was grounded upon an inadequate statement of basis.

 

327 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Barber v. Municipal Court


L.A. No. 30898


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 742; 598 P.2d 818; 157 Cal. Rptr. 658; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 282



August 10, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  The intrusion, through trickery, of the law enforcement agent in the confidential attorney-client conferences of petitioners cannot be condoned. The right to confer privately with one's attorney is "one of the fundamental rights guaranteed by the American criminal law -- a right that no legislature or court can ignore or violate." ( In re Rider, supra, 50 Cal. App. at p. 799.) The only effective remedy is the dismissal of the underlying charges. Therefore, it is ordered that a writ of prohibition be issued as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner suspects sought a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent Municipal Court for the San Luis Obispo County, Judicial District of San Luis Obispo County (California), from proceeding with petitioners' criminal trials following the denial of their motion to dismiss. Petitioners contended that the presence of an undercover agent at confidential attorney-client meetings had deprived them of their rights to effective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court rejected the assertion that exclusionary relief was the proper remedy in addressing the denial of petitioner suspects' motions to dismiss in the underlying criminal proceedings. The court held that the constitutional right to counsel, which embodied the right to private consultation with counsel, was violated when the undercover police officer, through his posing as a codefendant, attended petitioners' confidential attorney-client conferences. The court further noted that there was evidence that since the disclosure of the agent's undercover role, petitioners had become reluctant to cooperate fully with their attorney. The court noted that such a lack of cooperation, which resulted solely from the intrusion of the police officer in the attorney-client relationship, had resulted in counsel's inability to prepare adequately for trial. The court issued the prohibition writ requested by petitioners, concluding that dismissal of the underlying charges was warranted where the exclusionary remedy applied in the proceeding below had been inadequate to protect petitioners' rights and to deter real party in interest from such unlawful intrusions in the future.


OUTCOME: The court issued the writ of prohibition, concluding that the extraordinary relief of directing the dismissal of the underlying charges, instead of the exclusionary relief granted at the trial level, was the proper remedy where petitioner suspects' constitutional right to counsel was denied by the actions of an undercover police officer who posed as a codefendant and attended petitioners' confidential attorney-client conferences.

 

328 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. McDaniel


Crim. No. 20822


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 661; 597 P.2d 124; 156 Cal. Rptr. 865; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 275



July 19, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Madera County, No. 4388, Jack L. Hammerberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Madera County (California), which convicted defendant of a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11355.


OVERVIEW: Defendant offered to sell LSD to a police officer. Defendant denied that he showed the officer any slips of paper, or said that the substance was "bunk." Defendant claimed that he had nothing to deliver, his goal was to "rip off" would-be customers by obtaining the money and then leaving without delivering anything. Defendant's companion showed the officer slips of paper similar to those defendant showed the officer, but they did not contain LSD. The trial judge instructed the jury that an individual violated Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11355 if they offered to sell a controlled substance and then offered to deliver another substance in lieu thereof. The trial court convicted defendant pursuant to § 11355. The court reversed the decision and held that when the alleged violation consisted of an offer to sell a controlled substance and an offer to deliver another substance, the jury should have been instructed that the latter offer must have been made with the specific intent of delivering the substitute. The court held that delivery of a substance other than the particular controlled substance proffered for sale was not a necessary element of the crime defined by § 11355.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and held that when the alleged violation consisted of an offer to sell a controlled substance and an offer to deliver another substance, the jury should have been instructed that the latter offer must have been made by the defendant with the specific intent of delivering the substitute.

People v. Tanner


Crim. No. 20075


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


24 Cal. 3d 514; 596 P.2d 328; 156 Cal. Rptr. 450; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 354



June 14, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C 6054, Gerald E. Ragan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California) striking allegations, under Cal. Penal Code § 1385, which if proven would have eliminated defendant's opportunity for probation under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06 for using a firearm during a robbery.


OVERVIEW: The prosecution brought charges against defendant for robbery. Under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06, defendant would have been denied probation because he used a firearm during the robbery. The trial court, using its authority granted under Cal. Penal Code § 1385, struck the allegations that would have led to the denial of defendant's opportunity for probation. The court reversed the order of the trial court, holding that did not have the discretion to determine which defendants would receive probation. The court based its decision on the intent of the legislature. The court found that the legislature made it clear that it did not want the courts to have the discretion to decide issues of probation for crimes committed under Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order striking allegations against the defendant, which if proven could have enhanced his sentence, holding that it was not the intent of the legislature for the court's to have the power to grant probation to criminals using firearms during the commission of a robbery.

Gay Law Students Ass'n v. Pac. Tel. & Tel. Co.


S.F. No. 23625


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 458; 595 P.2d 592; 156 Cal. Rptr. 14; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 268; 19 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1419; 19 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9268



May 31, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of respondent Pacific Telephone and Telegraph Company for a rehearing was denied July 25, 1959. Clark, J., Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Rehearing Denied July 25, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 691750, Byron Arnold, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in favor of PT&T is reversed. The judgment in favor of the FEPC is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant student association challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which sustained respondent public utility's demurrer without leave to amend, denied appellant's prayer for mandate against respondent California Fair Employment Practice Commission, and entered judgment for all respondents in appellant's action alleging discrimination against homosexuals.


OVERVIEW: Appellant student association filed a class action against respondents, public utility and California Fair Employment Practice Commission (FEPC), which alleged discrimination against homosexuals and sought declaratory and injunctive relief to prevent the continuation of such practices. The trial court sustained respondent public utility's demurrer without leave to amend, denied appellant's prayer for mandate against respondent FEPC, and entered judgment for all respondents. The court reversed the judgment in favor of respondent public utility and affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent FEPC. In reversing the judgment for respondent public utility, the court held that arbitrary exclusion of qualified individuals from employment opportunities by a state-protected public utility did violate the state constitutional rights of the victims of such discrimination. In affirming denial of appellant's prayer for mandate against respondent FEPC the court held that the California Fair Employment Practice Act, Cal. Lab. Code § 1420(a), did not encompass discrimination on the basis of homosexuality, so the trial court correctly refused to grant plaintiffs' request for a writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: In appellant student association's class action alleging discrimination against homosexuals, the court reversed the judgment in favor of respondent public utility and affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent California Fair Employment Practice Commission. The arbitrary exclusion of qualified individuals from employment opportunities by a state-protected public utility did violate the state constitutional rights.

Tex-Cal Land Management v. Agric. Labor Relations Bd.


S.F. No. 23831


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 335; 595 P.2d 579; 156 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 261



May 24, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  Let a decree issue enforcing the order as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner management sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeal (California) that affirmed an order from respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board finding petitioner guilty of unfair labor practices and imposing remedial measures. Petitioner challenged the efficiency of the evidence to support the findings and the constitutionality of the standard of review under Cal. Lab. Code § 1160.8.


OVERVIEW: Union, real party in interest, claimed that petitioner management was illegally lying off workers and restricting access to its members. Respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board found petitioner guilty of unfair labor practices, ordering it to reinstate the workers illegally laid off for union activities and to post, mail, and read aloud to workers notices of their rights under the Agricultural Labor Relations Act, Cal. Lab. Code § 1140 et seq. The appellate court affirmed respondent's order and directed its enforcement. Petitioner challenged the standard of review under Cal. Lab. Code § 1160.8 as unconstitutional. Affirming the finality accorded to respondent's findings, the court found that the substantial evidence standard for reviewing the orders was an integral part of a statutory scheme that provided procedural safeguards and protected collective bargaining rights. Applying the standard to respondent's findings, the court held that substantial evidence showed that petitioner illegally laid off union workers and denied access to organizers. Finding that the remedies were proper, the court decreed that respondent's order should be enforced with minor modifications.


OUTCOME: The court issued a decree to enforce respondent labor board's order, which found petitioner management guilty of unfair labor practices and imposed remedial measures. Affirming the constitutionality of the substantial evidence standard of respondent's order, the court held that findings that petitioner illegally laid off workers and denied access to organizers of union, real party in interest, were supported and that the remedies were proper.

 

332 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kaiser Steel Corp. v. State Bd. of Equalization


L.A. No. 31058


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 188; 593 P.2d 864; 154 Cal. Rptr. 919; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 251



May 8, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C150352, William P. Hogoboom, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant manufacturer challenged a ruling of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's claim for recovery of certain sales and use taxes paid to appellee, the State Board of Equalization.


OVERVIEW: Appellant manufacturer purchased various materials including chemical elements which were processed and incorporated into appellant's steel in order to achieve a specific quality. The process also produced slag, which appellant resold. Appellant paid sales and use tax on the purchase of the materials at issue but filed a claim for a refund of such taxes and alleged that the materials were purchased for the purpose of resale. Appellant brought an action after appellee, the State Board of Equalization, refused appellant's refund claim and found that appellant purchased the materials primarily to aid in the steel manufacturing process, not for resale. The trial court agreed with appellee and affirmed that ruling. Appellant challenged the trial court ruling and, on appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that despite the fact that the process at issue produced slag which was resold, appellant's primary purpose in purchasing the materials at issue was to use those materials to remove impurities in appellant's molten steel. This was a purpose other than resale under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6007 and, therefore, the purchases were subject to sales and use tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court ruling, which denied appellant manufacturer's claim for recovery of certain sales and use taxes paid to appellee, the State Board of Equalization. The court held that where appellant purchased the materials at issue for a purpose other than resale, the transactions were subject to sales and use taxation.

Rubio v. Superior Court of San Joaquin County


S.F. No. 23711


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 93; 593 P.2d 595; 154 Cal. Rptr. 734; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 245



April 24, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied May 23, 1979. Bird, C. J., and Tobriner. J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner accused, who was charged with murder, sought a writ of prohibition to review, prior to trial, the ruling of respondent, the Superior Court of San Joaquin County, which denied petitioner's motion to quash the petit jury venire on the ground that it contained neither ex-felons nor resident aliens.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner accused, who was charged with murder, filed a motion with respondent superior court to quash the petit jury venire on the ground that it contained neither ex-felons nor resident aliens; such persons were excluded by statute from jury service. Petitioner argued that the exclusion denied him his constitutional right to be tried by a jury drawn from a representative cross-section of the community, as well as due process and equal protection of the laws. Respondent denied his motion, and petitioner sought a writ of review of that denial. The court denied the peremptory writ because it found that ex-felons and resident aliens were not cognizable groups within the meaning of the representative cross-section rule. It also held that there was no constitutional violation in excluding ex-felons from jury service because jury service was not a fundamental right, and such exclusion promoted the legitimate state goal of assuring impartiality of the verdict. The court also ruled that petitioner lacked standing to assert an equal protection challenge regarding the exclusion of resident aliens from jury service because he failed to allege that he was a resident alien.


OUTCOME: The court denied the peremptory writ because it found that petitioner accused's rights to be tried for murder by a representative cross-section of the community were not compromised by the exclusion from jury service of ex-felons and resident aliens.

San Diego Teachers Assn. v. Superior Court of San Diego County


L.A. No. 30977


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 1; 593 P.2d 838; 154 Cal. Rptr. 893; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 243; 101 L.R.R.M. 2461



April 10, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied May 10, 1979. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein. Kaus, J. * participated therein. Clark, J., Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Acting Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Gov. Code, §§ 3540- 3549.3.



DISPOSITION: The contempt orders are annulled on the ground that PERB had exclusive initial jurisdiction to determine whether the strike was an unfair practice and what, if any, remedies PERB should pursue. Our holding is limited to injunctions against strikes by public school employee organizations recognized or certified as exclusive representatives (§ 3540.1, subd. (e)).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a writ of review proceeding, petitioner teachers association sought to annul contempt orders issued by respondent superior court that punished petitioner for conducting a strike against a school district in violation of a restraining order and a preliminary injunction issued by respondent. Petitioner was the exclusive representative of the district's teachers under the Education Employment Relations Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3540-3549.3.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner teachers association and the school district in which petitioner was the exclusive representative for the district's teachers under the Education Employment Relations Act (EERA), Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3540-3549.3, filed unfair practice charges against each other with the Public Employment Relations Board (PERB). At the district's request, respondent superior court issued a restraining order and a preliminary injunction prohibiting a strike. On its own motion, respondent declared that petitioner violated the order and injunction and found it guilty of contempt. Petitioner sought a writ of review to have the contempt orders annulled. The court annulled the orders because it found that PERB had exclusive initial jurisdiction to determine whether the strike was an unfair practice and what, if any, remedies PERB should pursue. The court limited this holding to injunctions against strikes by public school employee organizations recognized or certified as exclusive representatives under EERA.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the contempt orders against petitioner teachers association because it found that respondent superior court lacked authority to issue such orders where the underlying restraining order and injunction were invalid because the school district failed to exhaust its remedies under the Education Employment Relations Act.

Metromedia, Inc. v. San Diego


L.A. No. 30782.


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 762; 592 P.2d 728; 154 Cal. Rptr. 212; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 227; 9 ELR 20437; 12 ERC (BNA) 2089



Mar. 21, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Withdrawn from the Bound Volume.
Rehg. granted May 23, 1979 (see 26 Cal.3d 848)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. 332881 and 333292, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this consolidated action, appellants, city and its representatives, challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which granted summary judgment to respondents, owners of billboards. The superior court found that appellant city's ordinance, which banned all off-site advertising billboards, was unconstitutional, and issued an injunction enjoining its enforcement.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, owners of billboards, challenged an ordinance enacted by appellants, city and its representatives, banning all off-site advertising billboards. In reversing the grant of summary judgment to respondents by the superior court, the court held that the ordinance was a valid exercise of respondents' police power in promoting traffic safety and improving the appearance of the community. The court further concluded that neither the federal nor state constitutions, nor the state's Outdoor Advertising Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5200 et seq., barred a municipality from enacting a zoning ordinance which prohibited off-site billboards and required removal of existing billboards after expiration of a reasonable amortization period. The court also held that the summary judgment could not be sustained on the grounds that city failed to comply with the California Environmental Quality Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 21151. The court remanded the case to the superior court to determine if the amortization period prescribed by the ordinance was unreasonably short as applied to some or all of respondents' structures.


OUTCOME: The court reversed summary judgment in favor of respondents billboard owners, finding that an ordinance banning off-site advertising billboards was a valid exercise of police power by respondent city and its representatives. The court found that the ordinance did not violate the First Amendment, and remanded the case to the superior court for further proceedings.
