Goldin v. Public Utilities Com.


S.F. No. 23714


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 638; 592 P.2d 289; 153 Cal. Rptr. 802; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 222



March 15, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner telephone customer sought review of a decision of respondent, Public Utilities Commission (California), which ordered that his business telephone service be terminated, claiming that the applicable rule for discontinuance of telephone service used for illegal purposes, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the applicable rule for discontinuance of telephone service used for illegal purposes, petitioner telephone customer's lines were disconnected without prior notice and hearing based on a warrant finding probable cause that they were being used for illegal purposes. Petitioner filed a complaint with respondent Public Utilities Commission challenging the rule's constitutionality. The court affirmed the decision that the rule was legal, holding that it was not a prior restraint on the right to free speech because purely commercial speech relating to illegal conduct was not protected. The court found that the rule was not unconstitutionally vague as applied because petitioner's conduct was illegal, and that the overbreadth doctrine did not apply to commercial speech. The court also held that petitioner was not denied due process because the initial termination was necessary to further an important public interest, there was a need for prompt action, and a hearing was held shortly after termination. The court found that there was ample evidence to support the warrant and that respondent's failure to review the adequacy of the showing before the magistrate was moot.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent Public Utilities Commission finding that the applicable rule for discontinuance of telephone service used for illegal purposes was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad, that termination of petitioner telephone customer's service was not a prior restraint on free speech and did not violate his rights to due process, and that the procedures for termination were valid as applied to petitioner.

People v. Privitera


Crim. No. 20340


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 697; 591 P.2d 919; 153 Cal. Rptr. 431; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 224; 5 A.L.R.4th 178



March 15, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petitions for a rehearing were denied April 12, 1979. Bird, C. J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, doctor and laypersons, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted them under Cal. Penal Code § 182 and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1707.1 of felony conspiracy to sell and prescribe the unapproved drug laetrile for the alleviation or cure of cancer, and convicted certain defendants under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1707.1 of selling laetrile for the alleviation or cure of cancer.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the convictions of defendants, doctor and laypersons, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 182 and Cal. Agins v. Tiburon


S.F. No. 23866


Supreme Court of California


24 Cal. 3d 266; 598 P.2d 25; 157 Cal. Rptr. 372; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 219; 9 ELR 20260



March 14, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied May 17, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 75873, Jack L. Blaine, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff developers challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which sustained defendant city's demurrers and dismissed plaintiffs' inverse condemnation claim and declaratory judgment claim, which alleged violations of U.S. Const. amend. V and Cal. Const. art. I, § 19. Plaintiffs argued that a zoning ordinance constituted an unconstitutional taking of their property and required compensation.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff developers claims regarding defendant city's zoning ordinance. The court first held that if a city passed a zoning ordinance in violation of U.S. Const. amend. V and Cal. Const. art. I, § 19, monetary relief was not available because of the chilling effect such a remedy would have on land use planning, but that declaratory relief to enjoin enforcement of the ordinance was an appropriate remedy. However, the court held that the zoning ordinance at issue was not unconstitutional because it did not deprive plaintiffs of substantially all reasonable use of their property without compensation as the property was zoned to allow development of at least five houses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which dismissed plaintiff developers' inverse condemnation and declaratory judgment claims against defendant city. The court held the appropriate remedy for an unconstitutional taking was declaratory judgment, not inverse condemnation, but that the zoning ordinance was not an unconstitutional taking because it did not deny substantially all reasonable use of the property.

Dew v. Appleberry


S.F. No. 23950


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 630; 591 P.2d 509; 153 Cal. Rptr. 219; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 221



March 14, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 680398, John E. Benson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's order granting summary judgment and dismissing the complaint is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challenged the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted summary judgment to defendant on the grounds that plaintiff's personal injury action was barred by the statute of limitations. Plaintiff argued the action was not barred because Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 351 tolled the statute of limitations for the five weeks that defendant was out of the state.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sought damages from defendant for injuries she sustained while on defendant's property, allegedly as the result of negligent maintenance of the premises. The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendant on the grounds that plaintiff's cause of action was barred by the statute of limitations. The court held Cal. Civ. Code § 351 tolled the statute of limitations for the five weeks defendant was out of state during the year after plaintiff was allegedly injured during a fall at defendant's home. The court rejected defendant's argument that because he was amenable to process for the entire year, § 351 did not apply. The court held that it was the legislature's decision whether to change the unambiguous statutory language, which tolled the statute of limitations because of any absence despite the availability of alternative means of serving process on a defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order, which granted summary judgment to defendant on the grounds that plaintiff's personal injury action was barred by the statute of limitations. The court held that a statute tolled the statute of limitations during the period of time that defendant was out of state, despite the fact that defendant was amenable to service of process during that time period, because the statute's language was unambiguous.

Griesel v. Dart Industries, Inc.


L.A. No. 30838


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 578; 591 P.2d 503; 153 Cal. Rptr. 213; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 217



March 8, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-54596, McIntyre Faries, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment for Dart is reversed. The appeal from the judgment of nonsuit for County of Kern and Bear Valley Springs Community Service District is deemed abandoned and accordingly is dismissed. (See fn. 1, ante.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff laborer sought review of an order of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) that entered judgment, upon a jury verdict, in favor of defendant developer in plaintiff's action for damages for personal injuries.


OVERVIEW: Defendant developer contracted with the employer of plaintiff laborer to construct a wastewater treatment plant. While plaintiff worked in an unshored trench, it caved in upon him, inflicting serious injuries. Subsequently, plaintiff filed suit against defendant developer and others. After trial, the jury deliberated for seven days. On the seventh day, one of the jurors advised the trial court that she had become too emotionally disturbed to continue. In response, the trial court ordered her discharged. Plaintiff moved for a mistrial, arguing the jury had deliberated too long for an alternate juror to become a meaningful and deliberative member of the jury. The trial court denied the motion, replaced the juror with an alternate, and instructed the jury to continue. Shortly thereafter, the jury returned a nine-to-three verdict for defendant. Plaintiff then appealed, based upon the seating of the alternate juror. On appeal, the court reversed, because the trial court erred in instructing the jury to continue with their efforts at the time the alternate was seated, and the error was prejudicial, thereby requiring reversal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that entered judgment for defendant developer because it was error to instruct the jury to continue with their efforts at the time the alternate was seated, and that error was prejudicial.

 

341 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Sonoma County Org. of Public Employees v. County of Sonoma


S.F. Nos. 23892, 23899, 23902, 23903, L.A. No. 31002


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 296; 591 P.2d 1; 152 Cal. Rptr. 903; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 201



February 15, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent local entities to pay to their officers and employees the salary increases provided in the 1978-1979 agreements without regard to the invalid restrictions contained in sections 16280 and 16280.5.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner labor organizations requested a peremptory writ of mandate from the Supreme Court of California in an action against respondents, the State, counties, and a city, to enforce a salary increase to which respondents had previously agreed. Cal. Gov't Code § 16280 prohibited the distribution of state funds to any agency that granted its employees a cost-of-living wage increase greater than the increase provided for state employees.


OVERVIEW: State funds were to be distributed to local public agencies. Cal. Gov't Code § 16280 prohibited the distribution to any agency that granted its employees a cost-of-living wage increase greater than the increase provided for state employees. Petitioner labor organizations sought enforcement of a salary increase to which respondents had previously agreed. The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate that directed payment of the increases. The court held that § 16280 impaired respondents' obligations, and there was a severe, permanent, and immediate change in petitioners' rights under the contract. Respondents failed to meet the threshold burden of establishing an emergency; therefore, the provision of § 16280 that invalidated agreements that granted cost-of-living increases was invalid as an impairment of contract, in violation of both the state and federal constitutions. The court held that § 16280 violated Cal. Const. art. XI because it interfered with respondents' right to determine compensation. Cal. Gov't Code § 16280.5 was unconstitutional insofar as it invalidated contracts between elected or appointed noncivil officers.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate that directed respondents, counties and city, to pay to petitioners, labor organizations, the agreed salary increases without regard to the statutory restrictions. Respondents failed to meet the threshold burden of establishing an emergency; therefore, the statutory provision that invalidated agreements that granted cost-of-living increases was invalid as an unconstitutional impairment of contract.

In re Lewallen


Crim. No. 20212


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 274; 590 P.2d 383; 152 Cal. Rptr. 528; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 199; 100 A.L.R.3d 823



February 9, 1979 



DISPOSITION:  The writ is granted, the judgment is vacated, and petitioner is remanded to the Municipal Court of the City and County of San Francisco for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed herein. 4

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After his conviction of drunk driving under Cal. Veh. Code § 23102(a), along with a number of weapons violations, and he refused to accept a plea negotiation and insisted on a jury trial. The jury acquitted petitioner on all but the drunk driving charge, and the trial court sentenced him to a suspended sentence of incarceration, a fine, and three years formal departmental probation with several conditions. The court granted the writ and ordered that petitioner's sentence be vacated and that he be remanded for resentencing. VI and Cal. Const. art. I, § 16.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of habeas corpus and vacated the judgment, remanding petitioner to the municipal court for resentencing. The court held that the trial judge's comments indicated that he improperly gave consideration to petitioner's refusal to accept a proffered plea bargain in determining sentence, in violation of petitioner's right to a jury trial.

 

343 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Conservatorship of Roulet


L.A. No. 30730


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 219; 590 P.2d 1; 152 Cal. Rptr. 425; 1979 Cal. LEXIS 195



February 6, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied March 29, 1979, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 105919, Arden T. Jensen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individual sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which named respondent county guardian the individual's conservator and designated the individual as gravely disabled under the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5350 et seq. The individual contended the proper standard to apply was reasonable doubt and a unanimous jury verdict.


OVERVIEW: The individual, who had been adjudicated mentally ill, sought review of the jury verdict finding her to be gravely disabled, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5350 et seq., and appointing the county guardian her conservator. The individual contended that the trial court erred in not applying the standard of reasonable doubt in making the determination whether she was gravely disabled and in not requiring a unanimous jury verdict. The court reversed and held that the commitment of a person to a mental institution against their will was a deprivation of liberty serious enough to require the highest standard of proof. Further, the court held that the destruction of an individual's personal freedoms effected by civil commitment was scarcely less total than that effected by confinement in a penitentiary. The court also held that it was constitutionally mandated that the choice of standard of proof implicated due process considerations which must be resolved by focusing not on the theoretical nature of the proceedings but rather on the actual consequences of commitment. The court also held that a unanimous jury must approve the commitment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and held that a gravely disabled finding must be supported by proof beyond a reasonable doubt and a unanimous jury as the deprivation of liberty by a civil commitment was incarceration against one's will.

 

344 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Mitchell P.


L.A. No. 30886


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 946; 587 P.2d 1144; 151 Cal. Rptr. 330; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 329



December 22, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. J 74683, John F. Ingro, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a juvenile, appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which found him to come within the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 and found defendant to be a ward of the court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found to be a ward of the court under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 after the trial court found him guilty of possession of stolen property based on the accomplice testimony of another juvenile. The court of appeal affirmed, rejecting defendant's argument that under Cal. Penal Code § 1111, he could not found to be a ward of the court based on uncorroborated accomplice testimony. Section 1111 did not apply because that section stated a defendant could not be "convicted" based on accomplice testimony, but a juvenile finding of wardship was not a conviction. There was no constitutional violation for having separate rules for juveniles and adults. The rule for accomplice testimony was created to prevent witness testimony in exchange for leniency which would sway the jury. Since juveniles did not have jury trials, this could not be a concern. If it was not unconstitutional to deny a jury trial to a juvenile, it could not have been unconstitutional to have separate rules for accomplish testimony.


OUTCOME: Judgment finding defendant to be a ward of the court affirmed. This finding could be based on uncorroborated accomplice testimony because the rule against using such testimony to convict only applied to "convictions" and a finding of wardship was not a conviction. It was not unconstitutional to apply separate standards for juveniles and adults.

People v. Tanner


Crim. No. 20075.


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 16; 587 P.2d 1112; 151 Cal. Rptr. 299; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 331



Dec. 22, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Withdrawn from the Bound Volume.
Rehg. granted Feb. 8, 1979 (see 24 Cal.3d 514)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C 6054, Gerald E. Ragan, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order from the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which struck the jury's finding that defendant used a firearm in the commission of a robbery, which permitted the trial court to place defendant on probation, and which ordered defendant incarcerated in the county jail rather than state prison.


OVERVIEW: The state contended that Cal. Penal Code § 1203.06 precluded the trial court from striking the jury's finding that defendant used a firearm in the commission of a robbery so as to place defendant on probation rather than sentence defendant to state prison. The court rejected the state's contentions, holding that the trial court had discretion to strike the use finding pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1385. The court ruled that the trial court's authority was not limited because the language of the relevant statutes did not explicitly restrict the trial court's power. The court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion because this case was extraordinary in that defendant robbed a grocery store for the purpose of convincing the store's owner to purchase defendant's security services and because the sentence imposed by the trial court included a one year term in the county jail. Accordingly, the court affirmed the holding of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court striking the jury's finding that defendant used a firearm in the commission of a robbery. The court held that the trial court was authorized to dismiss the finding and impose a sentence including probation because the relevant statutes did not explicitly limit the court's power and because the order was in the interest of justice.

 

346 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Associated Construction & Engineering Co. v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


L.A. No. 30693


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 829; 587 P.2d 684; 150 Cal. Rptr. 888; 43 Cal. Comp. Cases 1333; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 323



December 19, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  The award is annulled and the matter remanded to the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision from respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California) denying appellant's claim of credit under Cal. Lab. Code § 3861 against an award of permanent disability benefits to respondent employee.


OVERVIEW: Respondent employee contended that appellant employer's concurrent negligence barred appellant from a claim of credit against respondent employee's workers' compensation liability in the amount of respondent employee's recovery from a third party. Respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board found in favor of respondent employee. Appellant sought review, contending that the credit was permissible if the amount was reduced in accordance with appellant's comparative fault. The court agreed with appellant, finding that appellant's credit remedy was authorized under the relevant statutes, that respondent employee's third party settlement was not insulated from appellant's credit claim, and that appellant and the third party shared the burden of respondent employee's recovery. The court applied the principle that appellant, as a negligent employer, should not profit from his own wrong and held that a reduction of appellant's credit in the amount attributable to appellant's share of fault was required. Accordingly, the court annulled the award and remanded to respondent board for proceedings consistent with its ruling.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the award of workers' compensation benefits to respondent employee. The court held that appellant employer was entitled to a claim of credit against his workers' compensation liability based on respondent employee's third party settlement because appellant's negligence did not bar the claim but reduced the credit amount in proportion to appellant's share of fault.

Fox v. Los Angeles


L.A. No. 30830


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 792; 587 P.2d 663; 150 Cal. Rptr. 867; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 321



December 15, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 146131, Norman R. Dowds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is affirmed. The case is remanded for appropriate further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a city and its officials, sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted plaintiff taxpayer's request for a preliminary injunction enjoining defendants from displaying a lighted, single-barred cross on its city hall by any means whatsoever because it violated the establishment clause of Cal. Const. art. I, § 4.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a city and its officials, had authorized for 30 years the illumination on its city hall a cross to honor the Christmas holidays. Defendants had also authorized a similar display on Easter and Eastern Orthodox Easter in past years. The trial court granted plaintiff taxpayer's request for a preliminary injunction enjoining defendants from displaying the cross on its city hall. The court affirmed, finding that the display violated the establishment clause of Cal. Const. art. I, § 4. The court rejected defendants' contention that the cross constituted no more than participation in the secular aspects of the Christmas and Easter holidays, noting that illumination of only the Latin cross was preferential and did not constitute "interfaith" recognition. The court further noted that while the government could depict objects with spiritual content, it could not promote such spiritual content. The court noted that it could not conclude that defendants did not promote such spiritual content. Finally, the court found that while the mere display of the cross was clearly not a religious service, it could not be inferred that it was not action respecting an establishment of religion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order. The court found that defendants', a city and its officials, display of a cross on its city hall constituted establishment of religion in violation of the California constitution. The court noted that the display of only a Latin cross was preferential and did not constitute interfaith recognition. The court also found that defendants' display did not promote spiritual content.

 

348 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hawkins v. Superior Court


S. F. No. 23682


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 584; 586 P.2d 916; 150 Cal. Rptr. 435; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 305



November 9, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest for a rehearing was denied December 13, 1978. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to proceed in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought a writ of mandate to the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied defendants' motion for dismissal of the indictments against them or for a postindictment preliminary hearing. Defendants asserted a right under the due process and equal protection clauses of the federal and state constitutions to an adversarial preliminary hearing.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged by a grand jury indictment with conspiracy, grand theft, and other crimes. Defendants moved to dismiss the indictment, or in the alternative, for a post-indictment hearing, asserting both due process and equal protection challenges to the indictment process under the state and federal constitutions. The trial court denied the motion and defendants sought a writ of mandate. The court granted the writ, directing the trial court to hold, if demanded, a post-indictment preliminary hearing before or at the time of entering a plea. The court said that the grand jury indictment process violated the equal protection provisions of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7 because defendants indicted by grand juries had substantially less rights than those charged by information. The rights that accompanied being charged by information included the right to counsel, the right to present evidence, to cross-examine witnesses, and to learn the nature of the state's evidence. The court viewed these rights as fundamental and applied a strict scrutiny test, requiring the state to demonstrate a compelling interest that was promoted by the discriminatory classification.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing the trial court to grant defendants a post-indictment preliminary hearing because defendants indicted by grand juries enjoyed substantially fewer fundamental rights than those charged by information. The state failed to present a compelling interest which was advanced by the discriminatory deprivation of fundamental rights.

Scherling v. Superior Court


S.F. No. 23639


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 493; 585 P.2d 219; 149 Cal. Rptr. 597; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 300



October 20, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  We conclude the trial court properly denied the motion to dismiss the information. Therefore the writ of prohibition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information on the ground that the court lacked jurisdiction, from proceeding to trial on four counts of burglary on the grounds that Cal. Penal Code § 802 was unconstitutional as applied to him, and that he was deprived of his right to a speedy trial.


OVERVIEW: In 1976, the state filed an information charging defendant with four counts of burglary, committed in 1966 and 1967. The information also alleged that defendant left California in July 1969 and remained away until February 1976. Defendant moved to dismiss the information on the grounds that the court lacked jurisdiction because the three-year statute of limitations applicable to burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 800 had lapsed, and that he had been denied his right to a speedy trial. Respondent lower court denied the motion. Defendant sought a writ of prohibition to restrain respondent from proceeding to trial on the information. Defendant alleged that Cal. Penal Code § 802, which tolled the statute of limitations for the period defendant was out of state, was unconstitutional as applied to him. The court denied the writ of prohibition, holding that § 802 did not violate defendant's right to travel or his right to equal protection, because the state had an interest in assuring that one who had committed a crime remained in the jurisdiction to enhance detection of the crime. The court held defendant was not prejudiced by the delay, and was not deprived of the right to a speedy trial.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's writ of prohibition to restrain respondent lower court from proceeding to trial on an information because the state statute that tolled the statute of limitations for the period that defendant was out of state was not unconstitutional. The court held that the statute did not violate defendant's right to travel freely or his right to equal protection of the law.

CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: The trial court found that petitioner insane convict was guilty of felony hit and run driving, that he was insane at the time of the offense, and that he had not regained his sanity, and thus, ordered that he be committed in a state hospital until his sanity was restored. Petitioner sought habeas corpus relief pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1026(a). The court denied petitioner's request, because his actual confinement had not exceeded the five year maximum term of imprisonment for the offense. However, the court found that constitutional principles of equal protection required that the duration of institutional confinement of persons committed by the department of health following acquittal of criminal charges because of insanity could not exceed the maximum term for the underlying offense, unless the state or other committing authority established grounds for an extended commitment. After the expiration of such maximum term, as extended, if further confinement and treatment were sought the state had to either proceed in accordance with the civil commitment provisions of the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq., or rely upon outpatient supervision.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner insane convict's petition for habeas corpus, because the period of petitioner's actual confinement by the department of health had not yet exceeded five years; the maximum term of imprisonment for a conviction of felony hit and run driving.

353 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hale v. Morgan


S.F. No. 23641


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 388; 584 P.2d 512; 149 Cal. Rptr. 375; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 293



September 28, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of El Dorado County, No. 25905, William E. Byrne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court for retrial on the issue of the appropriate penalty only, consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landlord appealed from the decision of the Superior Court of El Dorado County (California), which assessed penalties on him under Cal. Civ. Code § 789.3 for willfully depriving plaintiff tenant of utility services.


OVERVIEW: Cal. Civ. Code § 789.3 permitted assessment of a penalty of $ 100 per day against a landlord who willfully deprived his tenant of utility services for the purpose of evicting the tenant. In a suit by plaintiff tenant, such penalties were imposed on defendant landlord, who claimed that the statute was unconstitutional. The court reversed the trial court's imposition of penalties and remanded the case for retrial on the issue of the appropriate penalty only. The court concluded that while the penalties provided by the statute survived an equal protection attack, it could violate both state and federal due process laws under the circumstances in which applied. The statutory provision permitted the assessment of arbitrary, excessive, and unreasonable penalties in excess of constitutional limits. The monthly rental for plaintiff's trailer space was $ 65, or $ 780 for a year. The penalties under the statute would have been $ 36,500 for one year. Almost one-half of this amount, or $ 17,300, was actually imposed against defendant. As such a result was wholly disproportionate to any discernible and legitimate legislative goal, it was unfair and could not be sustained.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the imposition of penalties on defendant landlord for willfully cutting off the utilities of plaintiff tenant. The court ordered a retrial as to the amount of damages only, as the amount assessed was constitutionally excessive under the circumstances.

People v. Wetmore


Crim. No. 19738


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 318; 583 P.2d 1308; 149 Cal. Rptr. 265; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 290



September 26, 1978  
September 26, 1978


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A072357, Bernard S. Selber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of commitment) is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a bench trial on the transcript of a preliminary hearing, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) found defendant not guilty of burglary by reason of insanity, and ordered his commitment for treatment. Defendant appealed the commitment order. He contended that the trial court erred in excluding evidence of his lack of specific intent to commit burglary in the guilt phase of the trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was charged with burglary, argued that psychiatric reports showed that because of mental illness he lacked the specific intent required for conviction of the crime. The trial court refused to admit psychiatric reports describing defendant's insanity as well as his diminished capacity, reasoning that such descriptions should not be admitted to prove lack of specific intent. Thereafter, the trial court found defendant guilty of second-degree burglary and, relying on the reports, it found him insane and, hence, not guilty because of insanity. At a subsequent hearing, the trial court ordered defendant committed for treatment based on its conclusion that he had not recovered his sanity. On appeal, the court reversed the order of commitment, concluding that the trial court could not properly refuse to admit evidence tending to prove that defendant was unable to entertain the requisite specific intent. Evidence of a defendant's mental illness indicating a lack of specific intent could not reasonably be ignored at the guilt trial merely because it possibly persuaded the trier of fact that defendant was insane.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's order of commitment and remanded the cause to the trial court based on its conclusion that the trial court improperly excluded evidence tending to prove defendant's inability to entertain the specific intent required for conviction of burglary in the guilt phase of the trial.

 

355 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Drew


Crim. No. 20081


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 333; 583 P.2d 1318; 149 Cal. Rptr. 275; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 291



September 26, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied November 1, 1978. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Imperial County, No. 10178, Victor A. Gillespie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded for a new trial on the issue raised by defendant's plea of not guilty by reason of insanity.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed criminal convictions entered by the Superior Court of Imperial County (California), following the jury's rejection of his insanity defense.


OVERVIEW: After being accused of battery and disturbing the peace, defendant pled not guilty by reason of insanity. The trial court instructed the jury on the insanity defense using the M'Naghten test, which required defendant to prove that he had a condition of the mind that made him incapable of understanding the nature of his act, or understanding that his act was wrong. The appellate court reversed, holding that the M'Naghten test was outdated. Instead, the court adopted the Model Penal Code's test, which permitted a finding of insanity if an individual was unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law. Here, there was evidence that while defendant had the capacity to understand the criminality of his conduct, which would not permit an insanity finding under the M'Naghten test, defendant lacked the capacity to control his behavior, which supported his insanity defense under the Model Penal Code definition.


OUTCOME: Convictions reversed, as test for insanity defense used during trial was outdated. Old test only allowed insanity finding if defendant could not understand that his act was wrong, whereas modern test also allowed finding of insanity if defendant was unable to conform his conduct to requirements of law.

 

356 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wheeler


Crim. No. 20233


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 258; 583 P.2d 748; 148 Cal. Rptr. 890; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 287



September 25, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of all the parties for a rehearing were denied October 25, 1978. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the respondent's petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A439988, William E. McGinley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of murder in the course of a robbery. The court had denied their motion for a mistrial after the prosecutor struck each potential black juror through the se of peremptory challenges.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of murdering a grocery store owner in the course of a robbery. Defendants were both black, while the man they were accused of murdering was white. The jury was all white. During voir dire, the prosecutor, over the objection of the defense attorney, struck each black from the jury using his peremptory challenges. Defendants moved for a mistrial, at which point the trial court offered the prosecutor a chance to explain the use of his challenges, which he declined to do. The trial court then denied defendants' motion. Defendants claimed they were denied their right to trial by an impartial jury. The court reversed, holding that defendants made a prima facie showing that the prosecutor was exercising peremptory challenges against black jurors on the ground of group bias alone. The trial court therefore erred in ruling that the prosecutor was not required to respond to the allegation, and in denying defendants' motions without a rebuttal showing by the prosecutor that each challenge was predicated on grounds of specific bias.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions, holding that the trial court erred in denying defendants' motion for a mistrial after the prosecution used his peremptory challenges to strike all blacks from the jury, because the prosecutor used those challenges against black jurors on the ground of group bias alone.

 

357 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Amador Valley Joint Union High Sch. Dist. v. State Bd. of Equalization


S.F. Nos. 23849, 23850, 23855


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 208; 583 P.2d 1281; 149 Cal. Rptr. 239; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 286



September 22, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  Having carefully considered them, we have concluded that article XIII A survives each of the substantial challenges raised by petitioners. The orders to show cause previously issued in these cases are discharged, and the respective petitions are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, governmental agencies and citizens, challenged the adoption of Cal. Const. art XIII A, known as Proposition 13, which imposed limitations upon the taxing powers of governments.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, governmental agencies and citizens, contested the adoption of Proposition 13, which added article XIII A to the California Constitution. Cal. Const. art. XIII A imposed limitations upon the taxing powers of state and local governments. Petitioners argued that art. XIII A represented a "revision" of the state constitution rather than a mere "amendment." The court held that art. XIII A could fairly be deemed a constitutional amendment. Specifically, the court found that art. XIII A, § 4 assisted in preserving home rule principles by leaving to local voters the decision whether or not to authorize "special" taxes to support local programs. The mere fact of reduction in local revenues did not lead to the conclusion that local agencies had forfeited control over remaining funds. The court held that the requirement of § 4, that any "special taxes" must be approved by a two-thirds vote, restricted but did not abolish the power of local governments in raising revenue. Such a requirement, standing alone and limited to the subject of taxes, did not constitute a substantial constitutional revision, which could not be accomplished through an initiative.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the orders to show cause as to whether Proposition 13 could be deemed a constitutional amendment rather than a revision.

 

358 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


San Francisco v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


S.F. No. 23338


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 103; 583 P.2d 151; 148 Cal. Rptr. 626; 43 Cal. Comp. Cases 984; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 278



September 13, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied October 20, 1978. Clark, J., Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The award of the WCAB is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review of the order of respondent board, which granted workers' compensation to respondent wife for the death of her husband.


OVERVIEW: The husband of respondent wife, a former police officer, died of a heart attack shortly after retirement. Petitioner city acknowledged that the heart attack manifested itself within five years of retirement, as required under Cal. Lab. Code § 3212.5. After respondent board made a workers' compensation award to respondent, petitioner sought to annul it because a 1959 amendment to § 3212.5 was unconstitutional. Petitioner claimed the legislature exceeded its constitutional authority under Cal. Const. art. XIV, § 4, and the amendment created an impermissible conclusive presumption in violation of due process guarantees. The court affirmed the award. Even without specific authorization, the legislature possessed the authority to adopt the disputed legislative measure. The legislature did not act arbitrarily or unconstitutionally in concluding that police officers with more than five years of experience would be given the benefit of the medical doubt and should not be precluded from obtaining workers' compensation if the only reason for finding the condition not work-related was that the employee already had the condition upon commencing employment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent board's award of workers' compensation to respondent wife over the objection of petitioner city, as the statutory provision under which the award was made was constitutional.

 

359 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. King


Crim. No. 20380


Supreme Court of California


22 Cal. 3d 12; 582 P.2d 1000; 148 Cal. Rptr. 409; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 273



August 29, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 61616, William J. Fernandez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: (See fn. 7.) The judgment is reversed. 7



7 Defendant filed an original petition for writ of habeas corpus in the Court of Appeal in conjunction with his appeal, claiming that he had not received constitutionally adequate representation by trial counsel. The Court of Appeal ordered the petition consolidated with the appeal and denied the writ in its order affirming the judgment. Our order granting the petition for hearing directed that the petition for writ of habeas corpus also be transferred to this court. It now appears, however, that the Court of Appeal did not issue an order to show cause. Therefore, the order denying the writ became final immediately after filing ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 24(a)), and the petition for hearing was untimely as to the petition for writ of habeas corpus. ( Cal. Rules of Court, rule 28(b).) Since the order of the Court of Appeal denying the petition for writ of habeas corpus in In re King (1 Crim. 17278) had become final prior to our transfer of the consolidated proceeding to this court (rule 28(a)), that order was unaffected by the granting of the hearing. No further action need be taken in the habeas corpus matter, which in any event is rendered moot by the reversal of the judgment.

 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which found him guilty for being in possession of a concealable weapon in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the jury's verdict finding him guilty of violating Cal. Penal Code § 12021, contending that the trial court erred in refusing his request to instruct the jury on self-defense. The court reversed defendant's conviction, because it was error to refuse to give instructions on self-defense, and the omission denied defendant his right to have the jury consider issues material to his guilt. There was no dispute that defendant had control of a concealable firearm, and he was the person who used it. Inasmuch as defendant's brief use of a concealable firearm, without pre-design or prior possession of the weapon, in the exercise of the right to self-defense, defense of others, or defense of a habitation would not constitute the possession, custody, or control of the firearm under § 12021, it was error for the trial court to fail to instruct the jury regarding the relevance of these defenses to the § 12021 charge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a concealable weapon, as the trial court erred in failing to give a jury instruction on the right to use such a weapon in self-defense.

 

360 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Cooper v. Bray


L.A. No. 30865


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 841; 582 P.2d 604; 148 Cal. Rptr. 148; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 265



August 16, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. WEC 24498, Delbert E. Wong, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. With respect to the appeal of defendants Bray and Colonial Oil, plaintiff shall recover her costs on appeal from those defendants. With respect to the appeal of plaintiff, defendant Tashma shall recover her costs on appeal from plaintiff.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, employee driver and employer, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) that allowed respondent owner-passenger to proceed on principles of ordinary negligence in her action against them for injuries received in auto accident. Appellants asserted that Cal. Veh. Code § 17158, which barred recovery under ordinary negligence for owner-passengers, was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed a judgment that found that Cal. Veh. Code § 17158, which prohibited owner-passengers from obtaining recovery from drivers who negligently caused their injuries, violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. Appellant gas station employee was driving respondent owner's car, with respondent as a passenger, when he caused an accident that injured respondent. The court agreed that the trial court correctly instructed the jury as to principles of ordinary negligence to determine liability, rather than the willful misconduct requirements of § 17158. The court found that the classification of § 17158, which singled out owner-passengers, was not rationally related to a conceivable legislative purpose. Examining legislative history, the court found that the intent of the provision was not to impose special burdens on owner-passengers but rather to place such owner-passengers on an equal plane with most other injured automobile passengers. The court rejected appellants' suggestions regarding alternative legislative purposes and affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that allowed respondent owner-passenger to proceed under a theory of ordinary negligence. The court found that the statute, which singled out owner-passengers as the one class of auto accident victims who could not recover from drivers who negligently caused their injuries, violated equal protection clauses because it was not rationally related to a conceivable legislative purpose.

 

361 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Ofsevit v. Trustees of California State University & Colleges


S.F. No. 23698


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 763; 582 P.2d 88; 148 Cal. Rptr. 1; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 260



August 1, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 668996, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant trustees appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), finding defendants denied plaintiff the benefit of university rules and regulations and violated his U.S. Const. Amend. I rights when they refused to reappoint him to his teaching position.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was a lecturer at defendant university for three years, but was not reappointed when defendant university decided to continue its graduate program. Plaintiff was recommended for reappointment by both the hiring committee and the grievance committee, but defendants refused to rehire him. Plaintiff brought suit seeking reinstatement and damages. The trial court found defendant trustees denied plaintiff the benefit of university rules and regulations and violated his U.S. Const. amend. I rights. Plaintiff was ordered reinstated with backpay. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment for two reasons. First, defendant trustees denied plaintiff the benefit of the university rules and regulations, and he was entitled to relief in order to enforce the binding decision of the chancellor's review committee under the university grievance procedures. Second, substantial evidence showed plaintiff's reappointment was denied due to his outspoken political alignments, and the action violated his U.S. Const. amend. I. Three, plaintiff was entitled to immediate reinstatement and to back pay and benefits from the date of the denial of reappointment, to the date he was reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgement for plaintiff on the grounds that defendant trustees denied him the benefit of the university rules and regulations which he was entitled to, and substantial evidence showed plaintiff's reappointment was denied due to his outspoken political alignments which violated his U.S. Const. amend. I rights.

 

362 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Merco Constr. Engineers, Inc. v. Municipal Court


L.A. No. 30825


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 724; 581 P.2d 636; 147 Cal. Rptr. 631; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 257



July 25, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 155293, Arthur K. Marshall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) denying a petition for writ of mandate directing respondent municipal court to allow petitioner to appear in a civil action through a corporate officer who was not an attorney. Petitioner contended that Cal. Civ. Proc. § 90 authorized such an appearance by a corporate officer in municipal courts.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner corporation appealed from a trial court judgment denying its petition for writ of mandate directing respondent municipal court to allow petitioner to appear in a civil action through a corporate officer who was not an attorney. Petitioner argued that Cal. Civ. Proc. § 90 authorized such an appearance in municipal courts. The court affirmed the trial court judgment against petitioner corporation, finding that the legislature lacked authority to vest in a person not licensed to practice law the right to appear in a court of record on behalf of another person, including a corporate entity. The judiciary, under the separation of powers of Cal. Const. art. III, § 3, dealt with bar admission, practice, and discipline. Therefore, a corporation was not able to represent itself either in propria persona or through an officer or agent who was not an attorney. The court noted that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 117.4 allowed corporations to be represented by non-bar members in small claims courts, but that small claims court practice was not extended by common law or the legislature beyond the small claims court venue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment against petitioner corporation, holding that the legislature could not have constitutionally vested in a person not licensed to practice law the right to appear in a court of record on behalf of another person, including a corporate entity. The statute allowing the limited representation was rejected as offending the separation of powers clause of the California Constitution.

 

363 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Regents of Univ. of Cal. v. Hartford Acci. & Indem. Co.


S.F. No. 23522


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 624; 581 P.2d 197; 147 Cal. Rptr. 486; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 253



July 13, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 451709-8, Leonard J. Dieden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, owner of a married student apartment project, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), granting summary judgment for defendant, as surety, in an action for damages due to latent construction defects caused by the architect that designed plaintiff's apartment building.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, owner of a married student apartment project, sought damages because of latent construction defects from the architect who designed the project, the general contractor, and defendant, as surety, on the contractor's performance bond. The trial court entered summary judgment for defendant based on the 10-year limitation of Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 337.15. The supreme court held that defendant could not benefit from § 337.15. Direct resort to the protection of § 337.15 failed because defendant was not among the parties protected. Indirect reliance upon § 337.15 failed because under the California law of suretyship the running of the statute of limitations on the principal obligation did not exonerate the surety. Finally, the terms of the surety bond did not state that defendant's liability terminated when the statute of limitations had run on the principal obligation.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment in favor of defendant, as surety, was reversed because the code of civil procedure did not bar plaintiff's claim against defendant; the surety bond did not indicate that defendant's liability terminated when the statute of limitations had run on the principal obligation.

Rice v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Bd.


S.F. Nos. 23631, 23632


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 431; 579 P.2d 476; 146 Cal. Rptr. 585; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 238; 96 A.L.R.3d 613; 1978-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P62,054



May 30, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's Application for a Rehearing in No. 23631 was Denied June 29, 1978. Bird, C. J., and Manuel, J., did not Participate therein.



DISPOSITION: The order of the board in Rice v. Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board is affirmed. The writ of review filed by Young's Market is discharged and that petition is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control, sought annulment of an order of respondent, Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board (California), reversing petitioner's decision to suspend the liquor license of the real parties in interest who sold alcoholic beverages for less than the posted price. Respondent determined that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755 was invalid under the Sherman Antitrust Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1 et seq.


OVERVIEW:  Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 24755, the retail price maintenance law, required that a manufacturer or brand owner file with petitioner, the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control, a minimum price schedule for distilled spirits which bore the brand name of the owner and prohibited an off-sale retail licensee from selling at less than that prescribed price. The real parties in interest sold alcoholic beverages for less than the posted price. After a hearing, petitioner suspended the real parties' retail license for ten days. On appeal to respondent, the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, respondent ruled that § 24755 was invalid as a violation of the Sherman Antitrust Act (Sherman Act), 15 U.S.C.S. § 1 et seq. The court agreed with respondent that price fixing in the state had resulted in the elimination of any semblance of competition within the industry. In affirming respondent's decision, the court ruled that the price maintenance policies of § 24755 were in conflict with the Sherman Act, and that the policies of the Sherman Act prevailed. The court held that § 24755 was therefore invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the an order of respondent, the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board, reversing the suspension of the liquor license of the real parties in interest and rejected the request of petitioner, the Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control, to annul the order. The court ruled that the liquor price maintenance law violated the policies underlying the Sherman Antitrust Act and was, therefore, invalid.

 

365 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Safeway Stores, Inc. v. Nest-Kart


S.F. No. 23596


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 322; 579 P.2d 441; 146 Cal. Rptr. 550; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 233



May 26, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 284768, Edward A. Panelli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court order, apportioning liability on a pro rata basis in contravention of the jury's special finding on the comparative fault issue, is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant cart manufacturer challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which apportioned liability in a judgment pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 875 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 876, between appellant and respondent corporation on a pro rata basis in contravention of the jury finding of comparative fault.


OVERVIEW: Appellant cart manufacturer and respondent corporation were defendants in a strict product liability and negligence action in which the trial court entered a judgment against them based on a jury's finding of comparative fault on a 20-80 percent basis, respectively. Respondent sought contribution from appellant for an additional 30 percent of the judgment, so as to achieve an equal apportionment between them. The trial court, acting pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 875, 876, granted respondent's motion and ordered a 50-50 percentage liability of the parties. On review sought by appellant, the court reversed the trial court's order, which apportioned liability on a pro rata basis in contravention of the jury's special finding on the comparative fault issue. The court held that the trial court's decision was based on its belief that apportionment on a comparative fault basis was not possible. The court held that the comparative indemnity doctrine could be utilized to allocate liability between a negligent and a strictly liable defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order apportioning liability on a pro rata basis in contravention of the jury's special finding on the comparative fault issue because the comparative indemnity doctrine could be utilized to allocate liability between a negligent and a strictly liable defendant.

Jara v. Municipal Court for San Antonio Judicial Dist.


L.A. No. 30788


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 181; 578 P.2d 94; 145 Cal. Rptr. 847; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 221



May 2, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 132127, Arthur K. Marshall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision from respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate or prohibition to compel respondent municipal court to appoint an interpreter in a civil action.


OVERVIEW: A property damage action arising from an automobile accident was filed against appellant in respondent municipal court. Represented by an attorney, appellant filed an answer and cross-complaint. Alleging indigency and inability to speak or understand English, appellant requested that the court appoint an interpreter skilled in English and Spanish. The court denied the request. Appellant then filed a writ. Respondent superior court concluded that appellant did not have a common law right to an interpreter, and that refusal to appoint an interpreter for an indigent litigant in a civil case did not constitute abuse of discretion or denial of either due process or equal protection of the law. Affirming that decision on appeal, the court held that language assistance to indigent but represented litigants was available and the court was satisfied that there was no need to require appointment of interpreters at public expense to assist litigants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent superior court's judgment because language assistance to indigent but represented litigants was available and consequently there was no need to require appointment of interpreters at public expense to assist litigants.

People v. Fogelson


Crim. No. 19823


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 158; 577 P.2d 677; 145 Cal. Rptr. 542; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 219



April 26, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Los Angeles Judicial District of Los Angeles County, Nos. 807628, 807462, Sidney A. Cherniss, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individual sought review of an order of the Municipal Court, Los Angeles Judicial District, Los Angeles County (California) that entered judgment convicting appellant of a misdemeanor, soliciting contributions on public property without a permit, conduct which violated Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 42.14.1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant individual belonged to a religious sect, the tenets of which required its adherents to go to public places to distribute books and pamphlets containing the teachings of the faith. Appellant was arrested as he offered books to persons passing through an airport. Subsequently, appellant was convicted in municipal court of violating Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 42.14.1, which required a permit for solicitation in public areas. On review, the appellate department reversed the conviction. Thereafter, the court of appeal transferred the case to itself on its own motion and affirmed the conviction. Appellant then sought further review, and the court reversed appellant's conviction, holding § 42.14.1 was unconstitutional on its face because it gave municipal officials unbridled power to prohibit constitutionally protected forms of solicitation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of conviction because the ordinance under which appellant individual was prosecuted gave administrative officials unlimited discretion to grant or deny permission to engage in constitutionally protected forms of solicitation.

 

368 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Marasco v. Wadsworth


L.A. No. 30735


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 82; 578 P.2d 90; 145 Cal. Rptr. 843; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 212



April 26, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SO C 29730, Max Z. Wisot, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to overrule the demurrer and allow time for defendant to answer.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the granting of defendant driver's demurrer in the wrongful death suit she brought in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). Defendant argued the statute of limitations barred the action, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340(3). Plaintiff had amended her complaint to name defendant following the invalidation of the guest statute, Cal. Veh. Code § 17158.


OVERVIEW: When plaintiff timely filed her suit for wrongful death from a three-car accident, she named a number of defendants fictitiously pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 474. After the California Supreme Court decided the guest statute, Cal. Veh. Code § 17158, was unconstitutional, plaintiff amended her complaint to name defendant driver. The court sustained defendant's demurrer citing the statute of limitations, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340(3). The court reversed, explaining that substituting defendant related back to the filing date of the original complaint. The court pointed out that the amended complaint substituting defendant involved the identical accident and the identical injuries presented in the original complaint. Rejecting defendant's argument concerning plaintiff's knowledge of her identity when the original complaint was filed , the court explained that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 474 was not interpreted literally, but also included situations when plaintiff knew the defendant and the facts, but was unaware that the law gave him a cause of action and discovered that right by reason of decisions rendered after the suit commenced.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment sustaining defendant driver's demurrer citing the statute of limitations in the amendment to the complaint plaintiff brought for wrongful death in a three-car accident. While the case was pending, the California Supreme Court declared unconstitutional the guest statute that had barred the claim against defendant. The court explained the amendment related back.
