Schmitz v. Younger


L.A. No. 30844


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 90; 577 P.2d 652; 145 Cal. Rptr. 517; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 213



April 26, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 25, 1978. Manuel, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizen sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent attorney general to title and prepare a summary of a proposed initiative measure.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner citizen submitted a proposed initiative measure to respondent attorney general for title and summary preparatory to obtaining signatures to qualify the measure for the ballot. The measure would have made it unlawful for any teacher to strike, prohibit campaign contributions by teachers' organizations, and prevent tax revenues from being used to provide transportation for purpose of racially balancing public schools. Respondent refused to issue the title on the ground that the proposed measure concerned more than one subject and thus violated Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d). The court let the preemptory writ issue as prayed because without prior judicial authorization, respondent could not delay or impede the initiative process while claims of the measure's invalidity were determined. Petitioner was entitled to have the proposal titled and summarized so that he could commence seeking signatures to qualify it for the ballot.


OUTCOME: Petitioner citizen's preemptory writ of mandate was issued as prayed because respondent attorney general could not delay the initiative process despite his claim that the initiative violated the single-subject rule.

 

370 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Younger v. Superior Court


S.F. Nos. 23517, 23597


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 102; 577 P.2d 1014; 145 Cal. Rptr. 674; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 214



April 26, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On May 25, 1978, the Judgment was Modified to Read as Printed Above.



DISPOSITION: In S.F. 23517, let a peremptory writ of mandate issue requiring respondeat superior court to vacate its order for destruction of records. In S.F. 23597, let a peremptory writ of mandate issue requiring respondent Attorney General to accept and act upon Mack's application for destruction of records in conformity with this opinion. In S.F. 23597 we reserve jurisdiction to determine Mack's motion for an award of attorneys' fees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, petitioner, a convicted offender, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent attorney general to destroy records pertaining to a prior conviction for marijuana possession. The attorney general also filed for a writ of mandate that sought to compel respondent court to vacate an order requiring destruction of such records.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner criminal, who was previously convicted for marijuana possession, filed a writ of mandate to compel destruction of the criminal records by respondent attorney general under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11361.5(b) (1976). In a consolidated action, petitioner attorney general sought a writ of mandate for respondent court to vacate its order for destruction of those records. The court granted both writs, concluding that the matter was controlled by § 11361.5, which replaced a prior statutory provision with the same section number. Because respondent court's order to destroy the records was not final when the new statutory provision was enacted, respondent court lacked jurisdiction to enforce its order, which was entered under the prior statutory provision. Thus, a writ of mandate was issued to vacate the order of respondent court. Petitioner criminal had filed a letter application for destruction of records, because respondent attorney general had not yet issued a new application form under the new statute. As a result, petitioner criminal lacked an adequate remedy at law, warranting grant of a writ compelling destruction of the records pursuant to his request.


OUTCOME: In the action by petitioner attorney general, the court granted a writ of mandate, requiring respondent court to vacate an order for destruction of criminal records, as respondent court no longer had jurisdiction over the matter. In a consolidated case, the court granted a writ of mandate to petitioner criminal to compel destruction of the records by respondent attorney general, as there was no adequate remedy at law.

 

371 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Boerner v. Colwell Co.


L.A. No. 30778


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 37; 577 P.2d 200; 145 Cal. Rptr. 380; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 210



April 24, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 24, 1978. Mosk, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA 32666, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, improvement purchasers, challenged the order originally issued by the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of defendant lender that the contracts between plaintiffs and building contractors that were assigned to defendant were bona fide credit sales and did not constitute loans subject to the usury laws.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, improvement purchasers, entered into contracts with building contractors that were then assigned to defendant lender. The trial court found that the contracts constituted bona fide credit sales, and the transactions did not constitute loans subject to the usury laws. The court affirmed and held that the mere participation by a nonexempt financing institution in a transaction for the sale of improvements did not operate to convert what was otherwise regarded as a bona fide credit sale into a "loan" subject to the usury laws. The court held that where the time sale price exceeded the cash sales price, and the difference amounted to more than the legal rate of interest, it did not follow that the transaction was usurious, but that other considerations than interest were properly involved, such as the risk incident to financing the contract and expenses connected therewith. The court held that defendant's participation in the shaping of the contracting parties' bargain did not operate to convert the resulting transactions, including the contemplated assignment, into usurious loans.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that held that contracts between plaintiffs, improvement purchasers, and building contractors, which were assigned to defendant lender in order to obtain financing, were bona fide credit sales and did not constitute loans subject to the usury laws. The mere participation by a nonexempt financing institution in a transaction did not convert a bona fide credit sale into a "loan" subject to the usury laws.

 

372 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Isbell v. County of Sonoma


S.F. No. 23604


Supreme Court of California


21 Cal. 3d 61; 577 P.2d 188; 145 Cal. Rptr. 368; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 211



April 24, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 15, 1978, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Clark, J., Richardson, J., and Manuel, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 78372, Joseph P. Murphy, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded with directions to enter a declaratory judgment in favor of plaintiffs determining that the confession of judgment procedure of Code of Civil Procedure sections 1132, subdivision (a), 1133, and 1134 does not conform to the due process standards of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution, and further determining that the judgment entered against plaintiff Isbell on January 27, 1967 and the judgment entered against plaintiffs Pearson on February 16, 1966, are void. 9



9 We hold here only that sections 1132, subdivision (a), 1133, and 1134 of the Code of Civil Procedure enact an unconstitutional procedure. The validity of judgments entered pursuant to stipulation or agreement under the authority of other statutes or rules of court is not before us in the present case, and we do not imply that such judgments are void.

 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff debtors appealed a declaratory judgment entered in the Superior Court, Sonoma County (California), which held that confessions of judgment obtained in conformity with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1132 and 1134 did not violate due process and, consequently, that the judgments rendered to defendant county against plaintiffs were valid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff debtors filed suit against defendant county seeking a declaration that the judgments rendered against them based upon signed confessions of judgment were void. The trial court entered judgment for defendant holding that a confession of judgment obtained in conformity with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1132 and 1134 did not violate due process. Plaintiffs appealed. The court held that a judgment based solely upon an executed confession was constitutionally defective because it violated due process rights. The relevant statutes did not permit a prejudgment judicial determination of whether the debtor had voluntarily, knowingly, and intelligently waived his due process rights. Thus, the judgments obtained against plaintiffs were unconstitutional. The ability to seek postjudgment relief did not cure the unconstitutionality of the judgments. Accordingly, the judgment was reversed and the cause remanded, with directions to enter a declaratory judgment for plaintiffs determining that the confession judgment procedure did not conform to the due process standards of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and to determine that the judgments entered against plaintiffs were void.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the declaratory judgment for defendant county and remanded the cause with directions to enter a declaratory judgment for plaintiff debtors determining that the state's confession of judgment procedure was unconstitutional because it violated a debtor's due process rights. The trial court was further directed to enter a judgment finding the judgments entered against plaintiffs in conformity with such procedure were void.

 

373 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Britt v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 30786


Supreme Court of California


20 Cal. 3d 844; 574 P.2d 766; 143 Cal. Rptr. 695; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 204



March 27, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the court (1) to vacate its discovery order with respect to defendant's inquiries into plaintiffs' private associational affiliations and activities and plaintiffs' lifetime medical histories and (2) to proceed in accordance with the views expressed herein.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners appealed the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which held that defendant airport owner's request for discovery of plaintiffs' private associational information did not violate plaintiffs' rights under U.S. Const. amend. I, in plaintiffs' action for diminution of property values, personal injuries, and emotional disturbance caused by the operation of defendant's airport.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff homeowners sought damages from defendant airport owner for diminution of property values, personal injuries, and emotional disturbance caused by the operation of defendant's airport. The court reversed defendant trial court's order compelling discovery of plaintiffs' private associational information. The court held that the discovery order was not justified by a compelling state interest and was not narrowly tailored and thus infringed upon plaintiffs' U.S. Const. amend. I rights. The court held that plaintiffs were entitled to associational privacy rights even though plaintiffs were not members of unpopular groups. The court found that plaintiffs did not waive all privacy rights by filing suit and that the discovery order was unconstitutionally overbroad because plaintiffs did not put into issue any aspect of their associational conduct. The court found that plaintiffs were not required to disclose lifetime medical histories under Cal. Evid. Code §§ 990 et seq., 1010 et seq. On remand, defendant airport owner would have to pose narrow interrogatories with respect to the mental conditions put into issue under Cal. Evid. Code § 1016.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of defendant trial court, which granted defendant airport owner's motion to compel discovery of plaintiffs' private associational information and medical histories. The court held that the discovery order violated plaintiffs' First Amendment rights because it was not justified by a compelling state interest and was not narrowly tailored. On remand, the discovery could not impinge on privileged information.

 

374 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Municipal Court for Pasadena Judicial Dist.


L.A. No. 30722


Supreme Court of California


20 Cal. 3d 523; 574 P.2d 425; 143 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 184; 2 A.L.R.4th 681



January 20, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real party in interest and respondent for a rehearing was denied March 9, 1978. Bird, C. J., and Tobriner, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C123383, John W. Holmes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the superior court with directions to issue the peremptory writ.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a judgment rendered by the Superior Court (California) that denied its writ of prohibition to stop respondent Municipal Court for the Pasadena Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) from allowing the real party in interest to depose a prosecution witness.


OVERVIEW: The state filed a complaint in municipal court charging real party in interest with drunk driving, obstructing a public officer, and vandalism. Real party's counsel filed a pretrial discovery motion seeking to depose the police officers involved in the various charges. Real party's motion was granted by respondent municipal court, despite objection by the state that the statutory conditions for invoking deposition procedure had not been met. The state unsuccessfully sought a writ of prohibition in superior court. The state appealed denial of the writ. Under Cal. Penal Code § 1335 et seq., a defendant could be permitted to depose a witness prior to trial if it could be established that the witness was about to leave the state or it was unlikely that the witness could attend trial. The real party did not plead such circumstances in his motion to depose the witnesses. The reviewing court concluded that respondent did not have the inherent power to allow the witness in a criminal case to be deposed unless the conditions were as stated in § 1335 et seq. Therefore, the reviewing court reversed respondent's judgment and remanded to allow a peremptory writ to issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that denied the state's writ to prohibit respondent municipal court from allowing real party in interest to depose a prosecution witness. Respondent did not have inherent power to allow the witness to be deposed if witness was not sick or unavailable. The court remanded with instructions to issue a peremptory writ.

 

375 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Teron


Crim. No. 20438.


Supreme Court of California


23 Cal. 3d 103; 588 P.2d 773; 151 Cal. Rptr. 633; 1978 Cal. LEXIS 336



January 11, 1978.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondent's petition for a rehearing was denied February 8, 1979. Clark, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-39264, Kenneth E. Lae, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment against defendant is modified to provide for a sentence of life imprisonment and, as so modified, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction of first-degree murder and death sentence from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), arguing that the trial court erred in permitting defendant to represent himself.


OVERVIEW: After ruling that defendant was competent to waive counsel, the trial court permitted defendant to represent himself at his trial on the charge of first-degree murder. Defendant did not question witnesses or present evidence or argument on his own behalf. Thereafter, the trial court convicted defendant as charged and sentenced him to death. Upon an automatic appeal, the court affirmed the judgment as modified to provide for a sentence of life imprisonment. Based on the record, the court concluded that the trial court properly ruled that defendant was competent to waive counsel and, thus, to represent himself. Defendant was not obligated to present a vigorous defense and had the right to decide not to oppose the prosecution's case. The sentence, however, could not stand because the state sought to impose the death penalty under a statute effective after the date the crime occurred. Because the constitutionally valid provisions in effect at the time of the commission of the crime did not provide for the death penalty, the statute that was intended to increase the punishment for first-degree murder did not apply retroactively.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment as modified to impose a sentence of life imprisonment. Death penalty legislation that increased the punishment for the crime of first-degree murder did not apply to defendant's offense that was committed prior to the statute's effective date.

Varjabedian v. Madera


S.F. No. 23592


Supreme Court of California


20 Cal. 3d 285; 572 P.2d 43; 142 Cal. Rptr. 429; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 195



December 9, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the defendant and appellant for a rehearing was denied January 5, 1978. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Madera County, No. 18659, Dean C. Lauritzen, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is amended by adding thereto a paragraph dismissing the fourth cause of action of the complaint (inverse condemnation) and awarding judgment thereon to defendant. The portion of the judgment thus added is reversed. The remainder of the judgment is affirmed. Plaintiffs shall recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant municipality appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Madera County (California), awarding plaintiff property owners damages on their nuisance claim, while plaintiffs appealed the court's order dismissing their inverse condemnation claim.


OVERVIEW: Defendant municipality appealed from a judgment awarding plaintiff property owners compensation for the nuisance caused by defendant's construction of a sewage treatment plant near plaintiffs' property. Plaintiffs cross-appealed the dismissal of their inverse condemnation claim. The court affirmed the nuisance judgment, including the award of damages, and reversed the rejection of the inverse condemnation claim, remanding the case for further proceedings. The statutory authority for municipalities to build sewage treatment plants did not mean that neighboring property owners could not pursue litigation due to the resultant odors emitted. Sufficient evidence existed to support the diminution-in-value, discomfort, and loss-of-loan-benefits damage awards. An erroneous jury instruction on nuisance damages was harmless, and plaintiffs were entitled to try and prove that the plant's effect on their enjoyment of their property was so invasive as to constitute a constructive taking.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded. Plaintiff property owners were correctly awarded damages on their nuisance claim, because that claim was supported by sufficient evidence and was not legally precluded. Plaintiffs' inverse condemnation claim was improperly dismissed, because plaintiffs were entitled to try and prove a constructive taking.

Sanchez v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Board


L.A. No. 30690


Supreme Court of California


20 Cal. 3d 55; 569 P.2d 740; 141 Cal. Rptr. 146; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 169



October 5, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 121346, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to issue a peremptory writ of mandate commanding respondent to set aside its decision and to reconsider the case in light of this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which denied appellant's application for a writ of mandate to compel respondent California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board to set aside its determination that appellant was ineligible for unemployment insurance benefits for the reason that appellant was not available for work pursuant to Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1253(c).


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimant was terminated from a waitress job after a work scheduling dispute, and appellant applied for unemployment insurance benefits. Respondent California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board affirmed a hearing referee's decision finding appellant ineligible for benefits. The trial court denied appellant's request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent board to set aside its decision, and appellant sought review. The court reversed, holding that the trial court erred in deciding under Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1253(c) that appellant was unavailable for work without finding that weekend work was suitable work that appellant lacked good cause to refuse. The case was remanded for respondent board to reconsider the claim under the correct standard. The court found that a claimant who was a parent had good cause for refusing employment that conflicts with parental duties reasonably necessary for the care of a minor child. The court held that once a claimant established availability for suitable work, the burden of proof to demonstrate whether the claimant was available to a substantial field of employment lay with the department of employment development.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and directed the issuance of a writ of mandate commanding respondent California Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board to reconsider appellant claimant's eligibility for unemployment insurance benefits, which involved a determination of whether weekend work was suitable work that appellant lacked good cause to refuse, and whether claimant made herself available to a substantial field of employment.

Supreme Court of California


20 Cal. 3d 25; 569 P.2d 1303; 141 Cal. Rptr. 315; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 168; 7 ELR 20795



October 4, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the defendants and appellants for a rehearing was denied November 17, 1977, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Bird, C. J., and Manuel, J., did not participate therein. Sullivan, J., * and Wright, J., + participated therein. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Clark, J., did not concur in the modification.


* Retired Associate Justice of the Supreme Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


+ Retired Chief Justice of California sitting under assignment by the Acting Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 938254, Bernard S. Jefferson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order concerning attorneys' fees filed August 1, 1975 is affirmed. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to hear and determine plaintiffs' motions for attorneys' fees filed in this court on January 28, 1977, July 7, 1977, and October 31, 1977, in conformity with the views herein expressed and to make and enter all necessary and appropriate orders.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state officials, and respondents, public interest groups, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded attorney fees in favor of respondents regarding respondents' successful lawsuit that held California's public school system in violation of California's constitution.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, public interest groups, had previously successfully sued the state, which resulted in the court finding that the public school financing system was unconstitutional. Respondents filed their first of several motions for attorney fees. Based upon its equitable powers, the trial court awarded attorney fees to respondents' counsel based on the private attorney general theory. Appellants, state officials, and respondents, respectfully, sought review of the award and amount of the attorney fees. The court adopted the private attorney general theory for California as it applied to vindication of a strong state constitutional public policy. The court affirmed the award of attorney fees, articulating that the private attorney general theory encouraged suits effectuating a strong public policy by awarding substantial attorney fees to those who successfully brought such suits and thereby brought about benefits to a broad class of citizens. The court withheld judgment on the issue of whether the private attorney general theory could be applied to a strong statutory policy. The court remanded for the trial court to determine respondents' remaining motions for attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award and amount of respondent's attorney fees under the private attorney general theory because the previous litigation vindicated a strong constitutional public policy and the result of the litigation benefited a broad class of persons. The court remanded to the trial court with directions to hear and determine respondents' remaining motions for attorney fees in conformity with the court's opinion.

Bailey v. Superior Court


L.A. No. 30746


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 970; 568 P.2d 394; 140 Cal. Rptr. 669; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 178



September 23, 1977 



DISPOSITION:  Let a writ of prohibition issue directing the respondent court to quash its order granting the motion of Sears to videotape the deposition. 12 



12 This court need not, and does not, decide whether parties can either agree to supplement a written transcript of a deposition with a videotape record or agree to entirely forego the written transcript and only videotape the deposition. (See, e.g., Kornblum, Videotape in Civil Cases, supra, 24 Hastings L.J. 9, 17-18.)

Additionally, the court does not reach the arguments urged by amici curiae, who represent legal assistance programs. They contend that (1) depositions would be made more available to indigent persons if audio tape recordings were held to be authorized substitutes for stenographic recordings; (2) this court has inherent in forma pauperis power to waive the Code of Civil Procedure section 2019 requirement of a stenographic record; and (3) section 2019 is unconstitutional on equal protection and due process grounds unless interpreted to permit trial courts to waive stenographic recordings for indigent persons. Since these contentions are not urged by the parties and indigency is not an issue, it is not necessary to reach these arguments at this time. (See, e.g., Short Stop, Inc. v. Fielder (1971) 17 Cal.App.3d 435, 439 [95 Cal.Rptr. 102]; Pratt v. Coast Trucking, Inc. (1964) 228 Cal.App.2d 139, 143 [39 Cal.Rptr. 332].)

 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant sought a writ of prohibition against respondent Superior Court of Kern County (California), which had granted real party in interest manufacturer's motion to videotape a deposition. Petitioner sought the writ to prohibit real party in interest from videotaping the proceedings at a deposition because Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 2004 only provided for the written transcription of a deposition.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner claimant was injured by a radial arm saw he purchased from real party in interest manufacturer. Real party in interest alleged that the injury was a result of petitioner's own negligence. It sought to take petitioner's deposition under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 2019, and requested permission from the trial court to videotape a reenactment of the accident. Respondent trial court granted the request. Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition on the ground that respondent trial court had exceeded its jurisdiction in authorizing the videotape recording. The court agreed with petitioner and directed that a writ of prohibition issue directing respondent trial court to quash its order granting real party in interest's motion to videotape the deposition. The court reasoned that § 2019, and related rules, were unequivocal in requiring written transcription, unless the parties otherwise agreed, and that any change was a matter for the legislature, not the court.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that a writ of prohibition issue, which directed respondent court to quash its order granting the motion of real party in interest manufacturer to videotape the deposition of petitioner injury claimant because the applicable rules of procedure required a written transcription and the legislature, not the court, was the place to change the law.

Hildebrand v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Board


S.F. No. 23583


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 765; 566 P.2d 1297; 140 Cal. Rptr. 151; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 163



August 9, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the plaintiff and respondent for a rehearing was denied September 15, 1977. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 243588, Murle C. Shreck, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employee sought judicial review of defendant unemployment insurance appeal board's denial of her benefits through a petition for administrative mandamus under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5. Appellant plaintiff's employer, a real party in interest, appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which rendered judgment for plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Defendant unemployment insurance appeals board denied plaintiff former employee's application for unemployment benefits on the grounds that she left her employment voluntarily and without good cause. Plaintiff sought judicial review of defendant's decision through a petition for administrative mandamus under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5. Plaintiff asserted that she terminated her employment due to her bona fide religious objections to working on Saturdays and that defendant's denial of her benefits unconstitutionally violated her freedom of religion. The trial court granted plaintiff's petition and ordered defendant to grant her application. On appeal, the court reversed in favor of defendant. The court held that plaintiff accepted employment with the full knowledge that the Saturday work requirement would conflict with her religious principles. Plaintiff's subsequent refusal to perform such work, which resulted in her termination, constituted leaving work voluntarily without good cause. Thus, plaintiff was properly denied unemployment benefits.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment granting benefits to plaintiff former employee, holding that plaintiff left work voluntarily and without good cause because she knew upon accepting employment that her Saturday work schedule would conflict with her religious beliefs.

Newland v. Board of Governors


S.F. No. 23557


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 705; 566 P.2d 254; 139 Cal. Rptr. 620; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 158



July 21, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 684509, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Since plaintiff has fulfilled two of the three requirements for relief under subdivision (b) of section 13220.16, and the remaining requirement -- that he apply for or obtain a certificate of rehabilitation -- cannot constitutionally be invoked to deny relief to misdemeanants, we conclude that defendant board cannot rely upon section 13220.16 to deny plaintiff's application for a community college credential. 11 Plaintiff having established his right to relief as prayed, the judgment of the superior court is reversed and the cause remanded with directions to issue a peremptory writ of mandate in conformity with the views expressed in this opinion.



11 Since defendant cannot invoke section 13220.16 to deny plaintiff a community college credential, it must either grant him that credential or, if it doubts his present fitness to teach, afford him a hearing on that issue. (See Ed. Code, § 13220.15.) We reject defendant's suggestion that a fitness hearing would serve no purpose on defendant's theory that all persons convicted of lewd conduct in a public place are unfit to teach as a matter of law. The case at bar is the counterexample; it cannot reasonably be maintained that because plaintiff masturbated in a closed toilet stall at a bus station in 1967 that now, 10 years later, he is necessarily unfit to teach community college students, and will forever be unfit. Even if plaintiff's 1967 conduct suggests a lack of judgment or discretion, these are not immutable traits, but defects which may disappear with greater maturity and experience. Consequently if defendant elects to conduct a fitness hearing, it cannot limit that hearing to a determination of whether plaintiff committed a violation of Penal Code section 647, subdivision (a), or was convicted therefor, but must conduct "a careful and reasoned inquiry into his fitness to teach." ( Morrison v. State Board of Education, supra, 1 Cal.3d 214, 238-239.)

 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challenged the decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) denying plaintiff's mandate action to compel defendant school board to provide him with a fitness hearing after defendant denied plaintiff a community college credential.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sought a community college credential from defendant, the board of governors for the state's community colleges. Defendant rejected the application on the grounds that he had been convicted of a misdemeanor sex offense. Defendant reasoned that Cal. Educ. Code § 13220.16 barred issuance of a credential to anyone convicted of a sex offense. Defendant also denied a hearing to determine plaintiff's fitness. The superior court denied plaintiff's mandate to compel defendant to have a hearing and upheld the constitutionality of Cal. Educ. Code § 13220.16. On appeal, the court found that under an amendment to § 13220.16 plaintiff would have qualified for the credential if he had been able to obtain a certificate of rehabilitation. Plaintiff argued that the statutory requirement for a certificate of rehabilitation denied misdemeanants the equal protection of the law because misdemeanants were ineligible to petition for the certificates. The court held that defendant could not rely upon § 13220.16. The court reasoned that the amendment to § 13220.16 could not be constitutionally invoked to deny relief to misdemeanants.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the superior court that denied plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandate. The court held that defendant could not deny plaintiff's application for a community college credential pursuant to a statute that denied credentials to misdemeanants.

Board of Education v. Jack M.


L.A. No. 30705


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 691; 566 P.2d 602; 139 Cal. Rptr. 700; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 157



July 21, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC30810, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Substantial evidence supports the trial court's finding that defendant's conduct did not demonstrate his unfitness to teach. Evidence admitted without objection demonstrated defendant's teaching competence, and rendered insubstantial any fear that he might repeat the charged act or otherwise harm students or fellow teachers. The trial court was not required to ignore that evidence and base a decision solely upon the single incident of October 19, 1972; it properly considered all evidence of fitness; on the basis of that evidence it found defendant was fit to teach. Since substantial evidence supports the trial court findings of fact and conclusions of law, we affirm its judgment directing reinstatement of defendant to his teaching position with back pay.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff school board appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which reinstated defendant teacher with backpay because defendant's lewd conduct, which led to an arrest, did not demonstrate unfitness to teach under Cal. Educ. Code § 13403.


OVERVIEW: Defendant teacher was arrested under Cal. Penal Code § 647 of soliciting anyone to engage in or engagement in lewd or dissolute conduct in any public place or in any place open to the public or exposed to public view. Charges were never brought. Based on the arrest and defendant's conduct leading to the arrest, plaintiff school board sought to dismiss defendant under Cal. Educ. Code § 13403(a), (e). The superior court ruled in favor of defendant, reinstating him with backpay. The court affirmed, noting that the charge of immoral or unprofessional conduct, § 13403(a), previously had been deemed unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. Under the remaining charge of unfitness for service, § 13403(e), substantial evidence supported the finding that defendant was not unfit to teach. Defendant's education, training, experience, and evaluations indicated competence. Psychological evidence supported the conclusion that defendant would not harm students or teachers. Defendant was not convicted of a sex offense which would preclude a hearing under Cal. Educ. Code § 12910.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendant teacher, because substantial evidence supported the finding that the lewd conduct that led to an arrest did not render defendant unfit to teach. Plaintiff school board was ordered to reinstate defendant with backpay.

In re Roger S.


Crim. No. 19558


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 921; 569 P.2d 1286; 141 Cal. Rptr. 298; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 176



July 18, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of both parties for a rehearing were denied September 15, 1977; the dissenting opinion was modified to read as printed above; and a concurring and dissenting opinion by Richardson, J., was filed.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is therefore discharged and the petition denied without prejudice to a renewed application for relief in the superior court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minor filed a writ of habeas corpus for release from a state psychiatric hospital on Due Process Clause and Equal Protection Clause grounds. U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a). Petitioner's request for a writ was denied by the Superior Court (California).


OVERVIEW: Parent committed petitioner minor to the state psychiatric hospital against his will. Petitioner's request for a writ of habeas corpus was denied in the superior court. The court denied the writ without prejudice, and held that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6000 procedures for admitting minors to state psychiatric hospitals violated the rights of minors aged 14 and above under the Due Process Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a). Minors aged 14 and over whose parents applied for their involuntary commitment were entitled to an administrative hearing, although a judge and jury were not required. The lesser preponderance of the evidence standard was applicable. The court concluded that the commitment procedures were not violative of the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a), because petitioner was not similarly situated with adults and minor wards of the court covered by the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 5000- 5401. The court advised petitioner of his ability to request a writ of habeas corpus at the superior court to secure an administrative hearing regarding commitment.


OUTCOME: The court denied the request without prejudice, holding that state involuntary commitment procedure for petitioner minor violated the Due Process Clause. Petitioner was advised to submit a second request to the superior court for a writ of habeas corpus to secure an administrative hearing regarding commitment.

People v. Thomas


Crim. No. 19476


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 630; 566 P.2d 228; 139 Cal. Rptr. 594; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 154



July 15, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NDA 17998, Leo S. Rich, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which ruled in favor of state and committed him to a rehabilitation center as an alleged narcotics addict, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested on suspicion of being under the influence of heroin. At the trial to determine whether defendant was a narcotics addict, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3051, as state alleged, the superior court rejected defendant's motions to require a unanimous verdict and the proof standard of beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant was found in violation of § 3051 and committed to the state narcotics rehabilitation facility. The court reversed, concluding that the proceedings under § 3051 seriously put at risk both the personal liberty and the good name of defendant, and therefore triggered procedural protections under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7(a), 16. Defendant was entitled to a unanimous jury verdict and the proof standard of beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that the standard in narcotics addict commitment proceedings must be as high as it is in juvenile delinquency proceedings, i.e., proof beyond a reasonable doubt. People v. Moore, 69 Cal. 2d 674, 446 P2d 800, 72 Cal. Rptr. 800 (1968), was overruled to the extent it conflicted with the holding. The court held that the ruling was retroactive.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's commitment to a rehabilitation facility because state did not afford defendant the procedural protections of a unanimous jury verdict and the proof standard of beyond a reasonable doubt. The court gave the holding retroactive effect.

Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 646; 566 P.2d 243; 139 Cal. Rptr. 609; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 155

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, superintendent of a prison, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California) granting petitioner a writ of habeas corpus seeking presentence credit.


OVERVIEW: The superior court granted petitioner's application for a writ of habeas corpus seeking, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5 presentence credit for jail time spent in custody in another state. Appellant, the superintendent of the prison, challenged the decision, arguing that petitioner was not entitled to have such time credited upon his sentence where it was spent in jail in a foreign jurisdiction while he was resisting extradition for trial upon charges of which he was ultimately convicted. Moreover, the court held that any person arrested and held for extradition or ordered to be extradited had both a federal and state right to test the validity of the arrest and extradition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court granting petitioner's writ of habeas corpus and granting petitioner presentence credit. The court reasoned that the statutes entitled petitioner to credit for the presentence time spent in jail in a foreign jurisdiction.

Borer v. American Airlines, Inc.


L.A. No. 30650


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 441; 563 P.2d 858; 138 Cal. Rptr. 302; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 140



May 6, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied June 2, 1977. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 112661, Christian E. Markey, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs children challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendants' airline and company demurrer for failure to state a cause of action in plaintiffs' complaint against defendants for negligence, breach of warranty, and manufacture of a defective product, and dismissed defendant airline. Plaintiffs sought damages for loss of consortium.


OVERVIEW: In the consolidated action, the court affirmed the demurrer of plaintiff children's action for negligence, breach of warranty, and manufacture of a defective product, which disabled their mother, against defendants airline and company, and plaintiffs' mother and father action for loss of companionship and affection of their disabled son. The court declined to recognize a new cause of action for loss of consortium in a parent-child relationship concluding that loss of consortium is an intangible injury for which money damages do not afford an accurate measure or suitable compensation. The court concluded that recognition of a right to recover for such losses might substantially increase the number of claims asserted in ordinary accident cases, the expense of settling or resolving such claims, and the ultimate liability of the defendants. The court distinguished between the marital relationship and the parent-child relationship and limited the cause of action for loss of consortium to the marital situation because social policy intervened to limit such liability imposed on defendants. The court found that no state permitted a child to sue for loss of parental consortium.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment declining to recognize a loss of parent-child consortium cause of action after taking into consideration all issues bearing on the question, including the inadequacy of monetary compensation to alleviate that tragedy, the difficulty of measuring damages, and the danger of imposing extended and disproportionate liability.

Estate of Fasken


S.F. No. 23409


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 412; 563 P.2d 832; 138 Cal. Rptr. 276; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 139



May 5, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County, No. 18150, Joseph G. Wilson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state controller challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Marin County (California), which ordered that inheritance taxes be fixed in decedent's estate, who died in 1968 while a resident of California. Decedent devised and bequeathed her entire estate, consisting of real and tangible personal property located in California and Texas, to her son, respondent.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which ordered that inheritance taxes be fixed in decedent's estate, who died in 1968 while a resident of California. Decedent devised and bequeathed her entire estate, consisting of real and tangible personal property located in California and Texas, to her son, respondent executor. The taxes were fixed at an amount $ 463,858 less than that claimed by appellant. Appellant objected to the report of the inheritance tax referee purporting to determine death taxes due pursuant to provisions of the Revenue and Taxation Code. The court found that the regulation appellant relied upon infringed jurisdictional limitations under federal due process concepts and that the trial court fixed the tax at its jurisdictional limit. The court reasoned that because the federal credit for state death taxes was rated and measured by the entire estate, regardless of situs, no state could assert a claim to all of that credit when some taxable portion of the estate is located in another state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the amount of inheritance taxes. The regulation appellant relied upon infringed jurisdictional limitations under federal due process concepts. The tax was fixed at its jurisdictional limit. Because the federal credit for state death taxes was rated and measured by the entire estate, regardless of situs, no state could assert a claim to all of that credit when some taxable portion of the estate is located in another state.

 

388 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Arp v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


L.A. No. 30685


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 395; 563 P.2d 849; 138 Cal. Rptr. 293; 42 Cal. Comp. Cases 369; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 138



May 5, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petitions of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board and petitioner Arp for a hearing were denied June 2, 1977, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The order of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board is affirmed. Each side shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widower sought review of a determination from respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (board) that denied him the benefit of the conclusive presumption of total dependency afforded to widows under Cal. Lab. Code § 3501a. On appeal, petitioner argued violation of equal protection under the federal and state constitutions and gender-based discrimination under title VII of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e-2(a).


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the order of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board that denied the benefit of conclusive presumption of total dependency to petitioner widower after petitioner filed following employee wife's fatal industrial accident. The court however held that the applicable statute Cal. Lab. Code § 3501a denied equal protection of the laws to both widowers and employed women, was invalid, and was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. V and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7. The court found that the statute was sexually discriminatory on its face, was without support of any ameliorative purpose, potentially disadvantaged large numbers of employed women, perpetuated a paternalistic notion that a woman's financial contributions were insubstantial, and failed to realize a compelling state goal. The court concluded it was unwise to strike the provision and opted for permitting the legislature to evaluate policies, obtain and examine information, and to act appropriately. Pending further legislative action, all applicants henceforth had to establish their dependency through Cal. Lab. Code § 5052.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board that denied the benefit of the conclusive presumption of total dependency for widows to petitioner widower. The court held that the statutory provision describing the presumption was invalid and denied equal protection of the laws to both widowers and employed women. Pending legislative action, applicants had to establish proof of dependency under a different statute.

McComb v. Commission on Judicial Performance


S.F. No. 23585.


Supreme Court of California


564 P.2d 1; 138 Cal. Rptr. 459; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 143; 19 Cal. 3d (Spec. Trib. Supp.) 1



May 2, 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judge appealed the decision of respondent Commission on Judicial Performance (California), which recommended the retirement or removal of petitioner from the California Supreme Court under Cal. Const. art. VI, §§ 8 and 18 and Cal. R. Ct. 901-922. Petitioner also requested a writ of review and requested the court to reject or modify respondent's recommendations.


OVERVIEW: The court ordered petitioner judge's retirement after independently evaluating evidence presented to respondent commission and found clear and convincing evidence sufficient to sustain a charge to a reasonable certainty, that petitioner was suffering from a disability which rendered him unable to perform his judicial duties, and that the disability was, or was likely to become, permanent. The record established specific instances of bizarre behavior by petitioner, which if viewed in isolation, would support discipline for conduct prejudicial to the administration of justice which brings the judicial office into disrepute. The court considered the conduct as symptomatic of the condition of chronic brain syndrome (senile dementia). The court concluded that discipline in the form of removal from office or reprimand was not appropriate, nor was petitioner engaging in wilful misconduct. The court rejected petitioner's argument that the proceeding denied him due process and equal protection of the laws because he was not charged with unlawful acts which constituted criminal offenses, nor was the proceeding designed to convict petitioner of a crime or to punish him for criminal acts.


OUTCOME: The court ordered petitioner judge's retirement finding that petitioner was suffering from a disability which rendered him unable to perform his judicial duties that was likely to become permanent. The court rejected respondent commissioner's conclusions that petitioner's discipline should be removal or reprimand because his condition was symptomatic of senile dementia.

People v. Superior Court of Alameda County


S.F. No. 23477


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 338; 562 P.2d 1315; 138 Cal. Rptr. 66; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 136



April 27, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the real parties in interest for a rehearing was denied May 26, 1977. Bird, C. J., and Tobriner, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let the peremptory writ of prohibition issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state petitioned the court for a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent superior court (California) from enforcing its order directing that a peremptory writ of prohibition be issued restraining the trial court from proceeding with prosecution of defendants for soliciting or engaging in prostitution.


OVERVIEW: The state petitioned the court for a writ of prohibition to prevent respondent superior court from enforcing its order. Defendants were charged with soliciting or engaging in prostitution under Cal. Penal Code § 647(b). They moved for dismissal of the charges, and the trial court denied the motion. Upon application by defendants, respondent issued a writ restraining trial proceedings, and the state appealed. On appeal, the court found that the challenged statute was constitutional both on its face and as applied. The court granted the state's petition for a peremptory writ of prohibition. Section 647(b), insofar as it prohibited soliciting an act of prostitution, was not unconstitutionally vague. Further the court found that there was ample evidence to support the conclusion that the police department made arrests for violations of § 647(b) based upon probable cause that defendants committed the offenses and not on the basis of the sex of defendants. Finally, the court held that the quarantine procedure was the responsibility of public health officials and its relevance to a claim of deliberate discrimination on the part of the police was highly questionable.


OUTCOME: The court granted the state's motion for peremptory writ of prohibition, as there was ample evidence to support the conclusion that the police department made arrests for the crime of soliciting or engaging in prostitution based upon probable cause to believe defendants committed the offenses, and not on the basis of the sex of defendants.

Kash Enterprises, Inc. v. Los Angeles


L.A. No. 30688


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 294; 562 P.2d 1302; 138 Cal. Rptr. 53; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 133; 2 Media L. Rep. 1716



April 15, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 124246, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant newspaper rack owner challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his request for a preliminary injunction in a constitutional challenge to the facial validity of an ordinance regulating newspaper racks on city sidewalks.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the judgment denying appellant newspaper rack owner's request for a preliminary injunction in a constitutional challenge to an ordinance regulating news racks on city sidewalks passed by respondent city. Appellant contended the ordinance was impermissibly vague and overbroad, and that the summary seizure, retention, and destruction of offending news racks without according a news rack owner any hearing as to the validity of the seizure violated both procedural due process and first amendment principles. Respondent maintained that the ordinance embodied a reasonable time, place, and manner regulation of news racks and was constitutional on its face. The court held that, although the substantive provisions of the ordinance were sufficiently definite and narrowly drawn to pass constitutional muster, the ordinance's seizure provision was unconstitutional on its face. The court reasoned that the seizure, retention and destruction of news racks without affording the owner of the rack either a pre- or post-taking hearing, the ordinance violated fundamental procedural due process and first amendment precepts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment denying appellant newspaper rack owner's request for a preliminary injunction in a constitutional challenge to an ordinance regulating news racks on city sidewalks passed by respondent city. The seizure, retention and destruction of news racks without affording the owner of the rack either a pre- or post-taking hearing, the ordinance violated fundamental procedural due process and first amendment precepts.

 

392 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Greer)


S.F. No. 23505


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 255; 561 P.2d 1164; 137 Cal. Rptr. 476; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 130



March 30, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied April 28, 1977, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People filed a petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) to permit a district attorney to prosecute a homicide despite the fact that respondent was convinced a conflict of interest existed. The People asserted that judicial intervention violated the principle of the separation of powers under Cal. Const. art. III, § 3 by usurping a function reserved to the executive.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Superior Court of Contra Costa County disqualified a district attorney from prosecuting a homicide because of a conflict of interest. The People contended that respondent's act usurped the executive function pursuant to Cal. Const. art. III, § 3. The court stated that the disqualification of a prosecutor did not impermissibly infringe on the executive's power to select a prosecutor. The court further found that the California Constitution did not deny a trial court's authority to disqualify a particular representative when his participation would taint the proceeding. Thus, the court concluded that a trial judge may exercise his power to disqualify a district attorney from prosecuting when the judge determines that the attorney suffers from a conflict of interest which might prejudice him against the accused and thereby affect his ability to impartially perform his duties. The court also held that respondent did not abuse its discretion in disqualifying the district attorney because the homicide victim was the son of a member of the district attorney's staff who worked in the office in which the prosecution was being prepared. Thus, the court denied the peremptory writ.


OUTCOME: The court denied the peremptory writ filed by the People against respondent the Superior Court of Contra Costa County. The court held that respondent had discretion to disqualify a district attorney when a conflict of interest existed and that it did not usurp the executive branch of government.

 

393 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Solberg v. Superior Court of San Francisco


S.F. Nos. 23449, 23469


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 182; 561 P.2d 1148; 137 Cal. Rptr. 460; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 125



March 22, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of the defendants and appellants for a rehearing was denied April 21, 1977. Tobriner, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The motion to appoint a referee and take evidence is denied. In S.F. 23449, let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed. In S.F. 23469, the appeal is dismissed. All parties shall bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant municipal court judge and petitioner criminal defendants sought, in separate actions, to void the judgment of respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), restraining defendant municipal court judge from taking any further proceedings in certain actions other than to disqualify herself and set the matters for hearing before another judge.


OVERVIEW: In a prosecution for prostitution, the people sought to disqualify defendant municipal court judge pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 170.6. Defendant judge refused to disqualify herself and the people sought a writ of mandate in the superior court. Petitioner criminal defendants then filed an action seeking a writ of mandate to disqualify respondent superior court judge from deciding the first action, after he refused to disqualify himself upon petitioners' § 170.6 motion. The court of appeal issued an alternative writ. The reviewing court dismissed the first action's appeal and issued the requested writ in the second action. The court reaffirmed that § 170.6 did not violate the doctrine of the separation of powers or impair the independence of the judiciary. In two successive § 170.6 actions a party could disqualify the judge in the later action without waiving that right by failing to so request in the earlier. Prejudice could properly be declared as the ground of such disqualification, and it was wholly unnecessary for the legislature to attempt to list the many conceivable factors which might cause a judge to be prejudiced. Thus, both judges should have recused themselves.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal in the first action. In the second action, the court issued a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent superior court to vacate his order denying petitioner criminal defendants' motion to disqualify him. All parties were to bear their own costs.

Levy v. Cohen


L.A. No. 30701


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 165; 561 P.2d 252; 137 Cal. Rptr. 162; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 123



March 17, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C45298, Stevens Fargo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: [] [The judgment is reversed.]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in favor of plaintiff, following a bench trial, in an action against defendants, as the general partners of a limited partnership, to recover on promissory notes executed by defendants on behalf of the partnership.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sued to enforce defendants' liability as general partners for an obligation of the limited partnership, a liability that was extinguished by an order confirming the plan of arrangement entered in a bankruptcy proceeding. Defendants argued for reversal of the trial court's judgment because the order of the bankruptcy court, that released defendants from liability for obligations of the partnership, was res judicata on the issue of defendants' liability on promissory notes that were the subject of the action. The court held that recovery was precluded by the defense of res judicata. Because any objections that plaintiff had could have been raised in the bankruptcy proceeding, the order confirming the plan of arrangement was conclusive in the subsequent action insofar as the order determined that defendants were not liable for obligations of the partnership.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court in favor of plaintiff was reversed because the order of the bankruptcy court releasing defendants from liability for obligations of the partnership was res judicata on the issue of defendants' liability on promissory notes that were the subject of plaintiff's action.

 

395 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Dewing


Crim. Nos. 19695, 19696


Supreme Court of California


19 Cal. 3d 54; 560 P.2d 375; 136 Cal. Rptr. 708; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 116



March 4, 1977 



DISPOSITION:  The orders to show cause, having served their purpose, are discharged. The petitions for writs of habeas corpus are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners each sought a writ of habeas corpus from the court (California) in a consolidated proceeding. Petitioners were committed to the Youth Authority after each was convicted of assault with a deadly weapon on June 20, 1974 and January 20, 1975, respectively. Effective September 20, 1976, Cal. Penal Code § 1070(b)(2) operated via 1976 Cal. Stat. 1070, § 7 to extend petitioners' maximum discharge date from Youth Authority custody.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners had been committed to the Youth Authority after each was convicted of assault with a deadly weapon. The legislation at 1976 Cal. Stat. 1070, § 7 (§ 7) declared that the amendments to Cal. Penal Code § 17 discussed therein applied to all persons under Youth Authority jurisdiction on the effective date of § 7 by reason of a commitment following conviction of the crime. The amendments effectively classified petitioners' crimes as felonies where they had been classified as misdemeanors, and this extended their maximum discharge date by two years because Youth Authority Act provided that every person convicted of a felony and committed to the Youth Authority was to be discharged when he reached his 25th birthday. Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 1771. Petitioners applied for writs of habeas corpus, and the court denied the writs. One petitioner had been discharged, and the other had not reached the maximum discharge date under the old law: his 23rd birthday. However, the court held that he could not be constitutionally held beyond the old maximum discharge date because § 7 was an ex post facto law prohibited by both U.S. Const. art. I, § 9 and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9.


OUTCOME: One petitioner was free on the date of decision, so the court said his petition was moot. Other petitioner was not yet eligible for discharge under his old maximum discharge date, so a writ of habeas corpus was denied. Petitioner in custody could not be constitutionally held after his old maximum discharge date because the statute making amendments applicable to persons in the present custody of the Youth Authority was an ex post facto law.

 

396 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. De Vaughn


Crim. Nos. 19468, 19469


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 889; 558 P.2d 872; 135 Cal. Rptr. 786; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 108



January 25, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 070463, Edward Rafeedie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments (orders granting probation) are reversed as to each defendant and their pleas are set aside.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of their convictions for burglary in the second-degree pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 459, 460 by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) and for the denial of their motion to set aside the informations and to suppress confessions that the police obtained during their arrest.


OVERVIEW: Defendants pled guilty to burglary in the second-degree in violation of Cal. Pen. Code §§ 459, 460 following their unsuccessful motion to suppress their confessions, which they alleged were involuntarily induced by the police. Defendants were convicted and sentenced accordingly and then sought review of their convictions and the admission of the confessions. The court reversed the convictions and set aside defendants' pleas, holding that defendants' guilty pleas were improperly induced because police officers had no basis to support a belief that a crime had been committed, and therefore, defendants' arrest was illegal. The court also found that the trial court could not bargain to preserve defendants' constitutional issues on appeal by issuance of certificates of probable cause. The court found no suggestion of an intervening act, which could have served to attenuate the taint of the unconstitutional arrest and thereby motivate the confessions. Therefore, the court held that defendants' confessions were obtained in violation of defendants' constitutional protections under the Fourth and Fifth Amendments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions for second-degree burglary. The court held that defendants' arrests were illegal because there was no basis to believe that a crime had been committed, and therefore, the confessions were obtained in violation of defendants' constitutional rights.

 

397 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Miller v. State of California


L.A. No. 30662


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 808; 557 P.2d 970; 135 Cal. Rptr. 386; 1977 Cal. LEXIS 101



January 4, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-107529, Parks Stillwell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff retiree appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his complaint for a writ of mandate to restore him to his employment with defendant state in an action for an alleged breach of plaintiff's employment contract.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff retiree appealed a trial court's judgment, which determined that a statutory change of the mandatory retirement age for state employees from age 70 to age 67 did not violate his rights, even though the change was made during his tenure. The trial court declined to reinstate him to his former position with defendant state. Plaintiff appealed, and the court affirmed, holding that plaintiff had no vested contractual right to remain in state employment until age 70 and that the legislature had the power to reduce plaintiff's tenure and shorten his state service. Thus, he had no right to be reinstated. The court further held that although plaintiff's right to a pension was vested, he was not assured of receiving maximum pension benefits because those benefits were subject to the condition that he remain in employment until age 70. Plaintiff did not satisfy that condition because he was lawfully retired at age 67. Therefore, even though his actual pension was smaller than anticipated, he suffered no impairment of vested pension rights because he had no constitutionally protected right to remain in employment until he had earned a larger pension at age 70.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that plaintiff retiree had no vested right to remain employed by defendant state because defendant had legally lowered its mandatory retirement age during plaintiff's tenure and that that change was applicable to plaintiff.

Serrano v. Priest


L.A. No. 30398


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 728; 557 P.2d 929; 135 Cal. Rptr. 345; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 380



December 30, 1976 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied January 27, 1977. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted. On February 1, 1977, the judgment was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 938254, Bernard S. Jefferson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. We reserve jurisdiction for the purpose of considering and passing upon respondent's motion for an award of attorneys' fees on appeal, filed January 28, 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The case was before the court on appeal from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) decreeing that the California public school financing system for public elementary and secondary schools, while not in violation of the Equal Protection Clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV, was invalid as being in violation of the equal protection of the laws provisions of the California Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that the California public school financing system for elementary and secondary schools, while not in violation of the equal protection clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV, was invalid under the California equal protection provisions. Appellants argued that the trial court employed inappropriate criteria insofar as it focused on the notion of so-called "fiscal neutrality" to the exclusion of other factors relevant to its determination. Appellants also argued that an improper legal standard of equal protection review was utilized and, that, assuming that the financing system before the court was to some extent inconsistent with state equal protection provisions, those provisions were to that extent in conflict with other provisions of the state constitution. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, finding that the discrimination in public school financing had been shown to exist and that the trial court, in so finding, employed proper criteria of strict scrutiny.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, agreeing that the school finance plan denied state equal protection provisions, and reserved jurisdiction for the purpose of considering and passing upon a motion for an award of attorneys' fees on appeal.

 

399 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Barton v. Panish


L.A. No. 30660


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 624; 557 P.2d 497; 135 Cal. Rptr. 65; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 373



December 20, 1976 



DISPOSITION:  Therefore, the alternative writ of mandate is discharged and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner judicial candidate sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent election officials to certify her election to a municipal court judgeship. Prior to the election, the governor had appointed a judge, real party in interest, to the position.


OVERVIEW: A municipal court judge retired nine months before the end of her term. Two weeks after the judge retired, petitioner judicial candidate filed papers to run in the election to fill the vacancy. Six weeks later, and one month before the election, the governor appointed real party in interest to fill the vacancy. Petitioner received a majority of the votes in the election, but respondent election officials would not certify her election because the election was void under Cal. Gov't Code § 71180. This statute provided that when the governor filled a vacant office by appointment, no successor to the appointee could be elected at any election held within 10 months of the date of the occurrence of the vacancy. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent election officials to certify her election. The court denied the petition because the 10-month requirement of § 71180 precluded an election when an appointment occurred during the course of the electoral process, but before its completion. The court held that § 71180 was not unconstitutional because the states were free to fill nonlegislative offices by appointment, election, or a combination of both.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner judicial candidate's request for a writ of mandate to compel respondent election officials to certify her election to a judgeship. The court held that her election was precluded because the governor had appointed a person to the office within 10 months of the vacancy and before the electoral process was complete.

 

400 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Associated Home Builders etc., Inc. v. City of Livermore


S.F. No. 23222


Supreme Court of California


18 Cal. 3d 582; 557 P.2d 473; 135 Cal. Rptr. 41; 1976 Cal. LEXIS 372; 92 A.L.R.3d 1038; 7 ELR 20155



December 17, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 425754, Lyle E. Cook, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court is reversed, and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipality challenged the judgment entered in the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) that granted respondent homebuilders' association's action to enjoin enforcement of an initiative ordinance, which prohibited issuance of further residential building permits until local educational, sewage disposal, and water supply facilities complied with specified standards.


OVERVIEW: Following case law precedent, the trial court held that appellant municipality, as a general law city, lacked authority to enact the initiative ordinance. Citing the precepts that inconsistencies in statutes had to be reconciled if reasonably possible, that statutes had to be construed to eliminate doubts as to their constitutionality, and that the initiative power was broadly construed, resolving all doubts in favor of the reserved power, the court overruled Hurst v. Burlingame, 277 P. 308, 207 Cal. 134 (1929). The court ruled that the statutory notice and hearing provisions of Cal. Gov't Code §§ 65853-65857 applied only to ordinances enacted by city council action and did not limit the power of municipal electors, reserved to them by the state constitution, to enact legislation by initiative. The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded the case to determine whether the challenged measure was reasonably related to the welfare of the region affected by its exclusionary impact, thus falling within the police power of the city.


OUTCOME: The court overruled the case relied upon by the trial court, and reversed the order that granted injunctive relief to respondent homebuilders' association.
